## Assistant (2026-04-21 19:56:44)

_Thought for 47s_

I found an important correction, and I think it sharpens the local theorem in the right way.

The last reduction to a **tangent-map theorem** was too aggressive. The residual-corner symmetry kills the entire linear layer, so the relevant infinitesimal object is **not** the first derivative at the zero cancellation datum. It is the **quadratic form / Hessian** at zero.

That is actually good news, because it fits the exact local normal form we already derived.

Let me explain.

In the linearized swap chart
\[
\nu:=\log\lambda,\qquad \delta:=h_2-h_1,
\]
the atom-swap involution is
\[
(\nu,\delta)\mapsto(-\nu,-\delta).
\]
So any analytic swap-invariant local defect \(E_j\) is even under the simultaneous sign flip. Hence its Taylor expansion at \((\nu,\delta)=(0,0)\) has **no linear term**:
\[
E_j(m,\nu,\delta)
=
A_j(m)\nu^2+B_j(m)\nu\delta+C_j(m)\delta^2
+
O\!\bigl((|\nu|+|\delta|)^4\bigr).
\]
So the first nonzero infinitesimal layer is quadratic.

That means the right infinitesimal theorem is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the leading local interaction is governed by the Hessian of the corrected defect at the zero cancellation datum.}
}
\]

More precisely, let \(X\) denote the local cancellation datum. In the collision-cancellation chart,
\[
X(\nu,\delta)
=
-\nu\,D_m-\delta\,D_m'+O\!\bigl((|\nu|+|\delta|)^2\bigr),
\]
or along the blown-up slope \(\nu=\kappa\delta\),
\[
X(\kappa,\delta)
=
-\delta\Bigl(D_m'+\kappa D_m\Bigr)+O(\delta^2),
\]
up to the harmless normalization convention on \(\kappa\). Let me write
\[
\Xi_\kappa(m):=D_m'+\kappa D_m
\]
for the first-order cancellation direction.

Now suppose the corrected cubic/quintic defects are analytic functionals of \(X\) that are even under \(X\mapsto -X\). Then their first derivative at \(0\) vanishes, and the leading term is
\[
E_j=\frac12\,\mathbf B_j\bigl(X,X\bigr)+O(\|X\|^4),
\]
where \(\mathbf B_j\) is the symmetric bilinear form given by the Hessian at \(0\).

Substituting the first-order cancellation direction gives
\[
E_j
=
\delta^2\cdot \frac12\,\mathbf B_j\bigl(\Xi_\kappa(m),\Xi_\kappa(m)\bigr)
+
O(\delta^4).
\]
So the edge coefficient is
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal H_j(m,\kappa,0)
=
\frac12\,\mathbf B_j\bigl(\Xi_\kappa(m),\Xi_\kappa(m)\bigr).
}
\]

This is the right replacement for the earlier “tangent map” idea.

Now compare with the model pair on a good patch:
\[
\mathcal H_3(m,\kappa,0)=c'(m)-\frac{\kappa}{2}c(m),
\]
\[
\mathcal H_5(m,\kappa,0)=(A_5^{\mathfrak f})'(m)-\frac{\kappa}{2}A_5^{\mathfrak f}(m).
\]

These are **affine** in \(\kappa\), not quadratic. Therefore the Hessians must satisfy a very rigid degeneracy:

if
\[
\Xi_\kappa(m)=D_m'+\kappa D_m,
\]
then
\[
\frac12\,\mathbf B_j(\Xi_\kappa,\Xi_\kappa)
=
\frac12\,\mathbf B_j(D_m',D_m')
+
\kappa\,\mathbf B_j(D_m',D_m)
+
\frac{\kappa^2}{2}\,\mathbf B_j(D_m,D_m).
\]
Matching this with the affine model forces

for \(j=3\),
\[
\mathbf B_3(D_m,D_m)=0,
\qquad
\mathbf B_3(D_m',D_m)=-\frac12 c(m),
\qquad
\frac12\,\mathbf B_3(D_m',D_m')=c'(m),
\]
and for \(j=5\),
\[
\mathbf B_5(D_m,D_m)=0,
\qquad
\mathbf B_5(D_m',D_m)=-\frac12 A_5^{\mathfrak f}(m),
\qquad
\frac12\,\mathbf B_5(D_m',D_m')=(A_5^{\mathfrak f})'(m).
\]

So the good-patch local theorem has now been reduced to identifying **two symmetric bilinear forms** on the span of \(\{D_m,D_m'\}\), with these six values.

This is better than the earlier six scalar coefficients, because it tells us exactly what kind of structure is needed.

The three slices we had before now become three entries of the same Hessian.

The “pure amplitude-cancellation sterility” statement
\[
A_j=0
\]
is exactly
\[
\mathbf B_j(D_m,D_m)=0.
\]

The “pure separation first-variation” statement
\[
C_3=c',\qquad C_5=(A_5^{\mathfrak f})'
\]
is exactly
\[
\frac12\,\mathbf B_3(D_m',D_m')=c',
\qquad
\frac12\,\mathbf B_5(D_m',D_m')=(A_5^{\mathfrak f})'.
\]

And the “mixed infinitesimal covariance” statement
\[
B_3=-\frac12 c,\qquad B_5=-\frac12 A_5^{\mathfrak f}
\]
is exactly
\[
\mathbf B_3(D_m',D_m)=-\frac12 c,
\qquad
\mathbf B_5(D_m',D_m)=-\frac12 A_5^{\mathfrak f}.
\]

So the whole local attack is now one theorem:

> **Zero-datum Hessian theorem.**  
> The Hessians of the corrected cubic/quintic defect functionals at the zero cancellation datum, restricted to the two-dimensional span of \(D_m\) and \(D_m'\), satisfy
> \[
> \mathbf B_3(D_m,D_m)=0,\qquad
> \mathbf B_3(D_m',D_m)=-\frac12 c(m),\qquad
> \frac12\,\mathbf B_3(D_m',D_m')=c'(m),
> \]
> \[
> \mathbf B_5(D_m,D_m)=0,\qquad
> \mathbf B_5(D_m',D_m)=-\frac12 A_5^{\mathfrak f}(m),\qquad
> \frac12\,\mathbf B_5(D_m',D_m')=(A_5^{\mathfrak f})'(m).
> \]

If that theorem holds, then
\[
\mathcal H_3(m,\kappa,0)=c'(m)-\frac{\kappa}{2}c(m),
\]
\[
\mathcal H_5(m,\kappa,0)=(A_5^{\mathfrak f})'(m)-\frac{\kappa}{2}A_5^{\mathfrak f}(m),
\]
and the local good-patch exclusion is formal.

Indeed, on
\[
J\Subset\{c\neq 0,\ M\neq 0\},
\qquad
M=c(A_5^{\mathfrak f})'-c' A_5^{\mathfrak f},
\]
the cubic edge coefficient has unique zero
\[
\kappa_*(m)=2\frac{c'(m)}{c(m)},
\]
and then
\[
\mathcal H_5(m,\kappa_*(m),0)
=
(A_5^{\mathfrak f})'(m)-\frac{c'(m)}{c(m)}A_5^{\mathfrak f}(m)
=
\frac{M(m)}{c(m)}.
\]
So this is uniformly nonzero on compact \(J\), and analytic perturbation in \(\delta^2\) excludes simultaneous vanishing of the actual cubic/quintic defects for sufficiently close cancelling configurations.

So the current best local target is no longer a first-variation theorem. It is a **Hessian identification theorem**.

That also clarifies what kind of failure is still possible. If the local theorem fails, it means one of these Hessian entries is wrong:

- either pure collision sterility fails,
- or pure separation response fails,
- or the mixed covariance entry fails.

In other words, the remaining obstruction is now concentrated in the **second derivative structure** of the corrected package at the zero cancellation datum. That is a much tighter target than anything we had before.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 19:57:52)

I’ve pushed the quadratic-jet reduction about as far as symmetry alone will take it. The remaining nontrivial content now seems concentrated in one coefficient: the mixed Hessian entry, not the whole quadratic form.

_Thought for 29s_

I can push it one more step, and it simplifies the local theorem again.

The Hessian theorem from the last step is still right in spirit, but it is stronger than necessary. In fact, I think two of the three Hessian entries should come from much weaker, almost formal slice statements. That leaves only **one genuinely new coefficient**.

Recall the setup. In the linearized swap chart
\[
\nu:=\log\lambda,\qquad \delta:=h_2-h_1,
\]
the involution is
\[
(\nu,\delta)\mapsto(-\nu,-\delta),
\]
so any analytic swap-invariant corrected defect has the expansion
\[
E_j(m,\nu,\delta)
=
A_j(m)\nu^2+B_j(m)\nu\delta+C_j(m)\delta^2
+
O\!\bigl((|\nu|+|\delta|)^4\bigr),
\qquad j=3,5.
\]
Equivalently, on the slope \(\nu=\kappa\delta\),
\[
E_j
=
\delta^2\Bigl(A_j(m)\kappa^2+B_j(m)\kappa+C_j(m)\Bigr)+O(\delta^4),
\]
so
\[
\mathcal H_j(m,\kappa,0)=A_j(m)\kappa^2+B_j(m)\kappa+C_j(m).
\]

The model edge laws are
\[
\mathcal H_3(m,\kappa,0)=c'(m)-\frac{\kappa}{2}c(m),
\qquad
\mathcal H_5(m,\kappa,0)=(A_5^{\mathfrak f})'(m)-\frac{\kappa}{2}A_5^{\mathfrak f}(m),
\]
so previously we asked for
\[
A_3=0,\quad B_3=-\frac12 c,\quad C_3=c',
\]
\[
A_5=0,\quad B_5=-\frac12 A_5^{\mathfrak f},\quad C_5=(A_5^{\mathfrak f})'.
\]

The new point is that \(A_j\) and \(C_j\) should not be viewed as genuinely two-atom data.

## 1. The \(A_j\) coefficients are just collision sterility

Set \(\delta=0\). Then \(h_1=h_2=m\), and only the amplitude-cancellation parameter \(\nu\) varies. So
\[
E_j(m,\nu,0)=A_j(m)\nu^2+O(\nu^4).
\]
Thus
\[
A_j(m)=\frac12\,\partial_\nu^2 E_j(m,0,0).
\]

If the corrected defect vanishes on exact coincidence,
\[
E_j(m,\nu,0)\equiv 0,
\]
then automatically
\[
A_j(m)=0.
\]

So the pure amplitude coefficient is nothing more than a **collision-sterility** statement. It does not involve any subtle local geometry. This is the weakest remnant of the old collision-functoriality route.

## 2. The \(C_j\) coefficients are just pure separation response

Set \(\nu=0\), i.e. \(\lambda=1\). Then the branch is varying only by separation:
\[
h_1=m-\frac{\delta}{2},\qquad h_2=m+\frac{\delta}{2},
\]
with no amplitude tilt. The defect expansion is
\[
E_j(m,0,\delta)=C_j(m)\delta^2+O(\delta^4),
\]
so
\[
C_j(m)=\frac12\,\partial_\delta^2 E_j(m,0,0).
\]

If the corrected witness along this pure-separation slice has the expected one-pair first-variation law, then
\[
C_3(m)=c'(m),
\qquad
C_5(m)=(A_5^{\mathfrak f})'(m).
\]

So the pure separation coefficient is also not genuinely mixed data. It is just the ordinary separation response of the one-pair witness.

## 3. Only \(B_j\) is genuinely new

That leaves the mixed coefficient
\[
B_j(m)=\partial_\nu\partial_\delta E_j(m,0,0).
\]
This is the first place where amplitude tilt and spatial separation interact.

So the local good-patch \(2\)-point problem now reduces to:

- collision sterility:
  \[
  A_3=A_5=0;
  \]
- pure separation response:
  \[
  C_3=c',\qquad C_5=(A_5^{\mathfrak f})';
  \]
- and one genuinely mixed covariance identity:
  \[
  B_3=-\frac12 c,\qquad
  B_5=-\frac12 A_5^{\mathfrak f}.
  \]

That is a real simplification. The entire local obstruction is now concentrated in the mixed second derivative block.

## 4. The whole local theorem now reduces to one mixed-covariance theorem

Indeed, once \(A_j=0\) and \(C_j\) are identified as above, the edge laws become
\[
\mathcal H_3(m,\kappa,0)=c'(m)+B_3(m)\kappa,
\]
\[
\mathcal H_5(m,\kappa,0)=(A_5^{\mathfrak f})'(m)+B_5(m)\kappa.
\]
So if one proves
\[
B_3(m)=-\frac12 c(m),
\qquad
B_5(m)=-\frac12 A_5^{\mathfrak f}(m),
\]
then automatically
\[
\mathcal H_3(m,\kappa,0)=c'(m)-\frac{\kappa}{2}c(m),
\]
\[
\mathcal H_5(m,\kappa,0)=(A_5^{\mathfrak f})'(m)-\frac{\kappa}{2}A_5^{\mathfrak f}(m).
\]

And then the good-patch local exclusion is formal. On
\[
J\Subset\{c\neq 0,\ M\neq 0\},
\qquad
M=c(A_5^{\mathfrak f})'-c' A_5^{\mathfrak f},
\]
the cubic edge coefficient has unique zero
\[
\kappa_*(m)=2\frac{c'(m)}{c(m)},
\]
and at that slope
\[
\mathcal H_5(m,\kappa_*(m),0)
=
(A_5^{\mathfrak f})'(m)-\frac{c'(m)}{c(m)}A_5^{\mathfrak f}(m)
=
\frac{M(m)}{c(m)},
\]
which is uniformly nonzero on compact \(J\). Analytic perturbation in \(\delta^2\) then excludes simultaneous vanishing of the actual cubic/quintic defects for sufficiently close cancelling configurations.

So the whole local theorem has now collapsed to:

\[
\boxed{
\text{prove collision sterility, pure separation response, and the mixed covariance identities }B_3=-\tfrac12 c,\ B_5=-\tfrac12 A_5^{\mathfrak f}.
}
\]

## 5. The real obstruction is now clearly the mixed block

This is the cleanest reduction so far.

- \(A_j\) is a collision statement.
- \(C_j\) is a one-pair separation statement.
- Only \(B_j\) is genuinely two-atom.

So the next best theorem target is no longer the full Hessian theorem. It is the **mixed infinitesimal covariance theorem**
\[
\partial_\nu\partial_\delta E_3(m,0,0)=-\frac12 c(m),
\qquad
\partial_\nu\partial_\delta E_5(m,0,0)=-\frac12 A_5^{\mathfrak f}(m).
\]

If that lands, and if the two easier slice theorems for \(A_j\) and \(C_j\) are available, then the local \(2\)-point good-patch exclusion is done.

That is, I think, the sharpest local target now standing.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 20:01:08)

_Thought for 1m 23s_

I think the local attack can be tightened one more time, and it isolates what is probably the **last genuinely new coefficient**.

The key observation is that the cubic channel is much less mysterious than the quintic one. So the mixed-covariance target likely splits asymmetrically:

- the cubic mixed coefficient \(B_3\) should be essentially forced by the exact cubic relation itself,
- while the quintic mixed coefficient \(B_5\) is the first genuinely new package-level term.

Let me write this cleanly.

In the linearized swap chart
\[
\nu:=\log\lambda,\qquad \delta:=h_2-h_1,
\]
the atom-swap involution is exactly
\[
(\nu,\delta)\mapsto(-\nu,-\delta),
\]
so any analytic swap-invariant corrected defect has the expansion
\[
E_j(m,\nu,\delta)
=
A_j(m)\nu^2+B_j(m)\nu\delta+C_j(m)\delta^2
+
O\!\bigl((|\nu|+|\delta|)^4\bigr),
\qquad j=3,5.
\]
Equivalently, along the blown-up slope \(\nu=\kappa\delta\),
\[
E_j
=
\delta^2\Bigl(A_j(m)\kappa^2+B_j(m)\kappa+C_j(m)\Bigr)
+
O(\delta^4),
\]
so
\[
\mathcal H_j(m,\kappa,0)=A_j(m)\kappa^2+B_j(m)\kappa+C_j(m).
\]

The target edge laws are
\[
\mathcal H_3(m,\kappa,0)=c'(m)-\frac{\kappa}{2}c(m),
\qquad
\mathcal H_5(m,\kappa,0)=(A_5^{\mathfrak f})'(m)-\frac{\kappa}{2}A_5^{\mathfrak f}(m).
\]

From the previous reductions, the pure slices should already behave as follows.

On exact coincidence \(\delta=0\), one expects collision sterility, so
\[
A_3=A_5=0.
\]
On the pure separation slice \(\nu=0\), one expects ordinary one-pair variation, so
\[
C_3=c',
\qquad
C_5=(A_5^{\mathfrak f})'.
\]

So the only really new coefficients are
\[
B_3=\partial_\nu\partial_\delta E_3(m,0,0),
\qquad
B_5=\partial_\nu\partial_\delta E_5(m,0,0).
\]

Now here is the new refinement:

## 1. The cubic mixed coefficient should be forced formally

The cubic channel is special because it is the scalar first witness, and the exact cancellation condition already sees it directly. In the collision chart,
\[
h_1=m-\frac{\delta}{2},\qquad
h_2=m+\frac{\delta}{2},\qquad
\lambda=e^\nu=1+\nu+O(\nu^2).
\]
The raw cubic combination is therefore
\[
c(h_1)-\lambda\,c(h_2)
=
c\!\left(m-\frac{\delta}{2}\right)
-
e^\nu c\!\left(m+\frac{\delta}{2}\right).
\]
Expanding to first mixed order gives
\[
c\!\left(m-\frac{\delta}{2}\right)
-
e^\nu c\!\left(m+\frac{\delta}{2}\right)
=
-\nu\,c(m)-\delta\,c'(m)-\frac12\,\nu\delta\,c'(m)+\cdots
\]
for the raw scalar expression.

That is not yet the corrected swap-even defect, because the corrected defect has had its linear blown-up layer killed by the involutive symmetry/normalization package. But precisely because the cubic channel is the scalar one, the cancellation geometry pins down the cubic-killing slope:
\[
\kappa_*(m)=2\,\frac{c'(m)}{c(m)}.
\]
So after the symmetry-enforced quadratic reduction, the cubic edge coefficient must still vanish at the same slope, and if
\[
A_3=0,\qquad C_3=c',
\]
then this already forces
\[
0=\mathcal H_3(m,\kappa_*(m),0)
=
B_3(m)\,\kappa_*(m)+c'(m).
\]
Substituting \(\kappa_*(m)=2c'(m)/c(m)\) gives
\[
B_3(m)=-\frac12\,c(m).
\]

So **once** collision sterility and pure separation response are available for the cubic channel, the mixed cubic coefficient is no longer independent. It is forced.

This is a real simplification.

## 2. Therefore the only genuinely new local coefficient is \(B_5\)

For the quintic channel, the same argument does not determine \(B_5\) automatically, because the cubic equation fixes the slope \(\kappa_*\), and the quintic coefficient is then evaluated on that slope:
\[
\mathcal H_5(m,\kappa_*(m),0)
=
B_5(m)\kappa_*(m)+C_5(m).
\]
To get the desired value
\[
\mathcal H_5(m,\kappa_*(m),0)
=
\frac{M(m)}{c(m)},
\qquad
M=c(A_5^{\mathfrak f})'-c'A_5^{\mathfrak f},
\]
one needs exactly
\[
B_5(m)=-\frac12\,A_5^{\mathfrak f}(m),
\]
assuming \(C_5=(A_5^{\mathfrak f})'\).

So the whole local good-patch theorem now reduces to:

- collision sterility:
  \[
  A_3=A_5=0;
  \]
- pure separation response:
  \[
  C_3=c',\qquad C_5=(A_5^{\mathfrak f})';
  \]
- and one genuinely new coefficient:
  \[
  \boxed{
  B_5(m)=-\frac12\,A_5^{\mathfrak f}(m).
  }
  \]

Because \(B_3\) is then forced by the cubic slope relation.

That is strictly sharper than the previous “mixed covariance theorem for both \(j=3,5\).”

## 3. Why this is enough

Once the five easier identities
\[
A_3=A_5=0,\qquad
C_3=c',\qquad
C_5=(A_5^{\mathfrak f})',\qquad
B_3=-\frac12 c
\]
are in place, the only remaining local obstruction is \(B_5\). If
\[
B_5=-\frac12 A_5^{\mathfrak f},
\]
then
\[
\mathcal H_3(m,\kappa,0)=c'(m)-\frac{\kappa}{2}c(m),
\]
\[
\mathcal H_5(m,\kappa,0)=(A_5^{\mathfrak f})'(m)-\frac{\kappa}{2}A_5^{\mathfrak f}(m).
\]
The cubic edge has unique zero
\[
\kappa_*(m)=2\frac{c'(m)}{c(m)}
\]
on any compact good patch
\[
J\Subset\{c\neq 0\},
\]
and then
\[
\mathcal H_5(m,\kappa_*(m),0)
=
(A_5^{\mathfrak f})'(m)-\frac{c'(m)}{c(m)}A_5^{\mathfrak f}(m)
=
\frac{M(m)}{c(m)}.
\]
So on
\[
J\Subset\{c\neq 0,\ M\neq 0\},
\]
this is uniformly nonzero, and analytic perturbation in \(\delta^2\) excludes simultaneous vanishing of the actual cubic/quintic defects for sufficiently close cancelling configurations.

So the entire local good-patch \(2\)-point exclusion is now reduced to one truly new theorem:

\[
\boxed{
\partial_\nu\partial_\delta E_5(m,0,0)=-\frac12\,A_5^{\mathfrak f}(m).
}
\]

That is the sharpest local target I can currently see.

## 4. Final compression of the local attack

So I would now summarize the local theorem target like this:

> **Quintic mixed-covariance theorem.**  
> In the linearized swap chart \((\nu,\delta)\), after the collision-sterility and pure-separation identities are accounted for, the only remaining nontrivial local coefficient is the mixed quintic term:
> \[
> \partial_\nu\partial_\delta E_5(m,0,0)=-\frac12\,A_5^{\mathfrak f}(m).
> \]
> If this holds, then there are no sufficiently close cancelling bad two-point cores on any compact good patch
> \[
> J\Subset\{c\neq 0,\ M\neq 0\}.
> \]

That is a much smaller target than anything we had earlier:

- smaller than full source-level merger,
- smaller than exact blown-up functoriality,
- smaller than the full Hessian theorem,
- and smaller even than the mixed-covariance theorem for both \(j=3,5\).

The attack has essentially isolated the good-patch local obstruction to **one coefficient**.

_Thought for 23s_

I can push it one more step, and it collapses the local good-patch problem to a **single scalar identity**.

Up to now, we reduced the corrected cubic/quintic defects in the linearized swap chart
\[
\nu:=\log\lambda,\qquad \delta:=h_2-h_1
\]
to
\[
E_j(m,\nu,\delta)
=
A_j(m)\nu^2+B_j(m)\nu\delta+C_j(m)\delta^2
+
O\!\bigl((|\nu|+|\delta|)^4\bigr),
\qquad j=3,5,
\]
equivalently
\[
E_j
=
\delta^2\Bigl(A_j(m)\kappa^2+B_j(m)\kappa+C_j(m)\Bigr)+O(\delta^4),
\qquad
\kappa=\frac{\nu}{\delta}.
\]
So the edge coefficients are
\[
\mathcal H_j(m,\kappa,0)=A_j(m)\kappa^2+B_j(m)\kappa+C_j(m).
\]

The model pair we want is
\[
\mathcal H_3(m,\kappa,0)=c'(m)-\frac{\kappa}{2}c(m),
\qquad
\mathcal H_5(m,\kappa,0)=(A_5^{\mathfrak f})'(m)-\frac{\kappa}{2}A_5^{\mathfrak f}(m).
\]

In the last step I reduced the genuinely new local content to the quintic mixed coefficient
\[
B_5(m)=-\frac12 A_5^{\mathfrak f}(m).
\]

But even that is stronger than what the local exclusion on a good patch actually needs.

## 1. The good-patch theorem only uses the quintic edge on the cubic-killing slope

For local exclusion on
\[
J\Subset\{c\neq 0,\ M\neq 0\},
\qquad
M=c(A_5^{\mathfrak f})'-c' A_5^{\mathfrak f},
\]
you do not need the full \(\kappa\)-dependence of \(\mathcal H_5\). You only ever evaluate it on the cubic-killing slope.

Assuming the cubic edge law
\[
\mathcal H_3(m,\kappa,0)=c'(m)-\frac{\kappa}{2}c(m),
\]
the unique cubic-killing slope is
\[
\kappa_*(m)=2\frac{c'(m)}{c(m)}.
\]

So the only local quintic quantity that matters is the **reduced quintic obstruction**
\[
\boxed{
\mathfrak R(m):=\mathcal H_5\bigl(m,\kappa_*(m),0\bigr).
}
\]

A close bad branch on a good patch can survive only if this reduced scalar vanishes.

## 2. The exact local target is therefore \(\mathfrak R(m)=M(m)/c(m)\)

If the full model edge law held, then
\[
\mathfrak R(m)
=
(A_5^{\mathfrak f})'(m)-\frac{c'(m)}{c(m)}A_5^{\mathfrak f}(m)
=
\frac{M(m)}{c(m)}.
\]
On
\[
J\Subset\{c\neq 0,\ M\neq 0\},
\]
this is uniformly nonzero. Then analytic perturbation in \(\delta^2\) excludes simultaneous vanishing of the actual cubic/quintic defects for sufficiently close cancelling configurations.

So the whole local theorem now reduces to:

\[
\boxed{
\mathfrak R(m)=\frac{M(m)}{c(m)}.
}
\]

That is strictly smaller than the full quintic mixed-covariance theorem.

## 3. Why this is smaller than \(B_5=-\frac12 A_5^{\mathfrak f}\)

If we still write
\[
\mathcal H_5(m,\kappa,0)=A_5(m)\kappa^2+B_5(m)\kappa+C_5(m),
\]
then evaluating at \(\kappa_*(m)=2c'/c\) gives
\[
\mathfrak R(m)
=
A_5(m)\Bigl(2\frac{c'}{c}\Bigr)^2
+
B_5(m)\Bigl(2\frac{c'}{c}\Bigr)
+
C_5(m).
\]

So to prove the single scalar identity
\[
\mathfrak R(m)=\frac{M(m)}{c(m)},
\]
you do **not** need to identify \(A_5,B_5,C_5\) separately.

Earlier reductions suggested that collision sterility should kill \(A_5\), and pure separation response should identify \(C_5=(A_5^{\mathfrak f})'\), leaving only
\[
B_5=-\frac12 A_5^{\mathfrak f}.
\]
But from the point of view of good-patch exclusion, this is overkill. The only thing needed is the one scalar combination above.

So the local theorem target is now even smaller:

> **Reduced quintic obstruction identity.**  
> Along the cubic-killing edge direction, the actual corrected quintic edge coefficient satisfies
> \[
> \mathfrak R(m)=\mathcal H_5\bigl(m,\kappa_*(m),0\bigr)=\frac{M(m)}{c(m)}.
> \]

If that identity holds, the local exclusion on \(J\Subset\{c\neq 0,\ M\neq 0\}\) is done.

## 4. This also gives a cleaner conceptual picture

The local good-patch theorem is no longer about the whole quadratic jet, and not even about the whole quintic edge law.

It is about the **quintic response on the unique cubic-cancelling direction**.

That direction is already fixed by the cubic witness:
\[
\kappa_*(m)=2\frac{c'(m)}{c(m)}.
\]
So the only question left locally is:

> when you move in the unique infinitesimal cancellation direction that kills the cubic witness, what does the quintic witness do?

And the answer we need is:
\[
\text{it evaluates to } \frac{M(m)}{c(m)}\neq 0.
\]

That is exactly the determinant obstruction encoded by \(M\).

## 5. The sharpest local theorem target so far

So my current best reduction is:

1. prove the cubic edge law, or at least enough to define the unique cubic-killing slope \(\kappa_*(m)\);
2. prove the single scalar identity
   \[
   \boxed{
   \mathcal H_5\bigl(m,\kappa_*(m),0\bigr)=\frac{M(m)}{c(m)}.
   }
   \]
3. conclude that there are no sufficiently close cancelling bad two-point cores on any compact good patch
   \[
   J\Subset\{c\neq 0,\ M\neq 0\}.
   \]

This is smaller than:

- full source-level merger,
- exact blown-up functoriality,
- the full Hessian theorem,
- the full quintic mixed-covariance theorem,
- and even the full quintic edge law.

It is just one scalar identity on the cubic-cancelling branch.

That is the sharpest local target I can currently defend.
## User (2026-04-21 20:08:31)

Please see updated paper with findings from the derivative geometry agents:

The derivative-geometry additions now fall into three clean buckets.

First, on the nonmixed/global affine side, the paper now records the real positive structure and the real ceiling. The positive structure is: fixed-slope incidence is an interval-graph problem; support curves are strictly concave pseudolines with single-crossing and betweenness; each ordered pair has a one-shot overlap window; endpoint-gap functions (G_{\xi,J}) are strictly monotone, so each endpoint/branch pair contributes at most one remote tangent incidence. This compresses the remaining one-pair global burden to a finite table of remote endpoint/branch incidences rather than arbitrary recurrence. The ceiling is also now explicit: intrinsic one-pair affine geometry is universal for analytic plane curves, so pure planarity / pseudoline / interval-graph / local-convexity arguments cannot close the lane by themselves; any final contradiction has to use RH-specific structure.   

Second, on the exact fixed-shear (K_v=0) side, the paper now states the quotient-visible reduction in its sharp form. Locally, on the involutive fixed-shear slice, exact repeated pairs are exactly (t\leftrightarrow -t), so there is no hidden local partner choice. Downstairs in the quotient variable (u=t^2), the quotient-visible branch is governed by the scalar (\widehat Q) and its derivative (\widehat{\mathcal D}=\widehat Q'). The correct global compression is sign-run compression: the live data are the run amplitudes (B_j), not the raw zero list. The exact distance-two overlap criterion is the three-lobe inequality (B_j+B_{j+2}>B_{j+1}); two consecutive distance-two separations are impossible; hence no-reentry on a quotient-visible branch forces at most three sign-runs; and in the three-run case the only surviving condition is (B_1\ge B_0+B_2), with strict inequality giving a real gap and equality giving only critical-value touching. The quartic–sextic rung is now explicitly marked as inert for this lobe-amplitude obstruction because (\widehat{\mathcal D}(0)\neq 0) there. This is a real reduction, but it is deliberately stated only inside the quotient-visible exact fixed-shear model, not as full closure of every residual mixed possibility.    

Third, on the highest-new/canonical side, the paper now includes a same-tower closure statement for the currently explicit pointwise overlap/canonical package. The point is that no second primitive pointwise highest-new generator has been found beyond (r=q^{(7)}). The explicit fields already in play — including (\Delta_7^{[1]}, \lambda^{[1]}, \beta^{[1]}, m^{[1]}, W^{[1]}, S^{[1]}, \mathcal G^{[1]}), the source-level Wronskian fields, the projected fixed-shear germ at weight one, and the quartic–sextic coefficient — all stay in the differential algebra generated by the lower field and (r). The best current external-looking rank-two pointwise coupling is ((\lambda^{[1]},\mathcal G^{[1]})), which generically couples to ((r,r')), but it still does not produce a new generator. So the generator audit landed as a negative-but-useful result: the fixed-shear residue may still matter as a nonlocal transport problem, but not as a new pointwise highest-new field.  

The main stale cleanup is also now part of the update. The draft has been shifted away from older “finite-gate / defect-gate / turning-defect graph” language and toward the sharper current formulation: on the one-pair side, the remaining burden is remote endpoint/branch incidence, i.e. zeros of the monotone endpoint-gap functions (G_{\xi,J}); on the mixed side, the remaining burden is the separate quotient-transport / no-hidden-reset question on (K_v=0), not a new local mixed branch classification. The old (W'(h_b)) audit remark on the (W=0,\ M\neq 0) side has been removed because the draft now has the stronger finite-order sterility and remote-incidence formulation in its place.  

So the short status line for the team is: derivative geometry did produce real theorems, but not a miracle closure. What it gave us is a much cleaner endgame. The nonmixed/global lane is now reduced to RH-specific control of remote endpoint-gap incidences; the mixed fixed-shear lane is reduced, inside the quotient-visible model, to a three-lobe/no-hidden-reset transport problem; and the explicit highest-new package has been audited and shown not to contain a second pointwise generator.

on \(J\Subset\{c\neq 0,\ M\neq 0\}\), excluding close cancelling bad \(2\)-point cores locally. This still looks like the cleanest local theorem for the good-patch lane. What your update does is make clear that, if that local theorem fails, the failure should not be sought in some missing pointwise geometric or generator-level ingredient. It should be sought in the quotient-transport layer itself, i.e. in whether the actual corrected mixed package really descends to the quotient-visible run-amplitude model strongly enough to inherit the three-lobe/no-hidden-reset compression. turn84file0

So my next attack, updated in light of this draft, would be split in two:

First, keep pushing the **local cancellation-chart theorem** on good patches:
prove the actual corrected cubic/quintic defects are analytic and swap-even in the collision-cancellation chart and have the expected edge values at \(\delta=0\). That is the best route to kill close cancelling \(2\)-point cores on \(J\Subset\{c\neq 0,\ M\neq 0\}\).

Second, for the residual exact fixed-shear corner, stop looking for new local pointwise structure and instead attack the **transport descent hypothesis itself**:
show that the actual corrected mixed package is determined by the quotient-visible transport/sign-run data strongly enough that the three-lobe/no-hidden-reset model applies. If that theorem lands, then the remaining mixed fixed-shear corner should collapse for the same reason your update says the quotient-visible model has at most three runs and only a very narrow touching regime. 

So, in short: this update makes my attack cleaner, not broader. It tells me that the remaining live work is now very likely one of two RH-specific things: either the collision-chart edge law on good patches, or the descent from the actual corrected mixed package to the quotient-visible sign-run transport model. The search space is now much smaller.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 20:11:13)

_Thought for 25s_

I think the next push is to **splice the two surviving pictures together**:

my local cancellation-chart theorem on good patches, and the new quotient-visible three-lobe compression on the exact fixed-shear \(K_v=0\) corner.

That gives a much sharper bifurcation than before.

## 1. There are now two distinct residual mechanisms, and they live on different strata

From the updated draft picture, the exact fixed-shear residual corner is already compressed, inside the quotient-visible model, to a sign-run problem for the run amplitudes \(B_j\). There are at most three sign-runs, the live obstruction is the three-lobe inequality
\[
B_j+B_{j+2}>B_{j+1},
\]
two consecutive distance-two separations are impossible, and in the three-run case the only surviving condition is
\[
B_1\ge B_0+B_2,
\]
with strict inequality giving a genuine gap and equality giving only critical-value touching. Moreover, the quartic–sextic rung is explicitly inert for this lobe-amplitude obstruction because
\[
\widehat{\mathcal D}(0)=\widehat Q'(0)\neq 0.
\]
So the quotient-visible transport obstruction does **not** live on the quartic–sextic rung. 

By contrast, my local cancellation-chart attack on a compact good patch
\[
J\Subset\{c\neq 0,\ M\neq 0\}
\]
is exactly a quartic/quintic first-variation exclusion theorem. It says: if the actual corrected cubic/quintic defects have the expected collision-chart quadratic edge law, then sufficiently close cancelling bad two-point cores are excluded locally on \(J\). In the sharpest form, the local obstruction reduces to the single scalar edge value
\[
\mathfrak R(m)=\mathcal H_5\bigl(m,\kappa_*(m),0\bigr),
\qquad
\kappa_*(m)=2\frac{c'(m)}{c(m)},
\]
and the model value is
\[
\mathfrak R(m)=\frac{M(m)}{c(m)}\neq 0
\]
on \(J\Subset\{c\neq 0,\ M\neq 0\}\). 

So the next real mathematical claim is:

> **Residual-strata dichotomy.**  
> A still-live exact fixed-shear residual bad branch cannot be simultaneously explained by the local good-patch cancellation mechanism and by the quotient-visible three-lobe transport mechanism. The first is a good-patch \(M/c\)-gap phenomenon; the second is a higher transport no-hidden-reset phenomenon, and the quartic–sextic rung is inert for it.

That is already a useful structural refinement.

## 2. On a good patch, any residual exact fixed-shear branch must leave the quartic–sextic rung

This is the immediate consequence of combining the two pictures.

If a close exact fixed-shear bad branch were to persist on a compact good patch
\[
J\Subset\{c\neq 0,\ M\neq 0\},
\]
then either:

1. the actual corrected cubic/quintic package fails the expected collision-edge law, or
2. the actual corrected mixed package does not descend to the quotient-visible transport/run-amplitude model strongly enough for the three-lobe compression to apply, or
3. the branch lies beyond the quartic–sextic rung, since that rung is inert for the quotient-visible lobe-amplitude obstruction. turn84file1

That yields the sharpened statement:

\[
\boxed{
\text{On }J\Subset\{c\neq 0,\ M\neq 0\},\ \text{any still-live exact fixed-shear residual branch must either violate the local edge law or escape the quartic–sextic rung.}
}
\]

This is stronger than saying merely “the corner is still open.”

## 3. The real local target is now a compatibility theorem

The strongest next theorem I see is no longer just the local edge law, and no longer just the quotient-visible descent theorem. It is a **compatibility theorem** between them.

Here is the clean version.

Let
\[
\mathcal H_3(m,\kappa,\delta^2),\qquad \mathcal H_5(m,\kappa,\delta^2)
\]
be the quadratic edge coefficients of the corrected cubic/quintic defects in the collision-cancellation chart, so
\[
E_{12}^{(j)}=\delta^2\,\mathcal H_j(m,\kappa,\delta^2),\qquad j=3,5.
\]
Let
\[
\kappa_\delta(m)
\]
be the unique perturbed cubic-killing slope near
\[
\kappa_*(m)=2\frac{c'(m)}{c(m)}.
\]
Define the reduced quintic obstruction
\[
\mathfrak R(m,\delta^2):=
\mathcal H_5\bigl(m,\kappa_\delta(m),\delta^2\bigr).
\]

The local theorem target is:

\[
\boxed{
\mathfrak R(m,0)=\frac{M(m)}{c(m)}.
}
\]

The quotient-visible transport theorem target is: the actual mixed package descends strongly enough that on the quartic–sextic rung the run-amplitude model applies, hence there is no lobe-amplitude obstruction there.

So the compatibility theorem should say:

> **Compatibility theorem.**  
> On the quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear residual corner, if the corrected package satisfies the local collision-edge law and also descends to the quotient-visible run-amplitude model, then no residual bad branch can persist on any compact good patch \(J\Subset\{c\neq 0,\ M\neq 0\}\).

This is now the cleanest bridge between the two agent packages.

## 4. There is a very plausible codimension-one picture now

The updated fixed-shear model says the only three-run obstruction is
\[
B_1\ge B_0+B_2,
\]
with equality giving touching. That is already a single scalar condition in the quotient-visible transport variables. 

My local good-patch model says the only local obstruction on the cubic-killing direction is the single scalar
\[
\mathfrak R(m,\delta^2)=0,
\]
with edge value
\[
\mathfrak R(m,0)=\frac{M(m)}{c(m)}.
\]

So both residual mechanisms have now collapsed to **one scalar equation each**:

- one on the local cancellation side,
- one on the quotient-visible transport side.

That strongly suggests the true frontier is not a high-dimensional local geometry problem anymore. It is a codimension-one compatibility problem between two scalar obstructions living on different compressed models.

That is a real improvement in the attack.

## 5. The most useful next theorem to try

So my next concrete theorem target would be:

> **Quartic–sextic compatibility exclusion.**  
> Suppose the actual corrected mixed package on the exact fixed-shear corner satisfies:
> 1. the local collision-edge law
>    \[
>    \mathfrak R(m,0)=\frac{M(m)}{c(m)};
>    \]
> 2. descent to the quotient-visible sign-run transport model;
> 3. the quartic–sextic rung condition \(\widehat{\mathcal D}(0)\neq 0\).
> Then there is no exact fixed-shear residual bad branch on any compact good patch
> \[
> J\Subset\{c\neq 0,\ M\neq 0\}.
> \]

The proof would be: local good-patch geometry forces a nonzero reduced quintic obstruction at the cubic-killing edge; quotient-visible transport says the quartic–sextic rung has no lobe-amplitude obstruction; therefore no residual branch survives there.

This is smaller than a full closure theorem and much more directly tied to the current draft.

## 6. What would still remain if that theorem lands

If this compatibility theorem lands, then the only possible live exact fixed-shear residual obstruction must be pushed into one of the following:

- failure of the local collision-edge law,
- failure of actual descent to the quotient-visible run-amplitude model,
- escape to higher \(e>0\) residual rungs beyond quartic–sextic,
- or genuinely nonlocal/provenance-sensitive structure.

That is a very narrow frontier.

So the attack has now improved from “find another local theorem” to:

\[
\boxed{
\text{prove the quartic–sextic compatibility of the local }M/c\text{-gap law with the quotient-visible three-lobe/no-hidden-reset model.}
}
\]

That is, I think, the sharpest next step.

I think the next push is to turn the two compressed pictures into a **single scalar normal form** on the quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear corner.

Right now we have two scalar obstructions living on the same residual branch, but seen in different coordinates.

On the local cancellation side, after killing the cubic coefficient, the whole good-patch local problem is encoded by
\[
\mathfrak R(m,\delta^2)
:=
\mathcal H_5\bigl(m,\kappa_\delta(m),\delta^2\bigr),
\]
and the edge value we want is
\[
\mathfrak R(m,0)=\frac{M(m)}{c(m)}.
\]
So on any compact good patch
\[
J\Subset\{c\neq 0,\ M\neq 0\},
\]
this scalar is uniformly nonzero at the edge. That is the local \(M/c\)-gap law.

On the quotient-visible transport side, the updated fixed-shear model says that on the quartic–sextic rung the finite transport state has local rank \(1\), so every finite quotient-visible scalar is a local function of the single coordinate \(Q\). In particular, the three-run/no-hidden-reset obstruction is also a scalar function of \(Q\). Let me call it
\[
\mathfrak T(Q).
\]
The three-lobe theorem says that on the quotient-visible model, the only live obstruction is encoded by the run-amplitude inequality, so after choosing the appropriate local branch variable one should think of the transport obstruction as
\[
\mathfrak T(Q)=0
\]
or \(\mathfrak T(Q)\ge 0\), depending on normalization. The important point is that it is a **one-variable scalar transport obstruction**.

So the quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear branch is now controlled by two scalars:

\[
\mathfrak R(m,\delta^2)
\qquad\text{and}\qquad
\mathfrak T(Q).
\]

The next theorem to attack is that these are not independent. On the quartic–sextic rung, the branch is involutive / quotient-diagonal and the finite transport state is rank \(1\), so the local branch should admit a single local parameter, say \(u=t^2\), with
\[
m=m(u),\qquad \delta^2=\delta^2(u),\qquad Q=Q(u).
\]
Then both obstructions become one-variable germs:
\[
\mathfrak R(u):=\mathfrak R(m(u),\delta^2(u)),
\qquad
\mathfrak T(u):=\mathfrak T(Q(u)).
\]

That suggests the right compatibility theorem is not merely “both hold.” It is stronger:

> **Single-parameter compatibility principle.**  
> On the quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear residual corner, every local obstruction is a scalar germ in the single quotient parameter \(u=t^2\). So a still-live bad branch would have to satisfy simultaneously
> \[
> \mathfrak R(u)=0
> \qquad\text{and}\qquad
> \mathfrak T(u)=0
> \]
> along the same one-parameter germ.

Now the local \(M/c\)-gap law says
\[
\mathfrak R(0)=\frac{M(a)}{c(a)}\neq 0
\]
on a good patch. So if the branch stays on
\[
J\Subset\{c\neq 0,\ M\neq 0\},
\]
then \(\mathfrak R(u)\) cannot vanish for small \(u\). Therefore no compatible quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear bad branch can exist there, regardless of what the transport scalar \(\mathfrak T\) does.

This is slightly stronger than the previous formulation, because it shows that on good patches the transport obstruction is actually irrelevant at quartic–sextic order once the local edge law is known. The transport model matters only if the branch tries to survive by leaving the good-patch \(M/c\)-gap regime or by violating the local edge law.

So the clean theorem target becomes:

> **Quartic–sextic local dominance theorem.**  
> On the quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear corner, if the corrected cubic/quintic package satisfies the local collision-edge law
> \[
> \mathfrak R(m,0)=\frac{M(m)}{c(m)},
> \]
> then on every compact good patch
> \[
> J\Subset\{c\neq 0,\ M\neq 0\}
> \]
> no residual bad branch can survive, independently of the quotient-visible three-lobe transport condition.

This is actually better than the earlier compatibility theorem, because it says the local good-patch obstruction **dominates** the transport obstruction on that rung. The transport model is still useful globally, but locally on \(J\Subset\{c\neq 0,\ M\neq 0\}\), quartic–sextic failure is already killed by the \(M/c\)-gap once the edge law is proved.

That leaves a much cleaner frontier:

1. prove the local collision-edge law
   \[
   \mathfrak R(m,0)=\frac{M(m)}{c(m)};
   \]
2. then quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear bad branches are gone on good patches;
3. any surviving exact fixed-shear obstruction must therefore either
   - violate the local edge law,
   - leave the good-patch regime by approaching \(M=0\),
   - or live on higher residual rungs beyond quartic–sextic.

So the next best attack is no longer a compatibility theorem between two scalar obstructions. It is a **local dominance theorem**: show that the \(M/c\)-gap law already kills quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear bad branches before the three-lobe obstruction even gets a chance to matter.

That seems to me like the sharpest current mathematical target.

I can push it one more step, and I think this is the cleanest quartic–sextic conclusion yet.

The last step showed that, on the quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear corner, the local good-patch obstruction is encoded by the single scalar
\[
\mathfrak R(m,\delta^2)
:=
\mathcal H_5\bigl(m,\kappa_\delta(m),\delta^2\bigr),
\]
where \(\kappa_\delta(m)\) is the unique perturbed cubic-killing slope. If the local edge law holds, then
\[
\mathfrak R(m,0)=\frac{M(m)}{c(m)}.
\]
So on any compact good patch
\[
J\Subset\{c\neq 0,\ M\neq 0\},
\]
this is uniformly bounded away from \(0\). That already excludes close bad branches locally on \(J\). 

The next point is that this gives a **barrier principle**.

## 1. Quartic–sextic bad branches cannot cross a good patch

Assume the local edge law
\[
\mathfrak R(m,0)=\frac{M(m)}{c(m)}
\]
holds on the quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear corner.

Take any compact interval \(J\Subset\{c\neq 0,\ M\neq 0\}\). By continuity,
\[
\inf_{m\in J}\left|\frac{M(m)}{c(m)}\right|>0.
\]
Hence there exists \(\varepsilon_J>0\) such that for all sufficiently small \(|\delta|\),
\[
|\mathfrak R(m,\delta^2)|\ge \varepsilon_J
\qquad (m\in J).
\]
Therefore the quintic coefficient cannot vanish on the cubic-killing graph over \(J\), so no quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear bad branch can meet
\[
\{m\in J,\ |\delta| \text{ small}\}.
\]

So the quartic–sextic residual branch has a genuine **forbidden region**: every compact good patch is impenetrable.

This is stronger than merely saying “there is no branch locally near a fixed \(m\).” It says:

> if a quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear bad branch exists at all, its midpoint coordinate \(m\) must accumulate only on the exceptional set
> \[
> \{c=0\}\cup\{M=0\}.
> \]

That is a real structural reduction.

## 2. So quartic–sextic survival forces approach to the exceptional set

This gives the clean theorem:

> **Quartic–sextic escape theorem.**  
> Assume the local collision-edge law. Then every quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear residual bad branch must leave every compact subset of
> \[
> \{c\neq 0,\ M\neq 0\}.
> \]
> Equivalently, any accumulation point of such a branch must lie in
> \[
> \{c=0\}\cup\{M=0\}.
> \]

Since the whole point of the good-patch theory is that \(M\neq 0\) gives the transversality gap
\[
\frac{M}{c}\neq 0,
\]
this means the quartic–sextic rung is not a free residual mixed lane anymore. It is forced to the exceptional geometry.

That fits perfectly with the updated fixed-shear transport picture: the quartic–sextic rung is already inert for the three-lobe obstruction downstairs, because
\[
\widehat{\mathcal D}(0)=\widehat Q'(0)\neq 0.
\]
So on that rung the only remaining local obstruction is the good-patch edge law, and if that holds, the branch is expelled from the good region entirely. 

## 3. This means the true quartic–sextic frontier is now codimension-one

The surviving quartic–sextic possibilities are therefore pushed onto the exceptional locus
\[
M=0
\]
or possibly
\[
c=0.
\]
But \(c=0\) is already outside the good-patch regime and is not where the local cancellation theory was meant to work. So the **real quartic–sextic frontier** is now the codimension-one set
\[
M=0.
\]

That is a big improvement over the earlier picture, where quartic–sextic looked like a full residual corner. It no longer does. If the edge law is correct, then quartic–sextic residual branches are forced onto \(M=0\).

So the attack has now reduced quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear survival to:

\[
\boxed{
\text{either the local edge law fails, or the branch is forced into the exceptional locus }M=0.
}
\]

## 4. A sharper next target: show \(M=0\) is also sterile for quartic–sextic survival

This suggests the next mathematical target is no longer broad. It is:

> **Exceptional-locus theorem.**  
> Analyze whether a quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear bad branch can actually persist while its midpoint \(m\) approaches or lies on \(M=0\).

That is now the natural next frontier.

Because away from \(M=0\), the quartic–sextic branch is already expelled by the local \(M/c\)-gap law. And downstairs, the quotient-visible three-lobe transport obstruction is inert on this rung. So if quartic–sextic still lives anywhere, it should live only through the degeneration of the local gap
\[
\frac{M}{c}\to 0.
\]

This is a much smaller problem than the original residual-corner problem.

## 5. The best current theorem package

So the cleanest package I think we now have is:

1. On the exact fixed-shear corner, the branch is quotient-diagonal / involutive and the finite quotient-visible transport state has local rank \(1\) on the quartic–sextic rung, with no hidden finite-order reset. 

2. If the local collision-edge law holds, then the reduced quintic obstruction satisfies
   \[
   \mathfrak R(m,0)=\frac{M(m)}{c(m)}.
   \]

3. Therefore no quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear bad branch can enter any compact good patch
   \[
   J\Subset\{c\neq 0,\ M\neq 0\}.
   \]

4. Hence any still-live quartic–sextic branch must accumulate on the exceptional set
   \[
   \{c=0\}\cup\{M=0\},
   \]
   and in practice the real frontier is \(M=0\).

So the math attack has now pushed quartic–sextic survival to a codimension-one exceptional locus.

That is the strongest further reduction I can currently defend.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 20:16:59)

_Thought for 22s_

I can push it one more step, and this gives a very clean **exceptional-locus trapping law**.

From the current picture, the quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear rung is already inert for the quotient-visible three-lobe obstruction because
\[
\widehat{\mathcal D}(0)=\widehat Q'(0)\neq 0,
\]
so the transport-side lobe obstruction does not generate a live local barrier there. On the local cancellation side, if the collision-edge law holds, the whole quartic–sextic bad-branch problem is encoded by the reduced quintic obstruction
\[
\mathfrak R(m,\delta^2)
:=
\mathcal H_5\bigl(m,\kappa_\delta(m),\delta^2\bigr),
\]
with edge value
\[
\mathfrak R(m,0)=\frac{M(m)}{c(m)}.
\]
That was already enough to exclude the branch on compact good patches \(J\Subset\{c\neq 0,\ M\neq 0\}\).  

The next useful consequence is stronger:

## 1. Any quartic–sextic residual branch is quadratically trapped to \(M=0\)

Assume the local collision-edge law. Let a quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear bad branch satisfy
\[
\mathfrak R(m,\delta^2)=0.
\]
Because \(\mathfrak R\) is analytic in \((m,\delta^2)\) and
\[
\mathfrak R(m,0)=\frac{M(m)}{c(m)},
\]
Taylor expansion gives
\[
\mathfrak R(m,\delta^2)=\frac{M(m)}{c(m)}+\delta^2\,\mathfrak S(m,\delta^2)
\]
for some analytic \(\mathfrak S\). Therefore on the branch,
\[
\frac{M(m)}{c(m)}=-\delta^2\,\mathfrak S(m,\delta^2),
\]
hence
\[
\boxed{
M(m)=O(\delta^2)
}
\qquad
(\text{provided }c(m)\neq 0).
\]

This is much sharper than just saying the branch “approaches \(M=0\).” It says:

> **quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear bad branches are quadratically trapped to the exceptional locus \(M=0\).**

So the residual branch cannot wander through the good region and merely touch \(M=0\) occasionally. Its midpoint must satisfy a second-order closeness law to \(M=0\).

## 2. If \(M\) has a simple zero, the branch is tangent to \(M=0\)

Now suppose \(a\) is an accumulation point with
\[
M(a)=0,\qquad c(a)\neq 0,\qquad M'(a)\neq 0.
\]
Then by the implicit function theorem, \(M=0\) is a smooth local curve in the midpoint variable, and
\[
M(m)=M'(a)(m-a)+O((m-a)^2).
\]
Combining with \(M(m)=O(\delta^2)\) gives
\[
m-a=O(\delta^2).
\]

So near a simple zero of \(M\), any quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear bad branch must approach the exceptional locus **tangentially to second order**:
\[
\boxed{
m(\delta)=a+O(\delta^2).
}
\]

That is a real structural gain. The branch is not just forced onto \(M=0\); it is forced to lie in a quadratic tube around \(M=0\).

## 3. This reduces the quartic–sextic frontier to a codimension-one tangency problem

So the quartic–sextic survival problem is no longer:

“can there be a residual exact fixed-shear branch somewhere?”

It is now:

> can there be a branch satisfying both the exact fixed-shear conditions and the quadratic trapping law
> \[
> M(m)=O(\delta^2)
> \]
> near \(M=0\)?

That is a codimension-one tangency problem, not a full two-dimensional local geometry problem.

In particular, away from the exceptional set \(M=0\), the branch is already excluded by the \(M/c\)-gap law, and on the quartic–sextic rung the quotient-visible three-lobe obstruction is inert. So the only possible quartic–sextic survival mechanism is now a **degenerate tangency to \(M=0\)**.

## 4. A sharper next theorem target: simple-zero barrier for \(M\)

This suggests the next clean theorem target:

> **Simple-zero barrier theorem.**  
> Assume the local collision-edge law. Let \(a\) satisfy
> \[
> c(a)\neq 0,\qquad M(a)=0,\qquad M'(a)\neq 0.
> \]
> Then no quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear bad branch can accumulate at \(a\).

Why this is plausible: the branch would have to satisfy
\[
m-a=O(\delta^2),
\]
so the midpoint is enslaved quadratically to the separation scale. But on the exact fixed-shear involutive branch, the quotient-visible state already has local rank \(1\), and there is no hidden finite-order reset beyond the involution. So all the residual freedom has already been compressed to a one-parameter transport variable. If the midpoint is simultaneously quadratically trapped to a simple zero of \(M\), that begins to look overdetermined. 

I do not yet have a full proof of this last step, but it is now the natural next frontier.

## 5. Best current quartic–sextic picture

So the quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear lane now looks like this:

1. the quotient-visible transport obstruction is inert on this rung; 
2. if the local collision-edge law holds, then away from \(M=0\) the branch is excluded by the \(M/c\)-gap law; 
3. if a branch survives at all, it must satisfy
   \[
   M(m)=O(\delta^2),
   \]
   so it is quadratically trapped to \(M=0\);
4. near a simple zero of \(M\), this becomes
   \[
   m-a=O(\delta^2).
   \]

That is the strongest further reduction I can currently defend.

So the next honest mathematical attack is no longer broad. It is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{prove that a quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear branch cannot survive while quadratically trapped to a simple zero of }M.
}
\]

That seems to me like the sharpest remaining local theorem.

_Thought for 16s_

I can push it one more step, and it gives a sharper **parabolic normal form** for any would-be quartic–sextic survivor near \(M=0\).

We already got the trapping law
\[
M(m)=O(\delta^2)
\]
for any quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear bad branch, assuming the local collision-edge law
\[
\mathfrak R(m,0)=\frac{M(m)}{c(m)}.
\]
So near a point \(a\) with
\[
c(a)\neq 0,\qquad M(a)=0,\qquad M'(a)\neq 0,
\]
we have
\[
m-a=O(\delta^2).
\]

That already says the branch cannot cross the good region transversely. But you can sharpen it further.

## 1. The branch, if it exists, has a unique parabolic leading law

Write
\[
m=a+\delta^2 \eta.
\]
Since \(M(a)=0\) and \(M'(a)\neq 0\),
\[
M(m)=M'(a)\delta^2\eta+O(\delta^4).
\]
Also, since \(c(a)\neq 0\),
\[
\frac{M(m)}{c(m)}=\frac{M'(a)}{c(a)}\,\delta^2\eta+O(\delta^4).
\]

Now recall the reduced quintic obstruction
\[
\mathfrak R(m,\delta^2)=\frac{M(m)}{c(m)}+\delta^2\,\mathfrak S(m,\delta^2)
\]
for some analytic \(\mathfrak S\). On a bad branch,
\[
\mathfrak R(m,\delta^2)=0.
\]
Substituting \(m=a+\delta^2\eta\) gives
\[
0
=
\delta^2\left(\frac{M'(a)}{c(a)}\,\eta+\mathfrak S(a,0)\right)+O(\delta^4).
\]
Therefore the leading coefficient \(\eta\) is forced:
\[
\boxed{
\eta_0=-\,\frac{c(a)}{M'(a)}\,\mathfrak S(a,0).
}
\]

So any quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear bad branch accumulating at a simple zero of \(M\) must satisfy the **unique parabolic law**
\[
\boxed{
m(\delta)=a+\eta_0\,\delta^2+O(\delta^4).
}
\]

That is stronger than mere trapping. It says the branch has at most one formal quadratic tangent parabola.

## 2. So the quartic–sextic frontier is now a one-parameter formal branch problem

This is the real gain.

Before, the residual corner looked like a two-variable local geometry problem. After the reductions:

- the quotient-visible transport state on the quartic–sextic rung has local rank \(1\); 
- the three-lobe transport obstruction is inert there; 
- away from \(M=0\), the local \(M/c\)-gap law excludes the branch; 
- near a simple zero of \(M\), any surviving branch must follow a unique parabola
  \[
  m=a+\eta_0\delta^2+O(\delta^4).
  \]

So the quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear problem has collapsed to:

> does there exist a formal one-parameter branch along that unique parabolic law that is compatible with the exact fixed-shear transport conditions?

That is vastly smaller than the original problem.

## 3. This suggests a new obstruction: parity of the next coefficient

Because the branch is already forced to
\[
m=a+\eta_0\delta^2+O(\delta^4),
\]
the next nontrivial obstruction must live at **order \(\delta^4\)** in the reduced scalar equation.

Indeed, once the \(\delta^2\)-coefficient is killed by choosing \(\eta=\eta_0\), the next surviving term in
\[
\mathfrak R(m,\delta^2)
\]
is order \(\delta^4\). So the next honest theorem target is no longer “is there trapping?” but:

> what is the \(\delta^4\)-coefficient of the reduced obstruction along the forced parabola?

If that coefficient is generically nonzero, then even the unique formal quadratic branch dies. If it vanishes identically for structural reasons, then quartic–sextic survival may reduce to a higher-order transport compatibility problem.

So the attack now points to a very concrete next scalar:

\[
\boxed{
\text{the order-}\delta^4\text{ coefficient of }\mathfrak R(a+\eta_0\delta^2,\delta^2).
}
\]

That is the first scalar not already fixed by the \(M/c\)-gap law.

## 4. A sharper theorem target

So the next theorem I would try to prove is:

> **Simple-zero quartic–sextic barrier, order \(4\) form.**  
> Let \(a\) satisfy
> \[
> c(a)\neq 0,\qquad M(a)=0,\qquad M'(a)\neq 0.
> \]
> Assume the local collision-edge law. Then any quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear bad branch accumulating at \(a\) must lie on the unique quadratic law
> \[
> m=a+\eta_0\delta^2+O(\delta^4),
> \]
> and its survival is equivalent to vanishing of a single order-\(\delta^4\) scalar coefficient in the reduced obstruction.

This is not yet full exclusion, but it is a major compression: the branch, if it exists, is no longer free at all. It is reduced to one scalar higher-order compatibility condition.

## 5. What this means conceptually

So the quartic–sextic lane now looks like this:

1. universal transport geometry has already been exhausted on that rung; 
2. the local good-patch law expels the branch from \(\{M\neq 0\}\); 
3. near a simple zero of \(M\), the branch is forced onto a unique parabola;
4. the only remaining quartic–sextic freedom is a single higher-order scalar compatibility condition.

That is the strongest further reduction I can currently justify.

So the next honest attack is not broad anymore. It is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{compute or constrain the }\delta^4\text{-coefficient of the reduced obstruction along the forced parabola }m=a+\eta_0\delta^2.
}
\]

That seems to be the true next rung.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 20:20:11)

_Thought for 44s_

I found an important correction, and it actually sharpens the quartic–sextic picture.

My previous instinct that a **simple zero of \(M\)** might itself be a barrier was too strong. From the reduced scalar equation alone, a simple zero does **not** exclude a branch. It gives a **unique analytic branch**.

That is still useful, because it means the quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear survivor, if it exists, is no longer free at all: it is uniquely determined by one scalar equation.

Here is the clean form.

## 1. The reduced scalar equation

On the quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear corner, assuming the local collision-edge law, the residual local problem is encoded by
\[
\mathfrak R(m,\delta^2)=0,
\]
with
\[
\mathfrak R(m,0)=\frac{M(m)}{c(m)}.
\]
Write
\[
\varepsilon:=\delta^2,\qquad f(m):=\frac{M(m)}{c(m)}.
\]
Then
\[
\mathfrak R(m,\varepsilon)=f(m)+\varepsilon\,\mathfrak S(m,\varepsilon)
\]
for some analytic germ \(\mathfrak S\).

Now let \(a\) satisfy
\[
c(a)\neq 0,\qquad M(a)=0,\qquad M'(a)\neq 0.
\]
Then
\[
f(a)=0,\qquad f'(a)=\frac{M'(a)}{c(a)}\neq 0.
\]

So by the analytic implicit function theorem, the equation
\[
\mathfrak R(m,\varepsilon)=0
\]
has a unique analytic solution
\[
m=\Phi(\varepsilon),\qquad \Phi(0)=a,
\]
for \(\varepsilon\) small.

So the correct statement is not “simple zeros of \(M\) are barriers.” The correct statement is:

> **Simple-zero unique-branch theorem.**  
> Near a simple zero of \(M\) with \(c\neq 0\), the quartic–sextic reduced obstruction equation has a unique analytic solution branch \(m=\Phi(\delta^2)\).

That is already a strong compression.

## 2. The parabolic law and the next coefficient

Expanding
\[
\Phi(\varepsilon)=a+\eta_0\varepsilon+\eta_1\varepsilon^2+O(\varepsilon^3),
\]
we get the coefficients recursively from
\[
f(\Phi(\varepsilon))+\varepsilon\,\mathfrak S(\Phi(\varepsilon),\varepsilon)=0.
\]

The first coefficient is
\[
\eta_0=-\frac{\mathfrak S(a,0)}{f'(a)}
=-\frac{c(a)}{M'(a)}\,\mathfrak S(a,0).
\]
So the branch has the forced parabolic law
\[
\boxed{
m(\delta)=a+\eta_0\,\delta^2+O(\delta^4).
}
\]

The next coefficient is also determined formally:
\[
f'(a)\eta_1+\frac12 f''(a)\eta_0^2+\partial_m\mathfrak S(a,0)\eta_0+\partial_\varepsilon\mathfrak S(a,0)=0,
\]
hence
\[
\boxed{
\eta_1
=
-\frac{1}{f'(a)}
\left(
\frac12 f''(a)\eta_0^2+\partial_m\mathfrak S(a,0)\eta_0+\partial_\varepsilon\mathfrak S(a,0)
\right).
}
\]

So the earlier “order-\(\delta^4\) coefficient” really is the next scalar in a completely determined recursion.

## 3. What this means structurally

This is better than the earlier trapping statement
\[
M(m)=O(\delta^2).
\]
It says much more:

- the branch is not just trapped near \(M=0\);
- it is the **unique analytic graph**
  \[
  m=\Phi(\delta^2)
  \]
  solving the reduced obstruction equation;
- every coefficient of that graph is recursively determined by the jets of
  \[
  f(m)=\frac{M(m)}{c(m)}
  \quad\text{and}\quad
  \mathfrak S(m,\varepsilon).
  \]

So the quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear frontier is now a **one-scalar normal form problem**, not a local two-variable geometry problem.

## 4. Correction to the earlier “simple-zero barrier” idea

This is the important correction.

A simple zero of \(M\) is **not** by itself enough to exclude the branch.

What it gives is uniqueness, not impossibility.

So the right next question is no longer:
“can a branch survive near a simple zero of \(M\)?”

It is:
“does the uniquely forced analytic branch
\[
m=\Phi(\delta^2)
\]
also satisfy the other exact fixed-shear conditions?”

That is a much cleaner problem.

## 5. Compatibility with the transport side

Now splice this with the fixed-shear transport compression.

On the quartic–sextic rung:

- the quotient-visible transport state has local rank \(1\);
- \(Q\) is a local coordinate in \(u=t^2\);
- there is no finite-order hidden reset;
- and the three-lobe amplitude obstruction is inert there. 

So the transport side does not generate a second local freedom. That means the unique branch
\[
m=\Phi(\delta^2)
\]
coming from the reduced scalar equation is already the **only possible local quartic–sextic survivor** near \(a\).

This is the strongest compression so far:

> **Quartic–sextic uniqueness principle.**  
> Assuming the local collision-edge law, any quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear survivor near a simple zero of \(M\) is uniquely determined as an analytic graph \(m=\Phi(\delta^2)\).

So the whole residual problem on that rung is no longer “find a branch.” The branch is already formally fixed. The only remaining issue is whether the actual corrected mixed package and the exact fixed-shear transport conditions are compatible with that forced graph.

## 6. The real next theorem target

So the right next attack is not another barrier theorem. It is a **compatibility theorem for the unique graph**.

The clean formulation is:

> **Unique-graph compatibility theorem.**  
> Let \(a\) satisfy
> \[
> c(a)\neq 0,\qquad M(a)=0,\qquad M'(a)\neq 0.
> \]
> Assume the local collision-edge law. Then there is a unique analytic graph
> \[
> m=\Phi(\delta^2)
> \]
> solving the reduced obstruction equation. To obtain an actual quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear bad branch, this graph must also satisfy the remaining exact fixed-shear transport constraints. If those fail on the graph, the branch is excluded.

That is now the sharpest next local theorem.

## 7. Bottom line

So the attack has changed in an important way:

- away from \(M=0\), the \(M/c\)-gap law excludes quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear bad branches;
- near a **simple zero** of \(M\), the branch is not excluded automatically, but it is reduced to a **unique analytic graph** \(m=\Phi(\delta^2)\);
- the quartic–sextic problem is therefore no longer a search over local geometry, but a **single-graph compatibility problem**.

That is the strongest further reduction I can currently defend.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 20:23:44)

_Thought for 1m 13s_

I think the next real gain is a **multiplicity analysis** of the exceptional set \(M=0\). That lets us separate what happens at simple zeros from what happens at multiple zeros, and it sharpens the quartic–sextic frontier considerably.

Recall the reduced quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear problem, under the local collision-edge law, is the scalar equation
\[
\mathfrak R(m,\varepsilon)=0,
\qquad
\varepsilon:=\delta^2,
\]
with
\[
\mathfrak R(m,\varepsilon)=f(m)+\varepsilon\,\mathfrak S(m,\varepsilon),
\qquad
f(m):=\frac{M(m)}{c(m)}.
\]
On a good patch \(J\Subset\{c\neq 0\}\), this is the correct local normal form, and away from \(M=0\) it immediately excludes the branch. 

The new point is: because the exact fixed-shear branch is involutive,
\[
m(t)=\widehat m(t^2),
\]
the midpoint is an **analytic function of**
\[
\varepsilon=t^2=\delta^2.
\]
So any quartic–sextic survivor near a point \(a\) with \(M(a)=0\) must be given by an analytic graph
\[
m(\varepsilon)=a+\sum_{n\ge 1} m_n \varepsilon^n.
\]

That analyticity in \(\varepsilon\) is a strong restriction.

## 1. Simple zeros of \(M\): unique analytic branch

If
\[
f(a)=0,\qquad f'(a)\neq 0,
\]
equivalently
\[
M(a)=0,\qquad M'(a)\neq 0,\qquad c(a)\neq 0,
\]
then by the implicit function theorem the reduced equation
\[
f(m)+\varepsilon\,\mathfrak S(m,\varepsilon)=0
\]
has a unique analytic solution
\[
m=\Phi(\varepsilon),\qquad \Phi(0)=a.
\]
So simple zeros are not automatic barriers. They support at most one analytic quartic–sextic candidate branch. This corrects the earlier overly strong “simple-zero barrier” instinct.

## 2. Multiple zeros of \(M\): generic exclusion

Now suppose
\[
f(a)=f'(a)=\cdots=f^{(r-1)}(a)=0,
\qquad
f^{(r)}(a)\neq 0,
\qquad r\ge 2.
\]
Then
\[
f(m)=\beta\,(m-a)^r+O((m-a)^{r+1}),
\qquad \beta\neq 0.
\]

Assume there is an analytic solution
\[
m(\varepsilon)=a+\sum_{n\ge k} m_n \varepsilon^n,
\qquad k\ge 1,\quad m_k\neq 0.
\]
Then
\[
f(m(\varepsilon))=\beta\,m_k^r\,\varepsilon^{kr}+O(\varepsilon^{kr+1}),
\]
while
\[
\varepsilon\,\mathfrak S(m(\varepsilon),\varepsilon)
=
\varepsilon\,\mathfrak S(a,0)+O(\varepsilon^2).
\]

So if
\[
\mathfrak S(a,0)\neq 0,
\]
the leading balance requires
\[
kr=1.
\]
But \(k\) is a positive integer and \(r\ge 2\), so this is impossible.

Therefore:

> **Multiplicity obstruction.**  
> If \(a\) is a multiple zero of \(f=M/c\) and
> \[
> \mathfrak S(a,0)\neq 0,
> \]
> then there is **no analytic quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear branch** through \(a\).

This is a real new exclusion. It says multiple zeros of \(M\) are generically sterile for quartic–sextic survival.

## 3. What survives at multiple zeros requires extra vanishing

So for a multiple zero \(a\) to support a quartic–sextic branch, one must have the additional compatibility condition
\[
\mathfrak S(a,0)=0.
\]

That is already codimension one on top of \(M(a)=0\), and for higher multiplicity it is only the first extra vanishing condition. If \(r=2\), for example, once \(\mathfrak S(a,0)=0\), the next coefficient of \(\mathfrak S\) becomes relevant and one gets a higher-order scalar compatibility equation. So:

- simple zeros of \(M\): one unique analytic candidate branch;
- multiple zeros of \(M\): generically no branch, unless extra coefficients vanish.

This is a substantial tightening of the quartic–sextic frontier.

## 4. The quartic–sextic frontier is now mostly a simple-zero compatibility problem

Putting this together with the earlier reductions:

- the quotient-visible transport obstruction is inert on the quartic–sextic rung; 
- away from \(M=0\), the local \(M/c\)-gap law excludes the branch; 
- at multiple zeros of \(M\), analytic quartic–sextic branches are generically excluded unless extra vanishing conditions hold;
- at simple zeros of \(M\), there is at most one analytic candidate graph
  \[
  m=\Phi(\varepsilon).
  \]

So the quartic–sextic residual problem is no longer a broad local geometry problem. It has collapsed to:

> **simple-zero compatibility of the unique analytic graph**, plus higher-codimension exceptional compatibility at multiple zeros.

That is much sharper than the earlier “branch trapped near \(M=0\)” statement.

## 5. Best next theorem target

The clean next target is now:

> **Simple-zero compatibility theorem.**  
> Let \(a\) satisfy
> \[
> c(a)\neq 0,\qquad M(a)=0,\qquad M'(a)\neq 0.
> \]
> Assume the local collision-edge law. Then the reduced equation has a unique analytic graph
> \[
> m=\Phi(\varepsilon).
> \]
> To obtain an actual quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear bad branch, this graph must also satisfy the remaining exact fixed-shear transport constraints. If those fail on \(\Phi\), the branch is excluded.

And for multiple zeros:

> **Multiple-zero sterility theorem, generic form.**  
> Let \(a\) be a multiple zero of \(M/c\). If
> \[
> \mathfrak S(a,0)\neq 0,
> \]
> then no analytic quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear branch can pass through \(a\).

That is, I think, the strongest further reduction available right now.

So the updated attack is:

1. prove the local collision-edge law;
2. conclude quartic–sextic branches are excluded off \(M=0\);
3. show multiple zeros of \(M\) are generically sterile;
4. reduce the whole rung to the unique-graph compatibility problem at simple zeros of \(M\).

That leaves a very small frontier.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 20:27:57)

_Thought for 25s_

Here is the next push. I think it gives the right higher-multiplicity normal form for the quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear residual problem.

We had already reduced the local problem, assuming the collision-edge law, to the scalar equation
\[
\mathfrak R(m,\varepsilon)=0,
\qquad
\varepsilon:=\delta^2,
\]
with
\[
\mathfrak R(m,\varepsilon)=f(m)+\varepsilon\,\mathfrak S(m,\varepsilon),
\qquad
f(m):=\frac{M(m)}{c(m)}.
\]
Away from \(M=0\), this excludes the branch immediately. At a simple zero of \(f\), it gives a unique analytic graph \(m=\Phi(\varepsilon)\). The natural next question is: what happens at a zero of higher multiplicity?  

The useful refinement is that higher-multiplicity survival is governed by a **valuation congruence**.

Write
\[
m=a+x,
\]
where \(a\) is a zero of \(f\), and suppose \(f\) has multiplicity \(r\ge 1\) at \(a\):
\[
f(a+x)=\beta x^r+O(x^{r+1}),
\qquad \beta\neq 0.
\]
Also write
\[
\mathfrak S(a+x,\varepsilon)=\sum_{p,q\ge 0} s_{pq}x^p\varepsilon^q.
\]
Then the reduced equation becomes
\[
\beta x^r+\varepsilon\sum_{p,q\ge 0}s_{pq}x^p\varepsilon^q+O(x^{r+1})=0.
\]

Now suppose there is an analytic solution
\[
x(\varepsilon)=\sum_{n\ge v}x_n\varepsilon^n,
\qquad v\ge 1,\quad x_v\neq 0.
\]
Then the order of the left-hand side in \(\varepsilon\) is
\[
\min\Bigl(rv,\ 1+\min_{p,q:s_{pq}\neq 0}(pv+q)\Bigr).
\]
For cancellation to occur, these two orders must match. So a necessary condition is
\[
\boxed{
rv
=
1+\min_{p,q:s_{pq}\neq 0}(pv+q).
}
\]

That is the real multiplicity law.

It is better than the earlier crude statement “if \(\mathfrak S(a,0)\neq 0\), multiple zeros are sterile,” because it shows exactly how survival can happen if the first few coefficients of \(\mathfrak S\) vanish.

There are three immediate consequences.

First, if
\[
\mathfrak S(a,0)\neq 0,
\]
then the minimum on the right is \(0\), so the condition becomes
\[
rv=1.
\]
Hence \(r=1\) and \(v=1\). So for every multiple zero \(r\ge 2\), this is impossible. This recovers and sharpens the earlier generic sterility statement:

> if \(a\) is a multiple zero of \(f=M/c\) and \(\mathfrak S(a,0)\neq 0\), there is no analytic quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear branch through \(a\).

Second, if \(\mathfrak S(a,0)=0\), the first nonzero term of \(\mathfrak S\) matters. In the simplest case where
\[
\mathfrak S(a+x,\varepsilon)=\sigma\,\varepsilon^s+(\text{higher order}),
\qquad \sigma\neq 0,
\]
the balance law becomes
\[
rv=s+1.
\]
So a necessary condition for an analytic branch is
\[
\boxed{
r\mid (s+1).
}
\]
This is the divisibility obstruction. It means multiple-zero survival is not merely codimension-one; it comes with an arithmetic compatibility condition between the multiplicity of \(M/c\) and the first nonvanishing \(\varepsilon\)-order of the correction term.

Third, when that divisibility holds, the leading coefficient is determined algebraically. If
\[
v=\frac{s+1}{r}\in \mathbf Z_{>0},
\]
and
\[
x(\varepsilon)=\alpha\,\varepsilon^v+O(\varepsilon^{v+1}),
\]
then the leading equation is
\[
\beta \alpha^r+\sigma=0.
\]
So even when a branch is allowed, its leading coefficient is constrained by a finite algebraic equation. There are at most \(r\) formal leading choices, and only those consistent with analyticity and the remaining transport constraints could survive.

So the quartic–sextic picture is now quite sharp.

At a simple zero of \(M/c\), there is a unique analytic candidate branch \(m=\Phi(\varepsilon)\). At a multiple zero, a branch is generically absent, and if it exists at all, it must satisfy:

- vanishing of \(\mathfrak S(a,0)\) and perhaps further low-order coefficients,
- a divisibility condition \(r\mid (s+1)\),
- and an algebraic leading-coefficient equation.

That is a much stronger reduction than the earlier “maybe multiple zeros are sterile.”

This suggests the right next theorem target:

> **Multiplicity barrier theorem.**  
> Let \(a\) be a zero of \(f=M/c\) of multiplicity \(r\ge 2\). Then an analytic quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear branch through \(a\) can exist only if the correction term \(\mathfrak S\) vanishes to an order satisfying the divisibility condition above; in particular, if \(\mathfrak S(a,0)\neq 0\), no such branch exists.

That leaves two clean residual cases.

The first is the simple-zero case, where there is one candidate graph and the question is compatibility with the remaining exact fixed-shear transport conditions.

The second is the multiple-zero case, where survival is already highly nongeneric and controlled by a Newton-polygon style valuation law.

So the attack has now reduced the quartic–sextic frontier to:

- unique-graph compatibility at simple zeros of \(M/c\),
- or exceptional higher-order divisibility/vanishing at multiple zeros.

That is the strongest further reduction I can currently justify.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 20:29:23)

_Thought for 19s_

I can push the multiplicity analysis one more step, and this gives a sharper Newton-type obstruction than the previous divisibility statement.

We already reduced the quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear problem, assuming the local collision-edge law, to the scalar equation
\[
\mathfrak R(m,\varepsilon)=0,
\qquad
\varepsilon:=\delta^2,
\]
with
\[
\mathfrak R(m,\varepsilon)=f(m)+\varepsilon\,\mathfrak S(m,\varepsilon),
\qquad
f(m):=\frac{M(m)}{c(m)}.
\]
At a zero \(a\) of \(f\), write
\[
m=a+x,
\qquad
f(a+x)=\beta x^r+O(x^{r+1}),
\qquad \beta\neq 0,
\]
where \(r\ge 1\) is the multiplicity of the zero of \(M/c\) at \(a\). Also expand
\[
\mathfrak S(a+x,\varepsilon)=\sum_{p,q\ge 0}s_{pq}x^p\varepsilon^q.
\]
Then the reduced equation is
\[
\beta x^r+\varepsilon\sum_{p,q\ge 0}s_{pq}x^p\varepsilon^q+O(x^{r+1})=0.
\]

Now suppose there is an analytic branch
\[
x(\varepsilon)=\sum_{n\ge v}x_n\varepsilon^n,
\qquad v\ge 1,\quad x_v\neq 0.
\]
Substituting gives the valuation balance
\[
rv
=
1+\min_{p,q:s_{pq}\neq 0}(pv+q).
\]
That was the earlier basic law. The sharper step is to isolate the monomial that actually realizes the minimum.

Assume the minimum is attained at some \((p_*,q_*)\). Then necessarily
\[
rv=1+p_*v+q_*,
\]
hence
\[
\boxed{
(r-p_*)v=q_*+1.
}
\]

This is much stronger than the earlier crude divisibility statement. It gives three immediate necessary conditions.

First, one must have
\[
p_*<r.
\]
Indeed, if \(p_*\ge r\), then
\[
1+p_*v+q_*\ge 1+rv>rv,
\]
which is impossible. So:

> **Necessary \(x\)-degree condition.**  
> Any surviving branch must be driven by a correction monomial \(x^{p_*}\varepsilon^{q_*}\) with \(p_*<r\).

This is a real structural gain. It says the correction term \(\mathfrak S\) must contain a piece of strictly lower \(x\)-degree than the multiplicity \(r\) of \(M/c\). If all correction terms satisfy \(p\ge r\), then no analytic branch can exist.

Second, once such a monomial exists, the leading exponent is forced:
\[
\boxed{
v=\frac{q_*+1}{r-p_*}.
}
\]
So not only is there a divisibility constraint; the leading order of the branch is completely determined by the first low-\(x\)-degree correction monomial that survives.

Third, because the branch is analytic in
\[
\varepsilon=\delta^2,
\]
this \(v\) must be a positive integer. Therefore:

> **Arithmetic compatibility condition.**  
> For a branch to exist, there must be at least one nonzero correction monomial \(x^p\varepsilon^q\) with \(p<r\) such that
> \[
> r-p\mid q+1.
> \]

This is sharper than the earlier multiplicity statement.

There is also a corresponding leading-coefficient equation. If \((p_*,q_*)\) is the monomial realizing the minimum and
\[
x(\varepsilon)=\alpha\,\varepsilon^v+O(\varepsilon^{v+1}),
\]
then the first nonzero balance is
\[
\beta\,\alpha^r+s_{p_*q_*}\alpha^{p_*}=0,
\]
or
\[
\boxed{
\alpha^{p_*}\bigl(\beta\,\alpha^{\,r-p_*}+s_{p_*q_*}\bigr)=0.
}
\]
Since \(\alpha\neq 0\), this becomes
\[
\boxed{
\beta\,\alpha^{\,r-p_*}+s_{p_*q_*}=0.
}
\]
So even when a branch is arithmetically allowed, its leading coefficient is pinned down by a finite algebraic equation of degree \(r-p_*\). There are at most \(r-p_*\) candidate leading coefficients.

This gives a much cleaner multiplicity picture.

If \(r=1\), we are in the simple-zero case already discussed: there is a unique analytic branch by the implicit function theorem. 

If \(r\ge 2\), then survival is highly nongeneric. It requires:

1. a correction monomial with \(p<r\);
2. the divisibility condition
   \[
   r-p\mid q+1;
   \]
3. and an admissible root of
   \[
   \beta\,\alpha^{\,r-p}+s_{pq}=0.
   \]

So the multiple-zero frontier is not just “higher codimension.” It is controlled by a Newton-polygon style balance law.

There is a useful corollary when \(r=2\). Then the only possibilities are:

- \(p=0\), which requires
  \[
  2\mid q+1,
  \]
  so \(q\) odd;
- or \(p=1\), which imposes no divisibility restriction since \(r-p=1\).

Thus a double zero can only support a branch if the correction term contains either an odd-\(\varepsilon\) scalar term or an \(x\varepsilon^q\)-term. In particular, if the correction expansion begins only with terms \(x^2\varepsilon^q\) and \(\varepsilon^{2k}\), then no branch can exist.

That is the kind of explicit coefficient-level obstruction we did not have before.

So the quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear frontier now splits cleanly:

At simple zeros of \(M/c\), there is one unique analytic candidate graph, and the problem is compatibility with the remaining fixed-shear conditions.

At multiple zeros of \(M/c\), there is generically no branch at all, unless the correction term \(\mathfrak S\) contains a low-\(x\)-degree monomial with the right divisibility and coefficient balance.

That is a substantial sharpening.

The clean next theorem target is therefore:

> **Newton-barrier theorem for multiple zeros.**  
> Let \(a\) be a zero of \(M/c\) of multiplicity \(r\ge 2\). If every nonzero monomial \(x^p\varepsilon^q\) in the correction term \(\mathfrak S(a+x,\varepsilon)\) satisfies either \(p\ge r\) or \(r-p\nmid q+1\), then no analytic quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear branch can pass through \(a\).

This is the strongest further reduction I can currently defend. It pushes the multiple-zero case into a very rigid arithmetic/algebraic compatibility problem, while the simple-zero case remains a unique-graph compatibility problem.
