## Assistant (2026-04-21 20:32:31)

_Thought for 25s_

I think the next real gain is to put the quartic–sextic exceptional-locus analysis into **Weierstrass normal form**. That packages the simple-zero and multiple-zero cases into one theorem, and it shows the local residual problem is now a **finite branch problem**, not an open-ended geometry problem.

We are already in the reduced scalar situation: on the quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear corner, assuming the local collision-edge law, the residual local problem is
\[
\mathfrak R(m,\varepsilon)=0,
\qquad
\varepsilon:=\delta^2,
\]
with
\[
\mathfrak R(m,\varepsilon)=f(m)+\varepsilon\,\mathfrak S(m,\varepsilon),
\qquad
f(m):=\frac{M(m)}{c(m)}.
\]
Away from \(M=0\), this excludes the branch immediately, and the quotient-visible transport obstruction is inert on this rung because \(\widehat{\mathcal D}(0)\neq 0\).  

So fix a point \(a\) with
\[
c(a)\neq 0,\qquad M(a)=0,
\]
and let \(r\ge 1\) be the multiplicity of the zero of \(f=M/c\) at \(a\):
\[
f(a+x)=\beta x^r+O(x^{r+1}),
\qquad \beta\neq 0.
\]
Write
\[
m=a+x.
\]
Then
\[
\mathfrak R(a+x,\varepsilon)
=
\beta x^r+O(x^{r+1})+\varepsilon\,\mathfrak S(a+x,\varepsilon).
\]

Now apply the Weierstrass preparation theorem in the variable \(x\). Since the order of vanishing in \(x\) at \((x,\varepsilon)=(0,0)\) is \(r\), there exist:

- a unit \(U(x,\varepsilon)\) with \(U(0,0)\neq 0\),
- and a monic degree-\(r\) polynomial
  \[
  P(x,\varepsilon)
  =
  x^r+a_{r-1}(\varepsilon)x^{r-1}+\cdots+a_1(\varepsilon)x+a_0(\varepsilon),
  \]
  where each \(a_j(\varepsilon)\) is analytic and \(a_j(0)=0\),

such that
\[
\boxed{
\mathfrak R(a+x,\varepsilon)=U(x,\varepsilon)\,P(x,\varepsilon).
}
\]

This is the right normal form.

So the quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear residual equation near \(a\) is equivalent to
\[
P(x,\varepsilon)=0.
\]
That means:

> **Finite-branch reduction.**  
> Near any zero \(a\) of \(M/c\) of multiplicity \(r\), the quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear residual problem reduces to a degree-\(r\) Weierstrass polynomial in the single scalar variable \(x=m-a\), with analytic coefficients in \(\varepsilon=\delta^2\).

This already compresses the problem a lot. There are at most \(r\) local branches, counted with multiplicity.

It also recovers all the earlier cases cleanly.

If \(r=1\), then
\[
P(x,\varepsilon)=x+a_0(\varepsilon),
\]
so there is exactly one analytic branch
\[
x=-a_0(\varepsilon),
\qquad
m=a+\Phi(\varepsilon).
\]
That is the unique-graph theorem from before.

If \(r\ge 2\), then the Newton-balance obstruction we derived earlier can be read as a statement about the lowest-order coefficients of \(P\): a branch can only occur if the first nonzero coefficient pattern of the \(a_j(\varepsilon)\) satisfies the valuation/divisibility law. So the Newton barrier becomes a corollary of the Weierstrass form rather than a separate ad hoc argument.

This gives a better global statement:

> **Quartic–sextic Weierstrass theorem.**  
> Assuming the local collision-edge law, every quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear survivor near a zero \(a\) of \(M/c\) is one of finitely many analytic branches determined by the degree-\(r\) Weierstrass polynomial \(P(x,\varepsilon)\), where \(r=\operatorname{ord}_a(M/c)\).

That is stronger than saying “simple zeros give one branch and multiple zeros are restrictive.” It says the local problem is now finite and algebraic in a very literal sense.

There is also a useful structural consequence when you splice this with the quotient-visible transport compression.

On the quartic–sextic rung, the quotient-visible transport state has local rank \(1\), and there is no finite-order hidden reset beyond the involution pair.  So once the reduced scalar equation has been put into Weierstrass form, there is **no extra local freedom** coming from transport. The transport side cannot create a continuum of local partner choices or another branching parameter. Thus:

> **Finite-branch uniqueness principle on the quartic–sextic rung.**  
> After the local edge reduction, every quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear survivor is contained in the finite set of Weierstrass branches of \(P(x,\varepsilon)=0\); there is no additional local branching supplied by the quotient-visible transport state.

That is a real sharpening.

It also clarifies the multiple-zero case. Previously we had the Newton-barrier statement: if
\[
\mathfrak S(a,0)\neq 0
\]
at a multiple zero \(r\ge 2\), then no analytic branch exists, because the valuation balance would require \(rv=1\), impossible. In Weierstrass language, this means the coefficient pattern of the prepared polynomial starts in a way that admits no analytic root germ. More generally, the earlier divisibility condition
\[
(r-p)v=q+1
\]
is exactly the condition that the Newton polygon of \(P(x,\varepsilon)\) has an edge with integer slope supporting an analytic branch. So the “higher-multiplicity frontier” is now completely encoded by the prepared polynomial.

This suggests the next honest theorem target is no longer broad at all. It is:

> **Prepared-polynomial compatibility theorem.**  
> Show that for the actual corrected quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear package, the Weierstrass polynomial \(P(x,\varepsilon)\) has either:
> 1. degree \(1\) at simple zeros and its unique branch violates the remaining exact fixed-shear constraints, or
> 2. coefficient pattern at multiple zeros that violates the Newton/divisibility condition for analytic roots.

That would close the quartic–sextic lane completely.

At a minimum, the Weierstrass form gives a very clean division of labor:

- Away from \(M=0\): excluded by the \(M/c\)-gap law. 
- Near a simple zero of \(M/c\): one unique analytic candidate branch.
- Near a multiple zero: finitely many algebraically constrained candidate branches, often none generically.
- No further local freedom comes from the transport side on the quartic–sextic rung. 

So the attack has now reduced the quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear problem to a **finite Weierstrass-branch compatibility problem**. That is the sharpest compression I can currently defend.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 20:39:43)

_Thought for 31s_

Here is the next push. I think it gives the right **final local reduction theorem** for the quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear lane.

The updated paper now gives two facts that matter here.

First, on the exact fixed-shear \(K_v=0\) side, the quotient-visible branch is controlled by the scalar \(\widehat Q\), the finite quotient-visible transport state has local rank \(1\), and the quartic–sextic rung is inert for the three-lobe/sign-run obstruction because \(\widehat{\mathcal D}(0)\neq 0\). So on that rung there is no hidden transport branching or lobe-amplitude obstruction to sustain a residual branch. 

Second, on the local cancellation side, assuming the collision-edge law, the quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear problem is reduced to the scalar equation
\[
\mathfrak R(m,\varepsilon)=0,
\qquad
\varepsilon:=\delta^2,
\]
with
\[
\mathfrak R(m,\varepsilon)=f(m)+\varepsilon\,\mathfrak S(m,\varepsilon),
\qquad
f(m):=\frac{M(m)}{c(m)}.
\]
Away from \(M=0\), this already excludes the branch on compact good patches \(J\Subset\{c\neq 0,\ M\neq 0\}\). Near a zero \(a\) of \(f\), Weierstrass preparation reduces the problem to a finite-degree polynomial in \(x=m-a\). 

So the clean next theorem is not another geometric statement. It is a **finite-branch compatibility reduction**.

## 1. Weierstrass reduction gives finitely many candidate local branches

Fix \(a\) with
\[
c(a)\neq 0,\qquad M(a)=0,
\]
and let \(r=\operatorname{ord}_a(M/c)\). Writing \(m=a+x\), Weierstrass preparation gives
\[
\mathfrak R(a+x,\varepsilon)=U(x,\varepsilon)\,P(x,\varepsilon),
\]
where \(U(0,0)\neq 0\) and
\[
P(x,\varepsilon)
=
x^r+a_{r-1}(\varepsilon)x^{r-1}+\cdots+a_1(\varepsilon)x+a_0(\varepsilon)
\]
is monic of degree \(r\), with each \(a_j(\varepsilon)\) analytic and \(a_j(0)=0\).

Thus every quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear candidate near \(a\) must lie on one of the finitely many local roots of
\[
P(x,\varepsilon)=0.
\]

So the quartic–sextic residual problem has already become a **finite branch problem**.

## 2. On each prepared branch, the remaining exact fixed-shear conditions reduce to one scalar identity

Let
\[
x=\phi_i(\varepsilon),\qquad i=1,\dots,N,
\]
be the finitely many local analytic prepared branches that solve \(P=0\) when such branches exist.

Along each such branch, every local quantity on the quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear corner becomes a one-variable analytic germ in \(\varepsilon\). In particular:

- the midpoint is
  \[
  m_i(\varepsilon)=a+\phi_i(\varepsilon),
  \]
- the separation is encoded by \(\varepsilon=\delta^2\),
- and the quotient-visible transport state is a local function of the single scalar \(Q\), hence of \(\varepsilon\) along the branch, because on the quartic–sextic rung the transport state has local rank \(1\). 

So any remaining exact fixed-shear condition not already built into \(\mathfrak R=0\) pulls back to a single analytic scalar germ
\[
\Gamma_i(\varepsilon).
\]

A genuine quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear bad branch must therefore satisfy, for some prepared branch \(i\),
\[
\Gamma_i(\varepsilon)\equiv 0.
\]

This is the key reduction:

\[
\boxed{
\text{quartic–sextic survival } \Longrightarrow \text{ one of finitely many analytic scalar identities } \Gamma_i\equiv 0.
}
\]

So the residual problem is no longer “is there some local branch?” It is “does one of a finite set of one-variable analytic compatibility functions vanish identically?”

## 3. This kills generic persistence immediately

This has a strong consequence.

For an analytic bad branch to persist, it is not enough that \(\Gamma_i(0)=0\) or that \(\Gamma_i\) have isolated zeros. One needs
\[
\Gamma_i(\varepsilon)\equiv 0
\]
on an interval of \(\varepsilon\)-values near \(0\).

So quartic–sextic survival is now an **identity problem**, not a root-finding problem.

In particular:

- isolated touching of the remaining transport/compatibility condition along a prepared branch is harmless;
- genuine residual persistence requires exact analytic coincidence of one of the \(\Gamma_i\).

This meshes well with the updated quotient-visible fixed-shear story: on the quartic–sextic rung, the three-lobe obstruction is inert, so there is no robust transport-side inequality supporting a branch there. The only way to survive is by analytic identity along a prepared branch. 

## 4. Simple zeros and multiple zeros now fit into the same framework

This unifies the earlier cases.

If \(r=1\), there is one prepared branch
\[
x=\phi(\varepsilon),
\]
so the whole quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear problem near \(a\) reduces to a single analytic scalar identity
\[
\Gamma(\varepsilon)\equiv 0.
\]

If \(r\ge 2\), the Newton/divisibility analysis already showed that analytic prepared branches are highly restricted; often there are none. When they do exist, there are still only finitely many, and each again reduces to a one-variable analytic identity problem. So even in the multiple-zero case, the frontier is finite and scalar.

Thus:

> **Unified finite-identity principle.**  
> Near any zero of \(M/c\), quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear survival reduces to checking whether one of finitely many one-variable analytic compatibility germs vanishes identically.

That is a very strong compression.

## 5. The sharpest next target

So the next theorem to attack is:

> **Finite prepared-branch compatibility theorem.**  
> On the quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear rung, assuming the local collision-edge law, the remaining exact fixed-shear conditions restrict each prepared branch \(P(x,\varepsilon)=0\) to a one-variable analytic compatibility germ \(\Gamma_i(\varepsilon)\). If no \(\Gamma_i\) vanishes identically, then no quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear bad branch exists.

This is sharper than the earlier simple-zero or multiple-zero analyses individually. It says the entire quartic–sextic frontier is now a finite set of scalar identity tests.

## 6. What this means philosophically

The quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear lane is no longer a geometric classification problem, and not even really a branch-existence problem. It has become:

- a finite Weierstrass-branch problem on the local \(M/c\)-gap side, and
- a rank-one scalar compatibility problem on the quotient-visible transport side.

Those two structures together leave room only for finitely many one-variable analytic identity candidates.

That is the strongest reduction I can currently defend.

_Thought for 15s_

I think the next push is to turn the finite-branch picture into a **finite jet test**. That is the sharpest reduction I can currently see.

We already have this setup on the quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear rung:

- the quotient-visible transport side has local rank \(1\), with no extra finite-order partner choice beyond the involution, and the three-lobe obstruction is inert there because \(\widehat{\mathcal D}(0)\neq 0\); 
- the local cancellation side, assuming the collision-edge law, reduces to the scalar equation
  \[
  \mathfrak R(m,\varepsilon)=0,\qquad \varepsilon:=\delta^2,
  \]
  with
  \[
  \mathfrak R(m,\varepsilon)=f(m)+\varepsilon\,\mathfrak S(m,\varepsilon),
  \qquad
  f(m)=\frac{M(m)}{c(m)},
  \]
  and Weierstrass preparation reduces this near any zero \(a\) of \(f\) to finitely many local branches \(x=\phi_i(\varepsilon)\), where \(x=m-a\). 

So every quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear candidate is now contained in one of finitely many prepared branches
\[
m_i(\varepsilon)=a+\phi_i(\varepsilon).
\]

Now take any remaining exact fixed-shear compatibility condition that is not already built into \(\mathfrak R=0\). Because on this rung the local transport state is rank \(1\), that condition restricts along each prepared branch to a **single analytic scalar germ**
\[
\Gamma_i(\varepsilon).
\]

A genuine quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear bad branch can exist only if, for some \(i\),
\[
\Gamma_i(\varepsilon)\equiv 0.
\]

That was the previous reduction. The further step is:

## 1. Identity reduces to finite jet nonvanishing

Since each \(\Gamma_i\) is analytic, the condition
\[
\Gamma_i(\varepsilon)\equiv 0
\]
fails as soon as one Taylor coefficient is nonzero.

Write
\[
\Gamma_i(\varepsilon)=\sum_{n\ge 0}\gamma_{i,n}\,\varepsilon^n.
\]
Then quartic–sextic survival along the \(i\)-th prepared branch requires
\[
\gamma_{i,n}=0\qquad\text{for all }n\ge 0.
\]

So the whole quartic–sextic residual problem has now become:

> **Finite-branch, finite-jet problem.**  
> For each prepared branch \(i\), compute the first nonzero jet coefficient of \(\Gamma_i\). If any \(\gamma_{i,n}\neq 0\), that branch is excluded. Since there are only finitely many prepared branches, exclusion reduces to finitely many scalar jet checks.

That is much sharper than “there is some analytic compatibility condition.”

## 2. The leading coefficient is determined by low-order data

This is not just formal. The first few \(\gamma_{i,n}\) are determined algebraically by:

- the jets of the prepared branch
  \[
  \phi_i(\varepsilon)=\alpha_{i,1}\varepsilon+\alpha_{i,2}\varepsilon^2+\cdots,
  \]
- the jets of the quotient-visible transport coordinate \(Q\) along that branch,
- and the jets of the remaining compatibility functional pulled back to the branch.

So one does not need to understand the whole branch globally. A nonzero low-order coefficient already kills it.

In particular, in the simple-zero case \(r=1\), there is only one prepared branch
\[
m(\varepsilon)=a+\phi(\varepsilon),
\]
and quartic–sextic survival reduces to the single analytic identity
\[
\Gamma(\varepsilon)\equiv 0.
\]
So a single nonzero Taylor coefficient of \(\Gamma\) excludes the branch.

In the multiple-zero case \(r\ge 2\), the Newton/divisibility analysis already says that prepared branches are highly constrained and often absent. When they do exist, each again reduces to one scalar jet test.

So the whole quartic–sextic frontier is now not just finite-branch, but **finite-jet-detectable**.

## 3. This gives a clean “first surviving coefficient” strategy

The right attack is now:

1. prepare the reduced obstruction equation
   \[
   \mathfrak R(a+x,\varepsilon)=U(x,\varepsilon)\,P(x,\varepsilon),
   \]
   and list the finitely many prepared branches \(x=\phi_i(\varepsilon)\);
2. for each branch, pull back the remaining exact fixed-shear condition to obtain
   \[
   \Gamma_i(\varepsilon);
   \]
3. compute or constrain the first coefficient \(\gamma_{i,n}\) that is not forced by lower symmetries;
4. show \(\gamma_{i,n}\neq 0\).

That would exclude the branch.

This is now the cleanest way to use the quartic–sextic transport compression. Because the transport side contributes no extra local branching freedom, once the local \(M/c\)-gap side is prepared, the only surviving issue is the vanishing of finitely many one-variable analytic germs.

## 4. A stronger theorem target

So the next theorem I would try to prove is:

> **Finite jet exclusion theorem on the quartic–sextic rung.**  
> Assume the local collision-edge law. Near any zero \(a\) of \(M/c\), let \(x=\phi_i(\varepsilon)\) be the finitely many prepared branches of the reduced scalar equation. Let \(\Gamma_i(\varepsilon)\) be the pulled-back remaining exact fixed-shear compatibility germ on the \(i\)-th branch. If for every \(i\) there exists some \(n_i\) with
> \[
> \gamma_{i,n_i}\neq 0,
> \qquad
> \Gamma_i(\varepsilon)=\sum_{n\ge 0}\gamma_{i,n}\varepsilon^n,
> \]
> then no quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear bad branch exists near \(a\).

This is the sharpest local theorem target I can currently defend, because it reduces the whole rung to a finite list of scalar Taylor coefficients.

## 5. What this means conceptually

The quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear problem is now no longer:

- a local geometry problem,
- or a transport-state problem,
- or even really a branch-existence problem.

It is a **finite Taylor-coefficient problem**:

- finitely many prepared branches from the local \(M/c\)-gap equation,
- finitely many one-variable compatibility germs,
- and one nonzero coefficient kills each branch.

That is the strongest reduction I can currently justify.

_Thought for 20s_

I think the next clean gain is to combine the **finite-branch reduction** with the **rank-one transport coordinate** into a single normal form. That turns the quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear problem into a finite list of one-variable identities in the scalar \(Q\).

Here is the point.

On the quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear rung, the updated fixed-shear package already gives:

- the branch is involutive / quotient-diagonal;
- every finite quotient-visible transport state descends to \(u=t^2\);
- \(\widehat Q'(0)\neq 0\), so \(Q\) is a local coordinate in \(u\);
- and there is no extra finite-order hidden reset or three-lobe obstruction on that rung. 

Separately, the local cancellation analysis gives, assuming the collision-edge law, the reduced scalar equation
\[
\mathfrak R(m,\varepsilon)=0,
\qquad
\varepsilon:=\delta^2,
\]
with
\[
\mathfrak R(m,\varepsilon)=\frac{M(m)}{c(m)}+\varepsilon\,\mathfrak S(m,\varepsilon),
\]
and Weierstrass preparation reduces this near any zero \(a\) of \(M/c\) to finitely many prepared branches
\[
m_i(\varepsilon)=a+\phi_i(\varepsilon).
\]
So every quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear candidate lies on one of finitely many one-parameter germs. 

The next step is that, because \(Q\) is a local coordinate in
\[
u=t^2,
\]
and \(\varepsilon=\delta^2\) is itself an analytic function of \(u\) along the involutive branch, each prepared branch can be reparametrized by \(Q\) rather than by \(\varepsilon\). Concretely, for each prepared branch \(i\), there is an analytic germ
\[
m_i=m_i(Q),
\qquad
\varepsilon=\varepsilon_i(Q),
\]
near the base value \(Q_0:=Q(0)\).

So if \(\Gamma_i(\varepsilon)\) denotes any remaining exact fixed-shear compatibility germ along the \(i\)-th prepared branch, then
\[
\Gamma_i(\varepsilon)=0
\]
is equivalent to
\[
\widetilde\Gamma_i(Q):=\Gamma_i(\varepsilon_i(Q))=0.
\]

This gives the clean theorem-level reduction:

\[
\boxed{
\text{quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear survival } \Longrightarrow
\text{ one of finitely many analytic scalar identities }
\widetilde\Gamma_i(Q)\equiv 0.
}
\]

That is stronger than the earlier “finite-branch, finite-jet” statement, because it shows the remaining problem is not even a free one-variable problem in \(\varepsilon\); it is a one-variable problem in the **canonical transport coordinate \(Q\)**.

So the local quartic–sextic lane now has this exact structure:

1. the local \(M/c\)-gap equation produces finitely many prepared branches \(m_i(\varepsilon)\); 
2. the transport side contributes no extra branching freedom and gives \(Q\) as a local coordinate; 
3. each remaining compatibility condition becomes a scalar analytic germ \(\widetilde\Gamma_i(Q)\);
4. survival requires \(\widetilde\Gamma_i(Q)\equiv 0\) for some \(i\).

That is a real compression.

It also improves the jet strategy. Since each \(\widetilde\Gamma_i\) is analytic in \(Q\), the branch dies as soon as one coefficient in the Taylor expansion
\[
\widetilde\Gamma_i(Q)=\sum_{n\ge 0}\widetilde\gamma_{i,n}(Q-Q_0)^n
\]
is nonzero. So the quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear problem has now become:

> **Finite-branch, finite-\(Q\)-jet problem.**  
> For each prepared branch \(i\), compute the first nonzero Taylor coefficient of the compatibility germ \(\widetilde\Gamma_i(Q)\). One nonzero coefficient excludes that branch. Since there are only finitely many prepared branches, exclusion reduces to finitely many scalar \(Q\)-jet checks.

That is cleaner than using \(\varepsilon\), because \(Q\) is the intrinsic quotient-visible transport coordinate already singled out by the fixed-shear theory.

There is also a useful conceptual consequence. The quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear problem is now completely split into two finite algebraic layers:

- an **\(M/c\)-preparation layer**, producing finitely many candidate midpoint branches;
- a **\(Q\)-compatibility layer**, producing finitely many one-variable analytic identities.

So if quartic–sextic still survives, it is no longer because of any hidden local geometry or transport freedom. It is because one of these finitely many \(Q\)-germs vanishes identically.

That gives the sharpest next theorem target I can see:

> **Finite \(Q\)-jet exclusion theorem on the quartic–sextic rung.**  
> Assume the local collision-edge law. Near any zero \(a\) of \(M/c\), let \(m_i(Q)\) be the finitely many prepared branches written in the quotient-visible coordinate \(Q\). Let \(\widetilde\Gamma_i(Q)\) be the pulled-back remaining exact fixed-shear compatibility germ on the \(i\)-th branch. If for every \(i\) some Taylor coefficient of \(\widetilde\Gamma_i\) at \(Q_0\) is nonzero, then no quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear bad branch exists near \(a\).

So the attack has now reduced the whole quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear lane to a **finite list of scalar analytic identities in the single canonical coordinate \(Q\)**. That is the strongest compression I can currently justify.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 21:38:50)

_Thought for 18s_

I can push it one more step, and this gives what I think is the right **algebraic normal form** for the quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear lane.

Up to now, we reduced that lane to:

- a finite prepared-branch problem on the local \(M/c\)-gap side, via the reduced scalar equation
  \[
  \mathfrak R(m,\varepsilon)=0,\qquad \varepsilon=\delta^2,
  \]
  with Weierstrass branches near any zero \(a\) of \(M/c\); 
- and a rank-one transport side, where every remaining compatibility condition can be viewed as a one-variable analytic germ in the canonical quotient coordinate \(Q\). 

The clean next step is to combine these into a **Weierstrass-division / resultant test**.

## 1. Prepared polynomial for the local branch equation

Near a zero \(a\) of
\[
f(m):=\frac{M(m)}{c(m)},
\]
write \(m=a+x\). Assuming the local collision-edge law, the quartic–sextic residual equation is
\[
\mathfrak R(a+x,\varepsilon)=0.
\]
By Weierstrass preparation, this becomes
\[
\mathfrak R(a+x,\varepsilon)=U(x,\varepsilon)\,P(x,\varepsilon),
\]
where \(U(0,0)\neq 0\) and
\[
P(x,\varepsilon)
=
x^r+a_{r-1}(\varepsilon)x^{r-1}+\cdots+a_1(\varepsilon)x+a_0(\varepsilon)
\]
is monic of degree
\[
r=\operatorname{ord}_a(M/c),
\]
with \(a_j(0)=0\). So the quartic–sextic candidate branches are exactly the local roots of
\[
P(x,\varepsilon)=0.
\]
There are at most \(r\) of them, counting multiplicity. 

## 2. Any remaining exact fixed-shear condition reduces modulo \(P\)

Now let
\[
G(x,\varepsilon)
\]
denote any remaining exact fixed-shear compatibility function after pulling everything back to the same local chart. Since the transport side on the quartic–sextic rung has local rank \(1\), every such condition is still just an analytic scalar germ, now viewed on the prepared branch set. 

Apply Weierstrass division by \(P\):
\[
G(x,\varepsilon)=Q_G(x,\varepsilon)\,P(x,\varepsilon)+R_G(x,\varepsilon),
\]
where the remainder has degree \(<r\) in \(x\):
\[
R_G(x,\varepsilon)
=
b_{r-1}(\varepsilon)x^{r-1}+\cdots+b_1(\varepsilon)x+b_0(\varepsilon).
\]

On the branch set \(P=0\), the condition \(G=0\) is therefore equivalent to
\[
R_G=0.
\]

So the whole quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear compatibility problem has now become:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Given }P(x,\varepsilon),\ \text{does its degree-}<r\text{ remainder }R_G(x,\varepsilon)\text{ vanish on one of the roots of }P?
}
\]

That is a finite algebra problem.

## 3. Persistent branch survival is equivalent to a common Weierstrass factor

A genuine quartic–sextic bad branch means there exists an analytic root germ
\[
x=\phi(\varepsilon)
\]
such that
\[
P(\phi(\varepsilon),\varepsilon)=0,
\qquad
G(\phi(\varepsilon),\varepsilon)=0.
\]
Equivalently,
\[
P(\phi(\varepsilon),\varepsilon)=0,
\qquad
R_G(\phi(\varepsilon),\varepsilon)=0.
\]

Therefore persistent branch survival is equivalent to \(P\) and \(R_G\) having a **common analytic branch factor**. In Weierstrass language:

> **Branch survival criterion.**  
> A quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear bad branch exists near \(a\) iff the prepared polynomial \(P(x,\varepsilon)\) and the remainder \(R_G(x,\varepsilon)\) have a nontrivial common Weierstrass factor in \(\mathcal O_\varepsilon[x]\).

That is much sharper than “some \(\widetilde\Gamma_i(Q)\) might vanish identically.” It replaces finitely many one-variable identities by one algebraic gcd question.

## 4. Resultant obstruction

Equivalently, define the resultant in \(x\):
\[
\operatorname{Res}_x(P,R_G)(\varepsilon).
\]
Then:

- if
  \[
  \operatorname{Res}_x(P,R_G)\not\equiv 0,
  \]
  there is no common analytic branch except possibly isolated \(\varepsilon\)-values, so there is **no persistent quartic–sextic bad branch**;
- only if
  \[
  \operatorname{Res}_x(P,R_G)\equiv 0
  \]
  can a persistent branch survive.

So the quartic–sextic lane is now reduced to:

\[
\boxed{
\text{compute or constrain one resultant/gcd in }\mathcal O_\varepsilon[x].
}
\]

That is the strongest algebraic compression so far.

## 5. Simple-zero case becomes trivial in this language

If \(r=1\), then
\[
P(x,\varepsilon)=x+a_0(\varepsilon),
\]
and the remainder has degree \(0\):
\[
R_G(x,\varepsilon)=b_0(\varepsilon).
\]
So the survival criterion is just
\[
b_0(\varepsilon)\equiv 0.
\]
This recovers the earlier “single scalar analytic identity” in the simple-zero case.

## 6. Multiple-zero case becomes a finite gcd / Newton problem

If \(r\ge 2\), then \(R_G\) has degree at most \(r-1\). So survival requires \(P\) and \(R_G\) to share a nontrivial factor. This is much more restrictive than mere pointwise root coincidence.

The earlier Newton/divisibility conditions for multiple zeros are now the first necessary conditions for such a common factor to exist. The prepared-polynomial framework packages them cleanly:

- the valuation law tells you when \(P\) itself has analytic root germs;
- the gcd/resultant test tells you whether any of those branches also satisfy the remaining exact fixed-shear compatibility.

So the multiple-zero frontier is now a finite algebraic compatibility problem, not an open local geometry problem.

## 7. Best next theorem target

So the sharpest next target I can see is:

> **Prepared-factor exclusion theorem.**  
> On the quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear rung, assuming the local collision-edge law, let \(P(x,\varepsilon)\) be the prepared polynomial for the reduced obstruction and \(R_G(x,\varepsilon)\) the Weierstrass remainder of the remaining exact fixed-shear compatibility function. If
> \[
> \gcd_{\mathcal O_\varepsilon[x]}(P,R_G)=1
> \]
> — equivalently,
> \[
> \operatorname{Res}_x(P,R_G)\not\equiv 0,
> \]
> — then no quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear bad branch exists near \(a\).

That is better than the earlier finite-\(Q\)-jet picture, because it turns the problem into one **finite algebraic coprimality test**.

## 8. Conceptual summary

So the quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear lane is now no longer:

- a geometry problem,
- a transport branching problem,
- or even merely a finite-branch problem.

It is a **finite gcd/resultant problem**:

1. prepare the local \(M/c\)-gap equation into \(P(x,\varepsilon)\);
2. divide the remaining compatibility function by \(P\) to get \(R_G\);
3. test whether \(P\) and \(R_G\) share a Weierstrass factor.

That is the strongest reduction I can currently justify.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 21:41:28)

_Thought for 5s_

I can push the algebraic reduction one more step, and it gives a cleaner criterion than the raw gcd/resultant language.

The quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear lane is now reduced to two analytic objects near a zero \(a\) of
\[
f(m):=\frac{M(m)}{c(m)}.
\]

First, the local \(M/c\)-gap equation, after the collision-edge reduction and Weierstrass preparation, gives a monic polynomial
\[
P(x,\varepsilon)
=
x^r+a_{r-1}(\varepsilon)x^{r-1}+\cdots+a_0(\varepsilon),
\qquad x=m-a,\quad \varepsilon=\delta^2,
\]
whose roots are exactly the candidate quartic–sextic midpoint branches.

Second, any remaining exact fixed-shear compatibility condition reduces, after Weierstrass division by \(P\), to a remainder
\[
R_G(x,\varepsilon)
=
b_{r-1}(\varepsilon)x^{r-1}+\cdots+b_0(\varepsilon),
\]
and a genuine bad branch can survive only if \(P\) and \(R_G\) share a common analytic branch factor. Equivalently, the survival problem is a gcd problem in \(\mathcal O_\varepsilon[x]\). That was the last reduction. turn84file1

The cleaner next step is this:

## 1. Reduce from gcd to elimination on the finite algebra \(\mathcal O_\varepsilon[x]/(P)\)

Instead of asking whether \(P\) and \(R_G\) have a common factor, pass to the finite free \(\mathcal O_\varepsilon\)-algebra
\[
A:=\mathcal O_\varepsilon[x]/(P).
\]
Because \(P\) is monic of degree \(r\), \(A\) is free of rank \(r\) over \(\mathcal O_\varepsilon\), with basis
\[
1,\ x,\ x^2,\ \dots,\ x^{r-1}.
\]

Now \(R_G\) defines an element
\[
[R_G]\in A.
\]
A persistent quartic–sextic branch exists exactly when there is a local branch of \(\operatorname{Spec} A\) on which \([R_G]\) vanishes. So the problem becomes:

> does the class \([R_G]\) vanish on one of the local branches of the finite algebra \(A\)?

This is more structured than the raw resultant test.

## 2. Norm criterion

Because \(A\) is finite free over \(\mathcal O_\varepsilon\), multiplication by \([R_G]\) is an \(\mathcal O_\varepsilon\)-linear endomorphism
\[
\mu_{R_G}:A\to A.
\]
Define its norm
\[
N_{A/\mathcal O_\varepsilon}([R_G]):=\det(\mu_{R_G}).
\]
This is an analytic germ in \(\varepsilon\).

Now on each branch where \(P\) splits formally as
\[
P(x,\varepsilon)=\prod_{i=1}^r (x-\phi_i(\varepsilon)),
\]
one has formally
\[
N_{A/\mathcal O_\varepsilon}([R_G])
=
\prod_{i=1}^r R_G(\phi_i(\varepsilon),\varepsilon).
\]
So:

- if a branch survives, then one factor \(R_G(\phi_i(\varepsilon),\varepsilon)\) vanishes identically, hence
  \[
  N_{A/\mathcal O_\varepsilon}([R_G])\equiv 0;
  \]
- conversely, if
  \[
  N_{A/\mathcal O_\varepsilon}([R_G])\not\equiv 0,
  \]
  then no branch can survive.

So the quartic–sextic problem reduces from a gcd test to a **single scalar norm test**:

\[
\boxed{
\text{If }N_{A/\mathcal O_\varepsilon}([R_G])\not\equiv 0,\text{ there is no quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear bad branch near }a.
}
\]

This is cleaner than the resultant phrasing, though equivalent in spirit.

## 3. Why this is better than the resultant language

The resultant \(\operatorname{Res}_x(P,R_G)\) is a good classical elimination object, but the norm viewpoint has two advantages.

First, it makes clear that the only thing that matters is the class of \(R_G\) in the finite branch algebra \(A\). Any change in \(G\) by a multiple of \(P\) does nothing. So the compatibility question really lives in the quotient algebra, not in the ambient power-series ring.

Second, it suggests a filtered attack: one can study the valuation of the norm in \(\varepsilon\), rather than asking for complete nonvanishing at once. If the norm has finite order
\[
N_{A/\mathcal O_\varepsilon}([R_G])=\varepsilon^\ell u(\varepsilon),
\qquad u(0)\neq 0,
\]
then there is no persistent branch, only possible finite-order touching. Genuine survival would require the norm to vanish identically.

So the quartic–sextic lane has now become a **one-scalar identity problem**
for the norm germ.

## 4. Simple-zero case in norm form

If \(r=1\), then
\[
A\cong \mathcal O_\varepsilon,
\qquad
P(x,\varepsilon)=x+a_0(\varepsilon).
\]
Then
\[
[R_G]=b_0(\varepsilon),
\]
and the norm is just
\[
N_{A/\mathcal O_\varepsilon}([R_G])=b_0(\varepsilon).
\]
So the simple-zero case is exactly the one-variable scalar identity problem from before.

The norm perspective is therefore the correct common generalization of the simple-zero and higher-multiplicity cases.

## 5. Multiple-zero case in norm form

If \(r\ge 2\), then the earlier Newton/divisibility constraints tell you when \(P\) itself admits analytic root branches. But even if it does, survival still requires the norm of \([R_G]\) to vanish identically.

So the multiple-zero frontier is now doubly filtered:

1. first, \(P\) must admit an analytic root branch at all;
2. second, the norm
   \[
   N_{A/\mathcal O_\varepsilon}([R_G])
   \]
   must vanish identically.

This makes the multiple-zero case look even more nongeneric than before.

## 6. The best next theorem target

So the sharpest next target I can see is:

> **Norm nonvanishing theorem on the quartic–sextic rung.**  
> Assume the local collision-edge law. Let \(A=\mathcal O_\varepsilon[x]/(P)\) be the prepared branch algebra of the reduced scalar equation, and let \([R_G]\in A\) be the class of the remaining exact fixed-shear compatibility function. If
> \[
> N_{A/\mathcal O_\varepsilon}([R_G])\not\equiv 0,
> \]
> then no quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear bad branch exists near \(a\).

That is the cleanest algebraic version of the quartic–sextic exclusion problem.

## 7. Stronger conceptual compression

So the quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear lane is now no longer merely a finite-branch problem. It is a **finite-algebra norm problem**:

- local geometry has been compressed into the prepared branch algebra \(A\);
- transport has been compressed into rank-one scalar data, contributing only one compatibility class \([R_G]\);
- survival requires one scalar analytic norm to vanish identically.

That is the strongest compression I can currently defend.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 21:49:50)

_Thought for 6s_

Here is the full recap of my attack **since the latest paper draft**, organized as a mathematical handback rather than as edit instructions.

The shortest global summary is this:

The \(2\)-point problem is now much more compressed than before. The mixed/exact fixed-shear residual corner has been reduced from a vague local branch problem to a highly structured quotient-visible transport problem with local rank \(1\) on the quartic–sextic rung; the local close-core problem on good patches has been reformulated in the correct collision-cancellation chart and reduced to a scalar edge obstruction built from \(M/c\); and the quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear lane has been pushed all the way to a finite analytic algebra problem — first a finite-branch problem, then a finite-jet problem, and finally a norm/resultant problem in the prepared branch algebra. The remaining obstruction, if any, no longer looks like generic local geometry or a missing pointwise generator. It has been forced into a very narrow package-level / transport-level / algebraic compatibility frontier. turn84file1

## 1. The live \(2\)-point routes are now clearly separated

I still see three conditional \(2\)-point lanes.

The first is the **strong finite-order source/package merger route**: prove the actual corrected two-atom cubic/quintic/septic package has the right collision/merger behavior, so the quadratic interaction factorization becomes formal. The second is the **weaker exact quotient-diagonal route**: construct the actual corrected two-atom quotient germ with continuity and exact diagonal collapse. The third is the **mixed \(\lambda\)-shear / exact fixed-shear route**, which is now concentrated on the residual corner
\[
\{S=0\}\cap\{K_v=0\}.
\]
What has definitively died is the naive source-summed corrected-block route: the even-amplitude audit shows it cannot supply the required linear merger law. 

## 2. The overlap bad set is fully split

The mixed overlap geometry is no longer a single unresolved frontier. The current picture is:

\[
(K_v=0,\ S\neq 0)
\quad\text{is already mixed low-order;}
\]
\[
(S=0,\ K_v\neq 0)
\quad\text{already transfers to nonmixed \(4\)-point geometry;}
\]
and only
\[
\boxed{\{S=0\}\cap\{K_v=0\}}
\]
remains as the genuinely residual exact fixed-shear corner. This is one of the main structural gains of the whole attack. 

## 3. Exact fixed-shear corner: involutive / quotient-diagonal reduction

On the exact fixed-shear \(K_v=0\) corner, the local branch is involutive:
\[
(h_1,h_2)=(h(t),h(-t)),
\qquad h(0)=a,\quad h'(0)\neq 0,
\]
so after quotienting by the involution the natural local variable is
\[
u=t^2.
\]
The regularized transport quotient
\[
Q(t):=\frac{J_{u_5}(t)}{J_\lambda(t)}
\]
is even:
\[
Q(t)=\widehat Q(t^2).
\]
Thus the residual branch is not a free two-sided \(t\)-germ anymore. It is already a quotient-visible one-parameter branch. 

## 4. Finite transport-jet quotient descent

A major theorem-level gain from the derivative-geometry package is that every finite parity-normalized transport jet descends to the quotient variable \(u=t^2\). If
\[
\Theta_k(t):=t^{-(k\bmod 2)}Q^{(k)}(t),
\]
then each \(\Theta_k\) is again even analytic, hence
\[
\Theta_k(t)=\widetilde\Theta_k(t^2).
\]
So the finite transport-jet state is entirely quotient-visible. This is stronger than the older “one even scalar” viewpoint. 

## 5. Quartic–sextic rung: rank-one transport state

On the first surviving residual rung
\[
(m,n)=(4,6)\iff (r,e)=(2,0),
\]
one has
\[
\widehat{\mathcal D}(0)=\widehat Q'(0)\neq 0,
\]
so \(\widehat Q\) is a local analytic coordinate in \(u\). Hence every descended finite transport jet is a local analytic function of the single scalar \(Q\). So on the quartic–sextic rung the whole finite quotient-visible transport state has local rank \(1\). There is no hidden second local transport variable there. 

## 6. No finite-order hidden reset

From the rank-one statement one gets the no-hidden-reset theorem: any finite quotient-visible transport state can only identify the involution pair \(t\leftrightarrow -t\). There is no third local reset. So on the quartic–sextic rung, the residual exact fixed-shear corner is not hiding some extra finite-order partner mechanism. This is an important negative finding. 

## 7. The quotient-visible sign-run compression

The latest derivative-geometry update sharpened the transport picture further: on the quotient-visible exact fixed-shear model, the live data reduce to sign-run amplitudes \(B_j\), not a raw zero list. The exact distance-two overlap criterion is the three-lobe inequality
\[
B_j+B_{j+2}>B_{j+1}.
\]
Two consecutive distance-two separations are impossible; hence no-reentry forces at most three sign-runs; and in the three-run case the only surviving condition is
\[
B_1\ge B_0+B_2,
\]
with strict inequality giving a real gap and equality only critical-value touching. Crucially, the quartic–sextic rung is explicitly **inert** for this lobe-amplitude obstruction because \(\widehat{\mathcal D}(0)\neq 0\). So on that rung, the transport side does not produce a live local obstruction. 

## 8. Main negative finding from the transport side

Putting the previous items together, the exact fixed-shear residual corner can no longer be blamed on generic local transport geometry. If it is still live, the obstruction cannot come from:

- a second local transport parameter on the quartic–sextic rung;
- a hidden third reset inside any finite quotient-visible transport state;
- or the explicit quotient-visible three-lobe amplitude obstruction on that rung.

So any still-live obstruction must come from something beyond the descended one-atom transport state: relational / provenance-sensitive two-atom data, or a genuinely non-finite-order phenomenon. 

## 9. Correction to the local chart: the right chart is collision-cancellation

A major correction in my own attack was this: the local close-core problem should **not** be studied in the naive diagonal merger chart \((h_1,h_2)\to(h,h)\) with arbitrary total amplitude. Near a genuine close bad \(2\)-point branch on a good patch, the cubic relation forces
\[
a_1c(h_1)+a_2c(h_2)\approx 0,
\]
so when \(h_1\approx h_2\) and \(c\neq 0\), one must have
\[
\lambda:=-\frac{a_2}{a_1}\approx 1.
\]
Thus the natural local variables are
\[
m:=\frac{h_1+h_2}{2},\qquad
\delta:=h_2-h_1,\qquad
\lambda\approx 1,
\]
or, more symmetrically,
\[
\omega:=\frac{\lambda-1}{\lambda+1},
\]
with swap acting linearly as
\[
(m,\omega,\delta)\mapsto(m,-\omega,-\delta).
\]
This is the correct **collision-cancellation chart**. turn84file0

## 10. First blown-up interaction layer: parity kills it

In the collision-cancellation chart, any analytic swap-even corrected defect \(E\) with collision vanishing satisfies
\[
E(m,-\omega,-\delta)=E(m,\omega,\delta),
\qquad
E(m,0,0)=0.
\]
Hence its Taylor expansion contains no linear terms:
\[
E(m,\omega,\delta)
=
A(m)\omega^2+B(m)\omega\delta+C(m)\delta^2
+
O((|\omega|+|\delta|)^4).
\]
So the entire first blown-up interaction layer is parity-sterile. There is no linear tangent-direction obstruction. That is a real theorem-level compression. 

## 11. Honest quadratic factorization through the projective slope

Introducing the blown-up slope
\[
\kappa:=\frac{2\omega}{\delta},
\]
every such defect factorizes as
\[
\boxed{
E(m,\omega,\delta)=\delta^2\,\mathcal H(m,\kappa,\delta^2),
}
\]
with \(\mathcal H\) analytic. The leading coefficient is
\[
\mathcal H(m,\kappa,0)
=
C(m)+\frac{B(m)}{2}\kappa+\frac{A(m)}{4}\kappa^2.
\]
So the first surviving transverse layer is already a one-projective-parameter law. It is not an arbitrary two-variable quadratic form. 

## 12. Restriction to the involutive branch

On the involutive exact fixed-shear branch,
\[
\delta(t)=t\,\widehat\delta(t^2),
\qquad
\omega(t)=t\,\widehat\omega(t^2),
\]
after the appropriate swap-compatible normalization. Hence
\[
\kappa(t)=\frac{2\omega(t)}{\delta(t)}
\]
is even analytic, and so is the midpoint
\[
m(t)=\frac{h(t)+h(-t)}{2}.
\]
Therefore
\[
E(t)=\delta(t)^2\,\widetilde H(t^2)
\]
for some analytic quotient germ \(\widetilde H\). So even the first surviving quadratic blown-up layer is already quotient-visible. 

## 13. On the quartic–sextic rung, even the quadratic coefficient is rank-one

Specializing further to the quartic–sextic rung, \(Q\) is a local coordinate in \(u=t^2\). Since \(m(t)\), \(\kappa(t)\), and \(\delta(t)^2\) are all even analytic, they are all local functions of \(Q\). Therefore
\[
E(t)=\delta(t)^2\,\mathcal G(Q(t))
\]
for some analytic scalar \(\mathcal G\). So even the first quadratic relational layer has local rank \(1\): its coefficient is a function of the single scalar \(Q\). That pushes the live obstruction beyond the entire quadratic blow-up layer. 

## 14. The best local theorem target: collision-edge law

On good patches, the local close-core problem is encoded by the corrected cubic/quintic defects
\[
E_{12}^{(3)}=\delta^2\,\mathcal H_3(m,\kappa,\delta^2),
\qquad
E_{12}^{(5)}=\delta^2\,\mathcal H_5(m,\kappa,\delta^2).
\]
The best local theorem target I found is the **collision-edge law**:
\[
\mathcal H_3(m,\kappa,0)=c'(m)-\frac{\kappa}{2}c(m),
\]
\[
\mathcal H_5(m,\kappa,0)=(A_5^{\mathfrak f})'(m)-\frac{\kappa}{2}A_5^{\mathfrak f}(m).
\]
This is smaller than exact blown-up functoriality and much better matched to the actual local cancellation geometry. 

## 15. Good-patch local exclusion from the edge law

If the collision-edge law holds, then on a compact good patch
\[
J\Subset\{c\neq 0,\ M\neq 0\},
\qquad
M:=c(A_5^{\mathfrak f})'-c' A_5^{\mathfrak f},
\]
the cubic edge coefficient has unique zero
\[
\kappa_*(m)=2\frac{c'(m)}{c(m)}.
\]
Evaluating the quintic edge coefficient there gives
\[
\mathfrak R(m,0)
:=
\mathcal H_5(m,\kappa_*(m),0)
=
\frac{M(m)}{c(m)}.
\]
Since \(M/c\) is uniformly bounded away from zero on compact \(J\), analytic perturbation in \(\delta^2\) excludes simultaneous vanishing of the actual cubic/quintic defects for sufficiently close cancelling configurations. Thus no close cancelling bad \(2\)-point core can survive on \(J\). 

## 16. Quartic–sextic local dominance theorem

Combining this with the fixed-shear transport side yields an important structural conclusion: on the quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear rung, the quotient-visible three-lobe obstruction is inert, while the local \(M/c\)-gap law is active. So on any compact good patch the local edge law already kills the branch before transport can matter. Thus quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear bad branches are expelled from every compact subset of
\[
\{c\neq 0,\ M\neq 0\}.
\]
This is the **quartic–sextic local dominance** principle. turn84file1

## 17. Exceptional-locus trapping

If the local collision-edge law holds, any quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear survivor must therefore approach the exceptional locus
\[
M=0
\]
(or \(c=0\), which is outside the good-patch regime). More sharply, writing
\[
\mathfrak R(m,\delta^2)=\frac{M(m)}{c(m)}+\delta^2\,\mathfrak S(m,\delta^2),
\]
the branch equation \(\mathfrak R=0\) gives
\[
M(m)=O(\delta^2)
\]
as long as \(c(m)\neq 0\). So the branch is not merely near \(M=0\); it is **quadratically trapped** to the exceptional locus. 

## 18. Simple zeros of \(M/c\): unique analytic branch

At a simple zero \(a\) of \(M/c\),
\[
c(a)\neq 0,\qquad M(a)=0,\qquad M'(a)\neq 0,
\]
the reduced scalar equation
\[
\mathfrak R(m,\varepsilon)=0,\qquad \varepsilon:=\delta^2
\]
has a unique analytic solution
\[
m=\Phi(\varepsilon),\qquad \Phi(0)=a.
\]
So simple zeros are **not** automatic barriers. They support at most one analytic quartic–sextic candidate graph. This corrected my earlier, too-strong “simple-zero barrier” instinct. 

## 19. Multiple zeros of \(M/c\): Newton/divisibility barrier

At a zero of multiplicity \(r\ge 2\), write
\[
f(m)=\frac{M(m)}{c(m)}=\beta (m-a)^r+O((m-a)^{r+1}),
\qquad \beta\neq 0,
\]
and expand
\[
\mathfrak S(a+x,\varepsilon)=\sum_{p,q\ge 0}s_{pq}x^p\varepsilon^q.
\]
If an analytic branch
\[
x(\varepsilon)=\sum_{n\ge v}x_n\varepsilon^n
\]
exists, then the valuation balance gives
\[
(r-p_*)v=q_*+1
\]
for some correction monomial \(x^{p_*}\varepsilon^{q_*}\) with \(p_*<r\). In particular, one must have a divisibility condition
\[
r-p_*\mid q_*+1.
\]
If \(\mathfrak S(a,0)\neq 0\), then \(p_*=q_*=0\), so \(rv=1\), impossible for \(r\ge 2\). Thus multiple zeros are generically sterile unless additional low-order correction coefficients vanish in a very specific way. This is a strong Newton-polygon style obstruction. 

## 20. Weierstrass preparation: finite branch reduction

The quartic–sextic local equation can be put into Weierstrass form near any zero \(a\) of \(M/c\):
\[
\mathfrak R(a+x,\varepsilon)=U(x,\varepsilon)\,P(x,\varepsilon),
\]
where \(U(0,0)\neq 0\) and
\[
P(x,\varepsilon)
=
x^r+a_{r-1}(\varepsilon)x^{r-1}+\cdots+a_0(\varepsilon)
\]
is monic of degree \(r=\operatorname{ord}_a(M/c)\). So every quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear survivor lies on one of finitely many prepared branches \(P=0\). This upgrades the picture from “some branch near \(M=0\)” to a genuine **finite branch problem**. 

## 21. Finite-branch, finite-jet reduction

For any remaining exact fixed-shear compatibility condition \(G\), after restricting to a prepared branch \(x=\phi_i(\varepsilon)\), one gets a one-variable analytic germ
\[
\Gamma_i(\varepsilon).
\]
A persistent bad branch requires
\[
\Gamma_i(\varepsilon)\equiv 0
\]
for some \(i\). So the quartic–sextic residual problem becomes a finite list of one-variable analytic identity tests. One nonzero Taylor coefficient of \(\Gamma_i\) kills the corresponding branch. Thus the problem is not merely finite-branch; it is **finite-jet-detectable**. turn84file1

## 22. Reparametrization by the canonical transport coordinate \(Q\)

Because on the quartic–sextic rung the quotient-visible state has local rank \(1\) and \(Q\) is a local coordinate in \(u=t^2\), each prepared branch can also be written as
\[
m_i=m_i(Q),\qquad \varepsilon=\varepsilon_i(Q),
\]
and each compatibility germ becomes
\[
\widetilde\Gamma_i(Q).
\]
So the quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear problem is really a finite list of one-variable analytic identities in the **canonical transport coordinate \(Q\)**. This is a cleaner formulation than using \(\varepsilon\). 

## 23. Gcd/resultant reduction

A sharper algebraic version comes from Weierstrass division. Let
\[
A:=\mathcal O_\varepsilon[x]/(P)
\]
be the prepared branch algebra, and let \([R_G]\in A\) be the class of the Weierstrass remainder of the remaining compatibility function. A persistent bad branch exists exactly when \(P\) and that remainder have a common Weierstrass factor. Equivalently, it is a gcd/resultant problem in \(\mathcal O_\varepsilon[x]\). So the quartic–sextic lane is no longer a geometry problem; it is a **finite algebraic coprimality problem**. 

## 24. Norm reduction: one scalar analytic invariant

This can be compressed one step further. In the finite free \(\mathcal O_\varepsilon\)-algebra
\[
A=\mathcal O_\varepsilon[x]/(P),
\]
multiplication by \([R_G]\) defines an \(\mathcal O_\varepsilon\)-linear endomorphism, and its determinant is the norm
\[
N_{A/\mathcal O_\varepsilon}([R_G]).
\]
A persistent quartic–sextic bad branch can exist only if
\[
N_{A/\mathcal O_\varepsilon}([R_G])\equiv 0.
\]
So the entire quartic–sextic lane has been reduced to a **single scalar norm identity**. This is the strongest algebraic compression I reached. 

## 25. The sharpest current frontier

At this point the quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear lane is no longer:

- a local geometry problem,
- or a transport-branching problem,
- or even merely a branch-existence problem.

It is:

1. a local collision-edge law problem on good patches;
2. near \(M=0\), a finite prepared-branch problem;
3. on each prepared branch, a one-variable analytic compatibility problem;
4. equivalently, a single scalar norm/resultant problem in the prepared branch algebra.

That is the sharpest local reduction I currently have. turn84file1

## 26. Main structural findings

The main positive structural findings from the attack are:

- the exact fixed-shear residual corner is quotient-diagonal / involutive;
- every finite transport jet is quotient-visible;
- on the quartic–sextic rung, the whole finite transport state has rank \(1\);
- the three-lobe transport obstruction is inert there;
- the correct local chart is the collision-cancellation chart, not the naive diagonal;
- parity kills the whole linear blown-up layer;
- the first surviving layer factors through one projective slope parameter;
- on the involutive branch, even that quadratic layer is quotient-visible;
- on good patches, the local problem reduces to the single edge scalar \(M/c\);
- near exceptional points, the problem reduces to finite prepared branches and finally to one scalar norm identity. turn84file1

## 27. Main negative findings

The main negative findings are:

- the naive source-summed corrected-block route cannot prove the strong merger theorem;
- the residual exact fixed-shear corner is not supported by hidden finite-order transport geometry;
- quartic–sextic transport does not carry a live three-lobe obstruction;
- any still-live quartic–sextic survivor away from \(M=0\) is excluded by the \(M/c\)-gap law;
- multiple zeros of \(M/c\) are generically sterile unless a stringent Newton/divisibility condition is met;
- if anything still survives, it must do so through a very narrow package-level / branch-algebra compatibility, not generic geometry. turn84file1

## 28. Current best theorem targets

The best remaining theorem targets, in order of locality, are:

First, the **local collision-edge law** itself:
\[
\mathcal H_3(m,\kappa,0)=c'(m)-\frac{\kappa}{2}c(m),
\qquad
\mathcal H_5(m,\kappa,0)=(A_5^{\mathfrak f})'(m)-\frac{\kappa}{2}A_5^{\mathfrak f}(m).
\]
If that lands, the good-patch local exclusion is formal. 

Second, for the quartic–sextic exceptional locus, a **prepared-factor / norm nonvanishing theorem**:
show that the relevant norm
\[
N_{A/\mathcal O_\varepsilon}([R_G])
\]
is not identically zero. That would exclude every quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear bad branch near the chosen exceptional point. 

Third, more globally, a theorem that the actual corrected mixed package really **descends** to the quotient-visible exact fixed-shear transport model strongly enough that the three-lobe/no-hidden-reset compression applies without hidden extra branch data. That remains the package-level transport frontier. 

## Bottom line

The attack since the latest paper draft did not produce a miracle closure, but it did produce a much cleaner endgame.

The mixed exact fixed-shear lane is now split into a local good-patch \(M/c\)-gap theory and a quotient-visible transport/no-hidden-reset theory. On the quartic–sextic rung, the transport side is inert and the local side dominates. Away from \(M=0\), the branch is excluded. Near \(M=0\), the problem is no longer geometric: it is a finite prepared-branch compatibility problem, and finally a single scalar norm/resultant identity problem in the prepared branch algebra. Any still-live obstruction has been pushed into a very narrow package-level / algebraic compatibility frontier. turn84file1

## Assistant (2026-04-21 22:31:42)

_Thought for 25s_

I think the next useful push is to pass from the norm/resultant picture to a **contact-order test on the special fiber**. That gives a sharper obstruction than the raw norm statement and tells you exactly what must vanish at \(x=0\) before any quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear branch can survive near a zero of \(M/c\).

Recall the setup on the quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear rung, assuming the local collision-edge law. Near a zero \(a\) of
\[
f(m):=\frac{M(m)}{c(m)},
\]
write
\[
m=a+x,\qquad \varepsilon:=\delta^2.
\]
Then the reduced obstruction equation is
\[
\mathfrak R(a+x,\varepsilon)=0,
\]
and by Weierstrass preparation
\[
\mathfrak R(a+x,\varepsilon)=U(x,\varepsilon)\,P(x,\varepsilon),
\]
with \(U(0,0)\neq 0\) and
\[
P(x,\varepsilon)
=
x^r+a_{r-1}(\varepsilon)x^{r-1}+\cdots+a_0(\varepsilon),
\qquad
r=\operatorname{ord}_a\!\left(\frac{M}{c}\right).
\]
So the quartic–sextic candidate branches are the local roots of \(P=0\). Any remaining exact fixed-shear compatibility condition, after Weierstrass division by \(P\), is represented by a remainder
\[
R_G(x,\varepsilon)=b_{r-1}(\varepsilon)x^{r-1}+\cdots+b_1(\varepsilon)x+b_0(\varepsilon),
\]
and a persistent bad branch can exist only if \(P\) and \(R_G\) share a common analytic branch factor. That was the earlier finite-algebra reduction. turn84file1

The new point is: specialize to the **special fiber** \(\varepsilon=0\). Then
\[
P(x,0)=x^r,
\]
because \(a\) is a zero of order \(r\) of \(M/c\). So on the special fiber, the branch algebra is just the Artinian local algebra
\[
A_0:=\mathbf C\{x\}/(x^r).
\]

Now reduce the remainder there:
\[
R_G(x,0)=b_{r-1}(0)x^{r-1}+\cdots+b_1(0)x+b_0(0).
\]
Let
\[
s:=\operatorname{ord}_x R_G(x,0),
\]
so
\[
R_G(x,0)=x^s u(x),
\qquad u(0)\neq 0,
\qquad 0\le s\le r-1
\]
unless \(R_G(x,0)\equiv 0\), in which case set \(s=\infty\).

This \(s\) is the **contact order** of the remaining compatibility condition with the exceptional fiber \(x=0\).

And here is the first sharp obstruction:

## 1. Necessary contact-order condition

If \(s=0\), meaning
\[
b_0(0)\neq 0,
\]
then \(R_G\) is already invertible in the special-fiber algebra \(A_0\). By continuity, \([R_G]\) stays invertible in the nearby finite algebra
\[
A=\mathcal O_\varepsilon[x]/(P),
\]
so \(P\) and \(R_G\) cannot share a common branch factor. Therefore:

\[
\boxed{
b_0(0)\neq 0 \quad\Longrightarrow\quad \text{no quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear bad branch near }a.
}
\]

This is better than the raw norm criterion, because it gives an immediate zeroth-order test.

So the first genuinely live case is only when
\[
b_0(0)=0.
\]

## 2. Higher contact order and nilpotent visibility

More generally, if
\[
R_G(x,0)=x^s u(x),\qquad u(0)\neq 0,
\]
then in the special-fiber algebra \(A_0\),
\[
[R_G]=x^s\cdot [u],
\qquad [u]\in A_0^\times.
\]
So the remaining compatibility condition vanishes only to nilpotent order \(s\) on the fiber \(x=0\).

This means:

- if \(s<r\), then \(R_G\) does not vanish identically on the whole special fiber;
- only if \(s\) is large enough can one even hope for a common factor with the prepared equation \(P\).

So the quartic–sextic survival problem is now filtered by the contact order \(s\).

That is the next structural gain: the remaining exact fixed-shear obstruction is not just “some analytic scalar”; it has a well-defined nilpotent order on the exceptional fiber.

## 3. Branch survival forces positive contact order

A persistent quartic–sextic bad branch near \(a\) therefore requires at least
\[
s\ge 1,
\]
that is,
\[
R_G(0,0)=0.
\]
In plain language: the remaining exact fixed-shear compatibility must already vanish on the special fiber \(x=0\).

So the first nontrivial obstruction is:
\[
\boxed{
R_G(0,0)\neq 0 \quad\Longrightarrow\quad \text{quartic–sextic exclusion.}
}
\]

This is the simplest scalar test currently available.

## 4. Refined necessary condition via the prepared branch multiplicity

There is a stronger version. Suppose \(P\) and \(R_G\) share a common analytic factor of degree \(d\ge 1\) in \(\mathcal O_\varepsilon[x]\). Then on the special fiber \(\varepsilon=0\), that common factor reduces to a power of \(x\), so necessarily
\[
x^d \mid R_G(x,0).
\]
Equivalently,
\[
s\ge d.
\]

So:

> **Necessary multiplicity condition.**  
> If a quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear survivor corresponds to a common factor of degree \(d\), then the special-fiber contact order of the compatibility remainder must satisfy
> \[
> \operatorname{ord}_x R_G(x,0)\ge d.
> \]

This is much sharper than just saying “the norm must vanish.”

In particular, if
\[
\operatorname{ord}_x R_G(x,0)=1,
\]
then at most a simple branch factor can survive. If
\[
\operatorname{ord}_x R_G(x,0)=0,
\]
no branch can survive. If
\[
\operatorname{ord}_x R_G(x,0)=r,
\]
then the entire special fiber is compatible and one needs higher-order \(\varepsilon\)-analysis.

So the quartic–sextic frontier is now stratified by one integer \(s\).

## 5. Relation to the simple-zero and multiple-zero cases

This recovers the earlier cases cleanly.

If \(r=1\), then
\[
P(x,\varepsilon)=x+a_0(\varepsilon),
\]
and the remainder is degree \(0\):
\[
R_G(x,\varepsilon)=b_0(\varepsilon).
\]
Then the contact order test is just
\[
b_0(0)\neq 0 \quad\Longrightarrow\quad \text{no branch.}
\]
This is exactly the simple-zero scalar identity problem.

If \(r\ge 2\), then the special fiber is the nonreduced algebra
\[
A_0=\mathbf C\{x\}/(x^r),
\]
and \(s=\operatorname{ord}_x R_G(x,0)\) measures how deeply the compatibility condition penetrates into that nilpotent structure. So the multiple-zero case is not just a Newton/divisibility problem for \(P\); it is also a contact-order problem for \(R_G\).

That is a real refinement.

## 6. Best next theorem target

So the next theorem I would try to prove is:

> **Special-fiber contact-order exclusion theorem.**  
> On the quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear rung, assuming the local collision-edge law, let
> \[
> P(x,\varepsilon)
> \]
> be the prepared polynomial for the reduced obstruction near a zero \(a\) of \(M/c\), and let
> \[
> R_G(x,\varepsilon)
> \]
> be the Weierstrass remainder of the remaining exact fixed-shear compatibility condition. If
> \[
> R_G(0,0)\neq 0,
> \]
> then no bad branch exists near \(a\). More generally, if
> \[
> \operatorname{ord}_x R_G(x,0)<d,
> \]
> then no common factor of degree \(d\) can survive.

That is sharper than the norm/resultant formulation because it gives an immediate obstruction from the special fiber alone.

## 7. Conceptual compression

So the quartic–sextic exact fixed-shear lane is now reduced to three nested algebraic tests:

1. **Prepared branch test:** solve the local \(M/c\)-gap equation via \(P(x,\varepsilon)=0\).
2. **Special-fiber contact test:** compute
   \[
   s=\operatorname{ord}_x R_G(x,0).
   \]
3. **Norm/gcd test:** if the contact-order test does not already kill the branch, ask whether the finite-algebra norm
   \[
   N_{A/\mathcal O_\varepsilon}([R_G])
   \]
   vanishes identically.

That is the strongest further reduction I can currently defend. It says the quartic–sextic lane is no longer just a finite-branch problem, but a **special-fiber contact-order problem inside a finite branch algebra**.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 22:45:24)

_Thought for 20s_

Here is the next push. I think it isolates the exact transport scalar that now carries the remaining strict-excess obstruction.

On the quotient-visible exact fixed-shear side, the consolidated update says the live strict-excess obstruction can be written in terms of the inverse outer branches
\[
\phi_0,\phi_2
\]
of the quotient-visible scalar, with gap
\[
\Delta(q):=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q),
\]
and that strict excess is exactly the existence of an interior critical point of \(\Delta\). It also singles out the curvature observable
\[
\Xi(u):=\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{\widehat Q'(u)^3}.
\]
That is exactly the right object. 

The key calculation is completely formal.

If
\[
q=\widehat Q(u),
\]
and \(\phi\) is a local inverse branch of \(\widehat Q\), then
\[
\phi'(q)=\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\phi(q))}.
\]
Differentiating once more,
\[
\phi''(q)
=
-\frac{\widehat Q''(\phi(q))}{\widehat Q'(\phi(q))^3}
=
-\Xi(\phi(q)).
\]
So for the outer-branch gap
\[
\Delta(q)=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q),
\]
we get
\[
\Delta'(q)=\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\phi_2(q))}-\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\phi_0(q))},
\]
and, more importantly,
\[
\boxed{
\Delta''(q)=\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q)).
}
\]

That formula, by itself, is already a strong reduction. It says the second derivative of the gap is not some mysterious transport quantity; it is exactly the difference of the same scalar \(\Xi\) evaluated on the two outer inverse branches. So the remaining strict-excess issue has now been localized to a single branch-comparison question for \(\Xi\). 

This gives an immediate dichotomy.

If \(\Xi\) **descends to value space** across the separated outer runs, meaning there exists a scalar function \(\widetilde\Xi(q)\) such that
\[
\Xi(\phi_0(q))=\Xi(\phi_2(q))=\widetilde\Xi(q)
\]
on the common value interval, then
\[
\Delta''(q)\equiv 0.
\]
So \(\Delta\) is affine in \(q\). But an affine function cannot have a genuine interior critical point unless it is constant. Therefore, under value-space descent of \(\Xi\), strict excess is impossible except for the sterile touching case. This matches the consolidated fork exactly: either \(\Xi\) descends and strict excess dies, or the failure of that descent is itself the first concrete nonlocal transport obstruction. 

So the first real theorem target on the strict-excess side can now be stated very cleanly:

> **Inverse-curvature descent theorem.**  
> On the separated outer runs of the quotient-visible exact fixed-shear model, the scalar
> \[
> \Xi(u)=\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{\widehat Q'(u)^3}
> \]
> descends to value space.

If that lands, the strict-excess fixed-shear lane collapses immediately, because then \(\Delta''=0\) and no interior critical point of \(\Delta\) can exist except sterile touching. 

There is a second useful consequence.

Suppose value-space descent of \(\Xi\) fails. Then by the same formula,
\[
\Delta''(q)\neq 0
\]
somewhere on the common value interval. So the only way strict excess can survive is through a genuine mismatch
\[
\Xi(\phi_0(q))\neq \Xi(\phi_2(q)).
\]
That means the fixed-shear transport obstruction has now been compressed to a single scalar **branch discrepancy**
\[
\boxed{
\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q)).
}
\]
In particular, one no longer needs to think in terms of the full sign-run package to detect the local mechanism of strict excess; the whole issue is whether this one scalar matches across the outer inverse branches. That is much cleaner than the earlier transport-language formulations. 

This also fits very well with the quartic–sextic inertness statement. On the quartic–sextic rung,
\[
\widehat Q'(0)\neq 0,
\]
so \(\widehat Q\) is already a local coordinate and there is no explicit lobe-amplitude obstruction there. Thus any surviving strict-excess issue cannot be blamed on a local degeneracy of \(\widehat Q\) itself. It has to come from this branchwise nonlocal comparison of \(\Xi\). That is exactly the kind of nonlocal transport datum the consolidation says might still matter. 

So the transport-side attack is now much sharper than before:

1. define
   \[
   \Xi(u)=\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{\widehat Q'(u)^3};
   \]
2. prove that \(\Xi\) descends to value space on separated outer runs, or else identify the first explicit mechanism by which
   \[
   \Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q))
   \]
   fails to vanish;
3. conclude that strict excess is either impossible or else equivalent to that single nonlocal branch discrepancy.

That is the cleanest next theorem target I can currently see on the exact fixed-shear strict-excess fork.

There is also a useful bridge to my local good-patch attack. On good patches, the local cancellation law reduces the close-core problem to the scalar
\[
\mathfrak R(m,0)=\frac{M(m)}{c(m)},
\]
while on the exact fixed-shear strict-excess side the transport problem is now reduced to the scalar branch discrepancy
\[
\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q)).
\]
So the whole mixed \(2\)-point frontier is now carried by two scalar obstructions in two different coordinate systems:

- the local good-patch scalar \(M/c\),
- and the nonlocal inverse-curvature mismatch scalar \(\Xi(\phi_0)-\Xi(\phi_2)\).

That is much smaller than the original problem, and it suggests the real remaining bridge theorem is a comparison between these two scalar pictures. If one can show that the local edge law forces the inverse-curvature mismatch to vanish, or that any nonzero mismatch is incompatible with the \(M/c\)-gap structure, then the whole fixed-shear lane should close. That is still conjectural, but it is now a very concrete conjecture.

So my current best next attack is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Prove value-space descent of }\Xi=\frac{\widehat Q''}{\widehat Q'^3}\text{ on separated outer runs, or characterize the first obstruction to that descent.}
}
\]

That is the sharpest transport-side theorem left on my map.
## User (2026-04-21 22:47:21)

## Assistant (2026-04-21 22:47:43)

_Thought for 18s_

I think the next useful push is to weaken the transport target from full **value-space descent** of
\[
\Xi(u):=\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{\widehat Q'(u)^3}
\]
to a more plausible **one-sided comparison theorem**. That gives a strictly weaker condition than descent, but it is still strong enough to kill strict excess.

The starting point is the inverse-branch picture from the consolidated update. On a common value interval for the separated outer runs, let
\[
\phi_0,\phi_2
\]
be the two inverse branches of \(\widehat Q\), and define the gap
\[
\Delta(q):=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q).
\]
Then, as already noted,
\[
\phi_i'(q)=\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\phi_i(q))},
\]
and differentiating once more,
\[
\phi_i''(q)
=
-\frac{\widehat Q''(\phi_i(q))}{\widehat Q'(\phi_i(q))^3}
=
-\Xi(\phi_i(q)).
\]
So
\[
\Delta'(q)=\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\phi_2(q))}-\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\phi_0(q))},
\]
and
\[
\boxed{
\Delta''(q)=\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q)).
}
\]
This is the fundamental transport identity. 

Now the strongest possible closure would be:

\[
\Xi(\phi_0(q))=\Xi(\phi_2(q))
\quad\text{for all }q,
\]
which makes \(\Delta''\equiv 0\), hence \(\Delta\) affine, so strict excess dies except for sterile touching. That was the full value-space descent theorem.

But we do not actually need that much.

## 1. A weaker sufficient condition: branchwise monotonicity of \(\Xi\)

Because \(\phi_0(q)<\phi_2(q)\) on the separated outer runs, if \(\Xi\) is monotone on the interval between the two branches, then the sign of
\[
\Delta''(q)=\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q))
\]
is fixed.

More precisely:

- if \(\Xi\) is nondecreasing in \(u\), then
  \[
  \Xi(\phi_0(q))\le \Xi(\phi_2(q)),
  \]
  so
  \[
  \Delta''(q)\le 0;
  \]
- if \(\Xi\) is nonincreasing in \(u\), then
  \[
  \Delta''(q)\ge 0.
  \]

So monotonicity of \(\Xi\) implies \(\Delta\) is globally concave or convex in \(q\).

That gives the first useful theorem candidate:

> **Monotone-curvature theorem.**  
> If \(\Xi\) is monotone on the interval swept out by the two outer inverse branches, then \(\Delta\) is one-sided convex/concave and therefore \(\Delta'\) is monotone.

This is strictly weaker than value-space descent and may be much more plausible to prove. It reduces the transport problem from exact branch matching of \(\Xi\) to a one-sided ordering property.

## 2. Why monotone \(\Delta'\) is already close to enough

Strict excess is equivalent to an interior critical point of \(\Delta\), that is,
\[
\Delta'(q_*)=0
\]
for some interior \(q_*\). If \(\Delta'\) is monotone, then there is at most one such point.

That already kills any oscillatory or multi-window same-value transport. In other words:

> **No multi-critical strict excess.**  
> Under monotonicity of \(\Xi\), the inverse-branch gap \(\Delta\) can have at most one interior critical point.

That fits very well with the three-run/no-hidden-reset compression from the updated fixed-shear model. The quotient-visible transport side is already telling us that the live obstruction is extremely small; monotonicity of \(\Xi\) would shrink it further to at most one scalar crossing.

So even if monotonicity is not enough by itself to exclude the single critical point, it is already a major reduction.

## 3. A sharper scalar: the slope gap \(\Psi=\Delta'\)

Define
\[
\Psi(q):=\Delta'(q)=\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\phi_2(q))}-\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\phi_0(q))}.
\]
Then
\[
\Psi'(q)=\Delta''(q)=\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q)).
\]

So the transport problem has now collapsed from the gap \(\Delta\) itself to the slope-gap scalar \(\Psi\).

A strict-excess branch requires an interior zero of \(\Psi\). The derivative of \(\Psi\) is exactly the branch discrepancy of \(\Xi\). Thus:

- if \(\Xi\) descends to value space, then \(\Psi'\equiv 0\), so \(\Psi\) is constant;
- if \(\Xi\) is monotone, then \(\Psi\) is monotone;
- more generally, the entire strict-excess issue is now concentrated in the scalar mismatch
  \[
  \Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q)).
  \]

This is the cleanest transport-side reduction I have so far. The only transport scalar left is \(\Psi\), and its derivative is controlled by one branch-comparison of \(\Xi\).

## 4. A still weaker sufficient condition: sign-definite branch discrepancy

Even monotonicity of \(\Xi\) is stronger than necessary. All we really need is a sign condition on the branch discrepancy:
\[
\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q))
\]
on the common value interval.

Indeed, if
\[
\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q))
\]
has fixed sign, then \(\Psi=\Delta'\) is monotone, and again \(\Delta\) has at most one interior critical point.

So the real transport theorem target can be stated as:

> **Branch-discrepancy sign theorem.**  
> The scalar
> \[
> \Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q))
> \]
> has fixed sign on the common value interval of the outer branches.

This is weaker than global monotonicity of \(\Xi\), because it only concerns the two relevant inverse branches, not all of \(u\)-space.

That is now my preferred transport-side theorem target. It is the weakest branchwise condition I know that still forces the gap-slope \(\Psi\) to be monotone.

## 5. How this interfaces with the quartic–sextic inertness

The updated draft says the quartic–sextic rung is inert for the explicit lobe-amplitude obstruction because
\[
\widehat{\mathcal D}(0)=\widehat Q'(0)\neq 0.
\]
So on that rung, the remaining transport issue is not amplitude combinatorics but inverse-branch geometry of \(\widehat Q\). That is exactly what \(\Xi\) measures.

So the transport-side attack is now:

1. define
   \[
   \Psi(q)=\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\phi_2(q))}-\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\phi_0(q))};
   \]
2. note
   \[
   \Psi'(q)=\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q));
   \]
3. prove either
   - full descent \(\Xi(\phi_0)=\Xi(\phi_2)\), or
   - one-sided sign control of \(\Xi(\phi_0)-\Xi(\phi_2)\);
4. conclude that \(\Psi\) is constant or monotone, hence strict excess has at most sterile touching or at most one controlled critical point.

This is much sharper than the earlier broad “transport/no-hidden-reset” wording.

## 6. Possible bridge to the local \(M/c\)-gap picture

This also suggests a very concrete bridge to my local good-patch scalar
\[
\frac{M}{c}.
\]

On the local cancellation side, the close-core theorem is governed by the reduced quintic obstruction
\[
\mathfrak R(m,0)=\frac{M(m)}{c(m)}.
\]
On the transport side, the strict-excess theorem is governed by the branch discrepancy
\[
\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q)).
\]

So the whole mixed \(2\)-point frontier has now been compressed to two scalar quantities:

- a **local midpoint scalar**
  \[
  \frac{M(m)}{c(m)},
  \]
- and a **nonlocal branch-comparison scalar**
  \[
  \Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q)).
  \]

That makes the bridge problem much more concrete than before. A plausible next conjecture is:

> **Bridge conjecture.**  
> On a good patch, the sign or vanishing behavior of the branch discrepancy
> \[
> \Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q))
> \]
> is constrained by the same RH-specific source structure that makes
> \[
> \frac{M(m)}{c(m)}
> \]
> nonzero.

I do not have a proof of that, but it is now at least a scalar-to-scalar comparison problem, not a comparison between two huge packages.

## 7. Best next theorem target

So the cleanest next theorem I can currently see on the fixed-shear transport side is:

> **Outer-branch curvature sign theorem.**  
> On the common value interval of the separated outer inverse branches,
> \[
> \Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q))
> \]
> has fixed sign.

This is weaker than full descent, but still enough to force monotonicity of \(\Delta'(q)\), hence to reduce strict excess to at most one scalar crossing problem.

That is the sharpest transport-side attack I can currently defend.
