## Assistant (2026-04-21 22:54:36)

_Thought for 1m 3s_

I think the next real gain is a **transport-jet hierarchy** for the inverse-branch gap. This sharpens the fixed-shear strict-excess fork a lot.

We already have the basic identity from the inverse outer branches \(\phi_0,\phi_2\) of \(\widehat Q\):
\[
\Delta(q):=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q),
\]
and
\[
\Delta''(q)=\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q)),
\qquad
\Xi(u):=\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{\widehat Q'(u)^3}.
\]
So the first nontrivial transport obstruction is the branch discrepancy of \(\Xi\). 

The useful refinement is to define the intrinsic \(Q\)-transport derivative
\[
\mathcal D_Q := \frac{1}{\widehat Q'(u)}\frac{d}{du},
\]
and then the hierarchy
\[
\Xi_0:=\Xi,\qquad
\Xi_{n+1}:=\mathcal D_Q \Xi_n
\quad(n\ge 0).
\]

Now differentiate the gap identities repeatedly. Since for any analytic scalar \(F(u)\),
\[
\frac{d}{dq}F(\phi_i(q))
=
\frac{F'(\phi_i(q))}{\widehat Q'(\phi_i(q))}
=
(\mathcal D_Q F)(\phi_i(q)),
\]
one gets by induction

\[
\boxed{
\Delta^{(n+2)}(q)=\Xi_n(\phi_0(q))-\Xi_n(\phi_2(q)),
\qquad n\ge 0.
}
\]

That is, to my mind, the cleanest transport-side formula we have yet.

It means the entire strict-excess fixed-shear problem is governed by a **single scalar hierarchy** \((\Xi_n)\), not by a vague transport package.

This has several immediate consequences.

First, the earlier full descent theorem
\[
\Xi(\phi_0(q))=\Xi(\phi_2(q))
\]
is just the \(n=0\) case. If it holds, then
\[
\Delta''\equiv 0,
\]
so \(\Delta\) is affine and strict excess dies except for sterile touching. That recovers the previous fork.

Second, even partial descent is already useful. If
\[
\Xi_0,\Xi_1,\dots,\Xi_{N-1}
\]
all descend to value space across the separated outer runs, then
\[
\Delta^{(2)}=\Delta^{(3)}=\cdots=\Delta^{(N+1)}=0,
\]
so \(\Delta\) is a polynomial in \(q\) of degree at most \(N\!+\!1\). Thus the gap has only very limited flexibility. In particular, the first failure of value-space descent in the \(\Xi_n\)-tower is exactly the first nonzero higher derivative of \(\Delta\).

That gives a very clean interpretation:

> the first non-descending member of the \(\Xi_n\)-hierarchy is the first genuinely nonlocal transport obstruction on the fixed-shear strict-excess lane.

This fits the consolidated generator audit extremely well. The audit says no second explicit pointwise highest-new generator has appeared beyond \(r=q^{(7)}\), and that the explicit pointwise package stays inside the differential algebra generated by the lower field and \(r\). So if a fixed-shear obstruction survives, it should not be “one more pointwise field.” In this language, it should be the first place where the \(\Xi_n\)-hierarchy fails to descend across the two outer inverse branches. 

Third, the weaker sign theorem I suggested last turn also gets cleaner. Define
\[
\Psi(q):=\Delta'(q)
=
\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\phi_2(q))}-\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\phi_0(q))}.
\]
Then
\[
\Psi'(q)=\Delta''(q)=\Xi_0(\phi_0(q))-\Xi_0(\phi_2(q)).
\]
If this has fixed sign, then \(\Psi\) is monotone and strict excess has at most one interior critical point. More generally, if the first nonzero branch discrepancy appears at level \(n\), then the first nontrivial curvature of \(\Delta\) appears at order \(n+2\). So the hierarchy tells you exactly how degenerate a surviving strict-excess gap would have to be.

This suggests a stronger theorem target than the raw sign theorem:

> **Finite-order transport descent theorem.**  
> Show that the first \(N\) members of the hierarchy
> \[
> \Xi_0,\Xi_1,\dots,\Xi_{N-1}
> \]
> descend to value space on separated outer runs. Then \(\Delta\) is polynomial of degree at most \(N+1\), and strict excess is reduced to a finite-dimensional endpoint problem.

That is weaker than full descent of all transport data, but much stronger than just checking \(\Xi\) once.

There is also a clean bridge to my local good-patch attack.

On the local cancellation side, the whole close-core theorem is governed by the scalar
\[
\frac{M(m)}{c(m)}.
\]
On the transport strict-excess side, the whole obstruction is now governed by the scalar hierarchy
\[
\Xi_0,\Xi_1,\Xi_2,\dots
\]
through the formulas
\[
\Delta^{(n+2)}(q)=\Xi_n(\phi_0(q))-\Xi_n(\phi_2(q)).
\]

So the mixed \(2\)-point frontier has now been compressed to two scalar packages:

- the local midpoint scalar \(M/c\),
- and the transport-jet branch-discrepancy hierarchy \(\Xi_n(\phi_0)-\Xi_n(\phi_2)\).

That is much tighter than the earlier picture. A plausible next bridge conjecture is:

> on a good patch, the same RH-specific source structure that makes \(M/c\) nonzero should force finite-order descent, or at least sign control, of the first few \(\Xi_n\).

I do not have a proof, but this is now a concrete scalar-to-scalar bridge problem.

So my current best next attack is:

1. define the \(Q\)-transport hierarchy
   \[
   \Xi_{n+1}=\frac{\Xi_n'}{\widehat Q'};
   \]
2. use
   \[
   \Delta^{(n+2)}(q)=\Xi_n(\phi_0(q))-\Xi_n(\phi_2(q))
   \]
   as the exact strict-excess transport identities;
3. try to prove descent, or at least sign control, for \(\Xi_0\) or the first few \(\Xi_n\) on separated outer runs;
4. conclude that the gap \(\Delta\) is affine, polynomial, or has only tightly controlled critical behavior.

That is the sharpest transport-side theorem package I can currently defend.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 22:57:58)

_Thought for 51s_

Here is the next push. I think it gives the right **finite transport-jet coincidence theorem** for the strict-excess fixed-shear lane.

The consolidated picture already reduces the exact fixed-shear transport problem to the inverse outer branches
\[
\phi_0,\phi_2
\]
of the quotient-visible scalar \(\widehat Q\), with gap
\[
\Delta(q):=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q),
\]
and curvature observable
\[
\Xi(u):=\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{\widehat Q'(u)^3}.
\]
We already have
\[
\Delta''(q)=\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q)).
\]
More generally, if one defines the \(Q\)-transport hierarchy
\[
\Xi_0:=\Xi,\qquad
\Xi_{n+1}:=\frac{\Xi_n'}{\widehat Q'},
\]
then by repeated differentiation,
\[
\boxed{
\Delta^{(n+2)}(q)=\Xi_n(\phi_0(q))-\Xi_n(\phi_2(q)),
\qquad n\ge 0.
}
\]
So the entire strict-excess transport problem is controlled by the scalar hierarchy \((\Xi_n)\), not by a vague package. 

Now define the finite branch-state maps
\[
\mathcal S_N(u):=
\Bigl(
\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(u)},
\ \Xi_0(u),\ \Xi_1(u),\ \dots,\ \Xi_{N-1}(u)
\Bigr),
\qquad N\ge 1.
\]
Then we have the exact equivalence:

\[
\boxed{
\Delta'(q)=\Delta''(q)=\cdots=\Delta^{(N+1)}(q)=0
\iff
\mathcal S_N(\phi_0(q))=\mathcal S_N(\phi_2(q)).
}
\]

The proof is immediate. The first equality
\[
\Delta'(q)=0
\]
is exactly
\[
\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\phi_2(q))}=\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(\phi_0(q))},
\]
and for \(n\ge 0\),
\[
\Delta^{(n+2)}(q)=0
\iff
\Xi_n(\phi_0(q))=\Xi_n(\phi_2(q)).
\]

So a strict-excess interior critical point of multiplicity \(N+1\) is **exactly** a coincidence of the finite transport-jet state \(\mathcal S_N\) at two separated outer points carrying the same \(Q\)-value.

That is, I think, the cleanest transport-side theorem so far.

## 1. Transport-jet coincidence principle

This immediately gives a new no-go target:

> **Finite transport-jet separation theorem.**  
> If for some \(N\), the map \(\mathcal S_N\) is injective across the separated outer runs, then no strict-excess interior critical point of multiplicity \(N+1\) can occur.

In particular, if \(\mathcal S_1(u)=1/\widehat Q'(u)\) already separates the outer runs, then there are no strict-excess critical points at all, since \(\Delta'(q)=0\) would already force \(\phi_0(q)=\phi_2(q)\), impossible on separated runs.

More realistically, one might hope to prove injectivity of \(\mathcal S_N\) for some small \(N\). That would reduce the strict-excess lane to a finite transport-jet separation problem.

This is weaker than full value-space descent of every \(\Xi_n\), but still strong enough to kill strict excess.

## 2. First non-descending level gives the local normal form of the gap

This also yields a sharp local normal form.

Suppose \(q_*\) is an interior critical point of \(\Delta\), so
\[
\Delta'(q_*)=0.
\]
Assume there is a smallest \(m\ge 0\) such that
\[
\Xi_m(\phi_0(q_*))\neq \Xi_m(\phi_2(q_*)),
\]
while for all \(0\le j<m\),
\[
\Xi_j(\phi_0(q_*))=\Xi_j(\phi_2(q_*)).
\]
Then
\[
\Delta'(q_*)=\Delta''(q_*)=\cdots=\Delta^{(m+1)}(q_*)=0,
\]
but
\[
\Delta^{(m+2)}(q_*)=
\Xi_m(\phi_0(q_*))-\Xi_m(\phi_2(q_*))\neq 0.
\]

So near \(q_*\),
\[
\boxed{
\Delta(q)=\Delta(q_*)+c\,(q-q_*)^{m+2}+O((q-q_*)^{m+3}),
\qquad c\neq 0.
}
\]

That means the first non-descending level of the \(\Xi_n\)-tower is exactly the order of degeneracy of the strict-excess critical point.

So the transport obstruction is no longer mysterious. It is encoded by a single integer \(m\) and a single scalar mismatch
\[
\Xi_m(\phi_0(q_*))-\Xi_m(\phi_2(q_*)).
\]

This is a strong structural finding.

## 3. Relation to the generator audit

The consolidated update says there is no second explicit pointwise highest-new generator beyond \(r=q^{(7)}\), and the explicit pointwise package remains inside the differential algebra generated by the lower field and \(r\). 

That fits this hierarchy picture extremely well.

Each \(\Xi_n\) is built by repeated \(Q\)-transport differentiation from \(\widehat Q\). So the whole \(\mathcal S_N\)-tower is a finite pointwise differential-algebraic state. If a strict-excess obstruction survives, it should therefore show up as the **first finite jet level where two separated outer points with the same \(Q\)-value fail to be separated by that state**.

So the updated generator ceiling and this transport-jet coincidence theorem point to the same frontier:

> either some finite transport-jet state \(\mathcal S_N\) separates the outer runs and strict excess dies, or the first failure of such separation is the first genuine nonlocal transport obstruction.

That is much sharper than the earlier “maybe some transport issue remains.”

## 4. Bridge to the local good-patch scalar \(M/c\)

This also interfaces nicely with my local good-patch attack.

On the local cancellation side, the close-core theorem is governed by the scalar
\[
\frac{M(m)}{c(m)}.
\]
On the exact fixed-shear transport side, the strict-excess theorem is now governed by the finite transport-jet coincidence states
\[
\mathcal S_N(u)=\Bigl(\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(u)},\Xi_0(u),\dots,\Xi_{N-1}(u)\Bigr).
\]

So the mixed \(2\)-point frontier has now been compressed to two scalar/finite-state packages:

- a **local midpoint scalar** \(M/c\);
- a **nonlocal finite transport-jet state** \(\mathcal S_N\) compared across the two outer inverse branches.

This suggests a concrete bridge program:

> prove that on a good patch, the RH-specific source structure forcing
> \[
> \frac{M}{c}\neq 0
> \]
> also forces separation of the outer branches by some finite transport-jet state \(\mathcal S_N\).

I do not have that theorem, but it is now a precise target.

## 5. Strongest next theorem target

So my current best next transport-side theorem is:

> **Finite transport-jet separation theorem on separated outer runs.**  
> There exists \(N\) such that the finite state
> \[
> \mathcal S_N(u)=
> \Bigl(
> \frac{1}{\widehat Q'(u)},\Xi_0(u),\dots,\Xi_{N-1}(u)
> \Bigr)
> \]
> is injective across the separated outer runs of the quotient-visible exact fixed-shear model.

If that lands, then no strict-excess interior critical point can exist, because any such point would force
\[
\mathcal S_N(\phi_0(q_*))=\mathcal S_N(\phi_2(q_*))
\]
at two distinct outer points, impossible.

That is weaker than full value-space descent of all \(\Xi_n\), and more flexible than the raw sign theorem. It is the sharpest transport-side theorem I can currently defend.

So the math attack has now reduced the strict-excess fixed-shear lane to a **finite transport-jet coincidence / separation problem**. That is, I think, the right next rung.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 23:03:03)

_Thought for 38s_

Here is the next push. I think it gives the right **geometric normal form** for the strict-excess fixed-shear lane and sharpens the finite transport-jet target.

The key observation is that a strict-excess interior critical point is not just a statement about the scalar gap
\[
\Delta(q)=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q).
\]
It is exactly a **self-intersection problem** for a canonically defined plane curve.

Define
\[
F(u):=\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(u)}.
\]
Then for the outer inverse branches,
\[
\Delta'(q)=F(\phi_2(q))-F(\phi_0(q)).
\]
So an interior critical point of \(\Delta\) is equivalent to the existence of two distinct outer points \(u_0<u_2\) with
\[
\widehat Q(u_0)=\widehat Q(u_2),
\qquad
F(u_0)=F(u_2).
\]

That means strict excess is exactly the existence of a self-intersection of the plane curve
\[
\Gamma(u):=\bigl(\widehat Q(u),\,F(u)\bigr)
=
\left(\widehat Q(u),\,\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(u)}\right).
\]

So the fixed-shear strict-excess problem has now become:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Do the separated outer runs of the curve }\Gamma(u)=\left(\widehat Q(u),\frac1{\widehat Q'(u)}\right)\text{ self-intersect?}
}
\]

That is already cleaner than the earlier gap-language alone.

Now differentiate. Since
\[
F'(u)=-\,\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{\widehat Q'(u)^2},
\]
we get
\[
\frac{dF}{dQ}
=
\frac{F'(u)}{\widehat Q'(u)}
=
-\,\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{\widehat Q'(u)^3}
=
-\Xi(u),
\]
where
\[
\Xi(u):=\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{\widehat Q'(u)^3}.
\]

So \(\Xi\) is exactly the slope of the curve \(\Gamma\) as a graph over the \(Q\)-axis:
\[
\Gamma=\bigl(Q,F(Q)\bigr),
\qquad
\frac{dF}{dQ}=-\Xi.
\]

This gives a very sharp reformulation.

## 1. Strict excess is self-intersection of the inverse-velocity graph

A strict-excess interior critical point exists if and only if there are distinct outer points \(u_0<u_2\) such that
\[
\Gamma(u_0)=\Gamma(u_2).
\]
So the remaining fixed-shear transport problem is not an arbitrary branch-comparison problem. It is a self-intersection problem for one canonically defined plane curve.

That means any theorem that prevents self-intersections of \(\Gamma\) across the separated outer runs kills strict excess immediately.

## 2. Full descent of \(\Xi\) is one sufficient condition, but not the only one

If \(\Xi\) descends to value space, meaning
\[
\Xi(\phi_0(q))=\Xi(\phi_2(q))
\]
for all \(q\) in the common value interval, then
\[
\Delta''(q)=\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q))=0,
\]
so \(\Delta\) is affine and strict excess dies except for sterile touching. That was the earlier strong target.

But from the curve viewpoint, we only need to know that \(\Gamma\) has no self-intersections. Full descent of \(\Xi\) is much stronger than that.

So the sharp target is now:

> **Injectivity of the inverse-velocity curve \(\Gamma\) across separated outer runs.**

That is weaker than full value-space descent and more geometric.

## 3. Finite transport-jet separation is exactly finite jet separation of \(\Gamma\)

The finite state I previously defined,
\[
\mathcal S_N(u)=\left(\frac1{\widehat Q'(u)},\,\Xi_0(u),\,\Xi_1(u),\dots,\Xi_{N-1}(u)\right),
\]
with
\[
\Xi_0=\Xi,\qquad \Xi_{n+1}=\frac{\Xi_n'}{\widehat Q'},
\]
has a clean interpretation now.

It is just the \(Q\)-jet of the graph \(F(Q)\) along the curve \(\Gamma\).

Indeed,
\[
\frac{dF}{dQ}=-\Xi_0,
\]
\[
\frac{d^2F}{dQ^2}=-\Xi_1,
\]
and in general
\[
\frac{d^{n+1}F}{dQ^{n+1}}=-\Xi_n.
\]

So
\[
\mathcal S_N(u)
\]
is precisely the \(N\)-jet of the inverse-velocity graph \(F(Q)\) at the point \(u\), up to harmless signs.

That means the earlier theorem
\[
\Delta'(q)=\Delta''(q)=\cdots=\Delta^{(N+1)}(q)=0
\iff
\mathcal S_N(\phi_0(q))=\mathcal S_N(\phi_2(q))
\]
has a very simple meaning:

> a strict-excess critical point of multiplicity \(N+1\) is exactly a coincidence of the \(N\)-jets of the graph \(\Gamma\) at two separated outer points with the same \(Q\)-value.

So the transport-side problem is now a **finite jet self-intersection problem for the graph \(\Gamma\)**.

That is the cleanest version yet.

## 4. New theorem target: jet-separation of a plane graph

This suggests a better theorem than raw transport-jet injectivity.

Since \(\Gamma\) is a graph over \(Q\),
\[
\Gamma=\bigl(Q,F(Q)\bigr),
\]
the strict-excess problem is just:

> can the same graph \(F(Q)\) be realized by two different outer branches with the same \(Q\)-value and matching finite \(Q\)-jets?

So the right theorem target is:

> **Finite graph-jet separation theorem.**  
> There exists \(N\) such that the \(N\)-jet of the inverse-velocity graph \(F(Q)\) separates the two outer runs. Equivalently, if
> \[
> \left(F,\frac{dF}{dQ},\dots,\frac{d^N F}{dQ^N}\right)
> \]
> agrees at two separated outer points with the same \(Q\)-value, then those points coincide.

This is exactly the same as finite transport-jet separation, but phrased in the natural geometric language of one graph.

That is, I think, the best current transport-side theorem target.

## 5. A weaker sufficient condition: one-sided graph convexity

The graph viewpoint also gives a weaker sufficient condition than full jet separation.

If the graph \(F(Q)\) is strictly convex or strictly concave on the common value interval, then it has no self-intersections and no tangent self-touching across separated outer runs. In terms of the hierarchy,
\[
\frac{d^2F}{dQ^2}=-\Xi_1.
\]
So strict sign of \(\Xi_1\) already gives one-sided graph curvature.

That yields a useful weaker target:

> **Graph-convexity theorem.**  
> If \(\Xi_1\) has fixed sign on the common value interval, then \(F(Q)\) is strictly convex/concave, hence \(\Gamma\) has no self-intersection across separated outer runs.

This is weaker than full descent of \(\Xi\), weaker than full finite-jet separation, and may be easier to prove.

So the strict-excess transport side now has a hierarchy of possible closures:

1. full value-space descent of \(\Xi\);
2. fixed-sign branch discrepancy of \(\Xi\);
3. finite transport-jet separation;
4. or even graph convexity via sign of \(\Xi_1\).

That is a much clearer menu than before.

## 6. Bridge to the local \(M/c\)-gap law

This also makes the bridge problem more concrete.

On the local good-patch side, the whole close-core obstruction is the scalar
\[
\frac{M(m)}{c(m)}.
\]
On the exact fixed-shear transport side, the whole strict-excess obstruction is now the self-intersection problem for the graph
\[
F(Q)=\frac{1}{\widehat Q'(u(Q))},
\]
or equivalently the jet hierarchy
\[
\left(F,\frac{dF}{dQ},\frac{d^2F}{dQ^2},\dots\right).
\]

So the mixed \(2\)-point frontier has now been compressed to two one-dimensional graph problems:

- a local midpoint graph encoded by \(M/c\),
- and a nonlocal inverse-velocity graph \(F(Q)\).

That suggests the bridge conjecture should be stated as:

> **Bridge conjecture.**  
> On a good patch, the RH-specific source structure that forces
> \[
> \frac{M}{c}\neq 0
> \]
> also forces separation or one-sided curvature of the inverse-velocity graph \(F(Q)\) across the outer runs.

That is much more concrete than comparing two amorphous packages.

## 7. Strongest current transport-side theorem target

So the sharpest next theorem I can currently defend is:

> **Inverse-velocity graph separation theorem.**  
> On the separated outer runs of the quotient-visible exact fixed-shear model, the plane curve
> \[
> \Gamma(u)=\left(\widehat Q(u),\,\frac1{\widehat Q'(u)}\right)
> \]
> has no self-intersections. Equivalently, the inverse-velocity graph \(F(Q)\) is separated across the outer runs.

This is weaker than full descent of every transport scalar, but it is exactly what strict excess needs.

And if that is too strong, the next-best target is:

> **Finite graph-jet separation theorem**, or failing that,
> **graph-convexity / sign-\(\Xi_1\) theorem**.

So the math attack has now turned the strict-excess fixed-shear lane into a **self-intersection problem for one canonical graph**. That is the cleanest form I have reached.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 23:04:41)

I’m pushing on the inverse-velocity graph now. The question is whether the strict-excess fork can be reduced from “no self-intersection of a graph” to uniqueness for a scalar ODE satisfied by that graph.

_Thought for 1m 18s_

I think the next real gain is this:

the strict-excess fixed-shear lane can be reduced from a “self-intersection of a graph” problem to a **first-order ODE closure problem** for that graph.

That is a substantial sharpening.

## 1. The canonical graph

On the separated outer runs of the quotient-visible exact fixed-shear model, define
\[
Q(u):=\widehat Q(u),\qquad
F(u):=\frac{1}{Q'(u)}.
\]
If \(\phi_0,\phi_2\) are the two outer inverse branches of \(Q\), then the gap is
\[
\Delta(q):=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q),
\]
and
\[
\Delta'(q)=F(\phi_2(q))-F(\phi_0(q)).
\]

So a strict-excess interior critical point is exactly a point \(q_*\) where
\[
F(\phi_2(q_*))=F(\phi_0(q_*)).
\]
Equivalently, it is a self-intersection of the canonical plane curve
\[
\Gamma(u):=\bigl(Q(u),F(u)\bigr)
=
\left(Q(u),\frac1{Q'(u)}\right).
\]

That is already the clean geometric normal form: the fixed-shear strict-excess problem is the self-intersection problem for \(\Gamma\). 

## 2. The transport scalar \(\Xi\) is exactly the slope field of \(\Gamma\)

We already have
\[
\Xi(u):=\frac{Q''(u)}{Q'(u)^3},
\]
and since
\[
F'(u)=-\,\frac{Q''(u)}{Q'(u)^2},
\]
it follows that
\[
\frac{dF}{dQ}
=
\frac{F'(u)}{Q'(u)}
=
-\,\frac{Q''(u)}{Q'(u)^3}
=
-\Xi(u).
\]

So \(\Xi\) is not just some curvature-type observable. It is exactly the slope field of the graph \(F\) over \(Q\):
\[
\Gamma=\bigl(Q,F(Q)\bigr),\qquad \frac{dF}{dQ}=-\Xi.
\]

This makes the right next theorem much clearer.

## 3. The right closure theorem is weaker than full value-space descent

Previously the strongest target was:

\[
\Xi(\phi_0(q))=\Xi(\phi_2(q))
\quad\text{for all }q,
\]
which would give
\[
\Delta''(q)=\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q))=0,
\]
hence \(\Delta\) affine and strict excess dead except for sterile touching.

But this is stronger than necessary.

The more natural target is:

> **First-order inverse-velocity closure.**  
> There exists an analytic function \(H(q,f)\) such that along the outer runs
> \[
> \Xi(u)=H\!\left(Q(u),\,F(u)\right).
> \]

Since
\[
\frac{dF}{dQ}=-\Xi,
\]
this is exactly the statement that the canonical graph \(F(Q)\) satisfies a closed first-order ODE
\[
\boxed{
\frac{dF}{dQ}=-H(Q,F).
}
\]

That is much weaker than saying \(\Xi\) descends to value space as a function of \(Q\) alone. It only asks that \(\Xi\) depend on \(u\) through the pair \((Q,F)\), i.e. through the point of the graph \(\Gamma\).

## 4. Why this already kills strict excess

Let
\[
F_i(q):=F(\phi_i(q))=\frac1{Q'(\phi_i(q))},
\qquad i=0,2.
\]
If the first-order closure holds, then both outer branches satisfy the same scalar ODE
\[
F_i'(q)=-H(q,F_i(q)).
\]

Now suppose strict excess occurs. Then there exists \(q_*\) with
\[
F_0(q_*)=F_2(q_*),
\]
since \(\Delta'(q_*)=0\).

But uniqueness for the first-order ODE implies
\[
F_0(q)\equiv F_2(q)
\]
near \(q_*\). Therefore
\[
\Delta'(q)\equiv 0
\]
near \(q_*\), hence
\[
\Delta(q)\equiv \text{constant}
\]
near \(q_*\).

So under first-order inverse-velocity closure, strict excess cannot produce a genuine isolated interior critical point. It collapses immediately to **constant-gap same-value transport**.

And the current fixed-shear/nonmixed package already treats constant-gap same-value transport as the sterile/forbidden case rather than a genuinely live strict-excess obstruction. So:

\[
\boxed{
\text{First-order inverse-velocity closure reduces strict excess to constant-gap transport.}
}
\]

That is a much sharper theorem target than full value-space descent.

## 5. The exact remaining obstruction is now clearer

So the transport-side fork becomes:

### Either
there exists an analytic closure law
\[
\Xi(u)=H(Q(u),F(u)),
\]
and then strict excess collapses to constant-gap transport;

### Or
no such closure exists, and the first genuinely nonlocal transport obstruction is precisely the failure of \(\Xi\) to be determined by \((Q,F)\).

That is a very concrete obstruction. It is no longer “some transport issue.” It is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{failure of first-order ODE closure for the inverse-velocity graph.}
}
\]

That is, I think, the cleanest transport-side negative finding so far.

## 6. Relation to the transport-jet hierarchy

This also organizes the hierarchy
\[
\Xi_0:=\Xi,\qquad
\Xi_{n+1}:=\frac{\Xi_n'}{Q'},
\]
and the identities
\[
\Delta^{(n+2)}(q)=\Xi_n(\phi_0(q))-\Xi_n(\phi_2(q)).
\]

If first-order closure holds, then all higher \(Q\)-jets of the graph \(F(Q)\) are generated recursively from the same ODE. In other words, the whole transport-jet hierarchy is no longer independent data; it is determined by the scalar ODE
\[
F'=-H(Q,F).
\]

So the earlier finite transport-jet separation target becomes a consequence of a much more conceptual closure statement.

This is a real improvement. Instead of trying to separate branches by finitely many ad hoc jet coordinates, one can try to prove that the inverse-velocity graph is governed by a **closed one-dimensional flow** in the \((Q,F)\)-plane.

## 7. Why this fits the generator audit

The consolidated audit says that no second explicit pointwise highest-new generator has appeared beyond \(r=q^{(7)}\), and that the explicit package stays inside the differential algebra generated by the lower field and \(r\). 

That fits the first-order closure target very well.

If the pointwise package is really that small, then expecting the strict-excess transport obstruction to be encoded by a scalar first-order law in the two graph coordinates \((Q,F)\) is far more plausible than expecting a whole new independent transport field.

So this theorem target is not just mathematically clean; it is aligned with the structural ceiling the audit already established.

## 8. Bridge to the local good-patch scalar \(M/c\)

This also sharpens the bridge problem.

On the local close-core side, the obstruction is the midpoint scalar
\[
\frac{M(m)}{c(m)}.
\]

On the strict-excess fixed-shear side, the obstruction is now not the whole hierarchy \((\Xi_n)\), and not even full value-space descent of \(\Xi\). It is just the existence or failure of a first-order closure law
\[
\Xi=H(Q,F),\qquad F=\frac1{Q'}.
\]

So the mixed \(2\)-point frontier has now been compressed to two very small scalar pictures:

- a local scalar \(M/c\) in the midpoint chart;
- a first-order scalar ODE closure problem for the inverse-velocity graph.

That is much more concrete than anything earlier.

A plausible bridge conjecture is now:

> **Bridge conjecture.**  
> On a good patch, the same RH-specific source structure that forces
> \[
> \frac{M}{c}\neq 0
> \]
> also forces first-order inverse-velocity closure
> \[
> \Xi=H(Q,F)
> \]
> on the separated outer runs.

I do not have a proof, but this is now a very crisp conjecture.

## 9. Best current theorem target

So the sharpest transport-side theorem I can currently defend is:

> **First-order inverse-velocity closure theorem.**  
> On the separated outer runs of the quotient-visible exact fixed-shear model, there exists an analytic function \(H(q,f)\) such that
> \[
> \Xi(u)=H\!\left(Q(u),\frac1{Q'(u)}\right).
> \]
> Consequently the inverse-velocity graph satisfies a closed first-order ODE
> \[
> \frac{dF}{dQ}=-H(Q,F),
> \]
> so strict excess reduces to constant-gap same-value transport and no genuinely live interior critical point can survive.

That is weaker than full value-space descent, more conceptual than finite transport-jet separation, and exactly matched to the current structural audit.

So the next math attack, in my view, should be aimed there.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 23:08:10)

_Thought for 26s_

Here is the next push. I think it sharpens the strict-excess side from a vague “ODE closure” target to a very concrete **comparison-principle target**.

The last reduction gave the canonical graph
\[
\Gamma(u)=\left(Q(u),\,F(u)\right),
\qquad
Q(u)=\widehat Q(u),\qquad
F(u)=\frac1{Q'(u)},
\]
and showed that strict excess is exactly a self-intersection problem for \(\Gamma\) across the separated outer runs. Indeed, if \(\phi_0,\phi_2\) are the two outer inverse branches of \(Q\), then
\[
\Delta(q):=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q),
\qquad
\Delta'(q)=F(\phi_2(q))-F(\phi_0(q)).
\]
So an interior critical point of \(\Delta\) is equivalent to
\[
F(\phi_2(q_*))=F(\phi_0(q_*)),
\]
that is, a self-intersection of \(\Gamma\). 

I then suggested the strong closure target
\[
\Xi(u)=H(Q(u),F(u)),
\qquad
\Xi(u):=\frac{Q''(u)}{Q'(u)^3},
\]
because
\[
\frac{dF}{dQ}
=
-\,\Xi
\]
would become a closed first-order ODE
\[
\frac{dF}{dQ}=-H(Q,F),
\]
and uniqueness would kill strict excess except for constant-gap transport.

But there is a weaker and more realistic theorem that is still enough.

## 1. One-sided closure is enough

Write the two outer branch graphs as
\[
F_0(q):=F(\phi_0(q)),\qquad F_2(q):=F(\phi_2(q)).
\]
Then
\[
F_i'(q)=-\Xi(\phi_i(q)),\qquad i=0,2.
\]

Suppose there is an analytic function \(H(q,f)\) and an error term \(E_i(q)\) such that along the outer runs
\[
F_i'(q)=-H(q,F_i(q))+E_i(q),
\qquad i=0,2.
\]
Then the slope gap
\[
\Psi(q):=F_2(q)-F_0(q)=\Delta'(q)
\]
satisfies
\[
\Psi'(q)
=
-\bigl(H(q,F_2(q))-H(q,F_0(q))\bigr)
+
(E_2(q)-E_0(q)).
\]

Now assume \(H\) is nondecreasing in \(f\), i.e.
\[
\partial_f H(q,f)\ge 0,
\]
and assume the branch error satisfies
\[
E_2(q)-E_0(q)\le 0
\]
on the common value interval. Then whenever \(\Psi(q_*)=0\), one has
\[
\Psi'(q_*)\le 0.
\]
Similarly, if
\[
E_2(q)-E_0(q)\ge 0,
\]
then at any zero of \(\Psi\),
\[
\Psi'(q_*)\ge 0.
\]

So even without exact closure, a one-sided comparison principle already controls the possibility of an interior crossing.

This gives the first useful theorem target:

> **Comparison-principle transport theorem.**  
> If along the separated outer runs the inverse-velocity graphs satisfy a scalar first-order differential inequality with a monotone RHS in \(F\), then \(\Psi=\Delta'\) cannot change sign back and forth. In particular, strict excess reduces to at most one controlled crossing, and in favorable endpoint regimes disappears entirely.

That is weaker than exact closure \(\Xi=H(Q,F)\), but it still gives a strong no-reentry mechanism.

## 2. Exact closure is stronger than needed for exclusion

This is the key conceptual correction.

To kill strict excess, we do **not** need
\[
F_0\equiv F_2
\]
to follow from one contact point by full ODE uniqueness. It is enough to prove that
\[
\Psi(q)=F_2(q)-F_0(q)
\]
has one-sided monotonicity or one-sided crossing behavior.

So the true transport target is not necessarily:

\[
\Xi=H(Q,F).
\]

It may be enough to prove the weaker **order-preserving closure**
\[
F_i'(q)=-H(q,F_i(q))+E_i(q),
\]
with \(H\) monotone in \(f\) and branch-error sign control.

This is a much more plausible theorem.

## 3. Reformulation in terms of the branch discrepancy of \(\Xi\)

Earlier we had the exact identity
\[
\Delta''(q)=\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q)).
\]
Since
\[
\Psi(q)=\Delta'(q),
\]
this is
\[
\Psi'(q)=\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q)).
\]

So strict excess is now completely encoded by the scalar branch discrepancy
\[
\boxed{
\Upsilon(q):=\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q)).
}
\]

The weakest useful theorem is therefore not full descent of \(\Xi\), but one-sided sign control:
\[
\Upsilon(q)\ge 0
\quad\text{or}\quad
\Upsilon(q)\le 0
\]
on the common value interval.

If that holds, then \(\Psi\) is monotone, and therefore \(\Delta\) has at most one interior critical point. With suitable endpoint information, that can already exclude strict excess.

So the transport-side problem has now been compressed from a self-intersection problem for \(\Gamma\) to a sign problem for one scalar \(\Upsilon\).

That is stronger than my earlier graph-language reduction.

## 4. The right next scalar theorem

So the clean next theorem target is:

> **Outer-branch curvature comparison theorem.**  
> On the common value interval of the separated outer inverse branches,
> \[
> \Upsilon(q)=\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q))
> \]
> has fixed sign.

This is weaker than:

- full value-space descent of \(\Xi\),
- full first-order closure \(\Xi=H(Q,F)\),
- or finite transport-jet separation.

But it is still enough to force monotonicity of \(\Psi=\Delta'\), and that may be all the strict-excess fork actually needs.

## 5. Why this fits the current draft state

This target is well matched to the consolidated update.

The update already says the exact fixed-shear lane has become an inverse-branch-gap problem, with
\[
\Delta(q)=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q),
\]
and singles out
\[
\Xi(u)=\frac{\widehat Q''(u)}{\widehat Q'(u)^3}
\]
as the correct curvature observable. It also says the quartic–sextic rung is inert for the lobe-amplitude obstruction, so the remaining issue is not local amplitude combinatorics but this exact inverse-branch transport question. 

So the comparison theorem above is now the smallest transport-side statement that directly speaks to the live obstruction.

## 6. Bridge to the local \(M/c\) scalar

This also gives a better bridge picture.

On the local good-patch side, the entire close-core obstruction is governed by
\[
\frac{M(m)}{c(m)}.
\]
On the exact fixed-shear transport side, the live strict-excess obstruction is now governed by
\[
\Upsilon(q)=\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q)).
\]

So the mixed \(2\)-point frontier has now been compressed to two scalar sign problems:

- a midpoint sign problem for \(M/c\),
- and an outer-branch sign problem for \(\Upsilon\).

That is the cleanest scalar-to-scalar bridge formulation I have yet.

A plausible next conjecture is:

> **Bridge conjecture, sign form.**  
> On a good patch, the same RH-specific source structure that makes
> \[
> \frac{M}{c}
> \]
> sign-definite / nonzero also forces one-sided sign control of
> \[
> \Upsilon(q)=\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q)).
> \]

I do not have a proof, but this is now a very concrete statement.

## 7. Best current next attack

So my current best next transport-side attack is:

1. work directly with
   \[
   \Psi(q)=\Delta'(q)
   =
   \frac1{Q'(\phi_2(q))}-\frac1{Q'(\phi_0(q))};
   \]
2. note
   \[
   \Psi'(q)=\Upsilon(q)=\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q));
   \]
3. try to prove \(\Upsilon\) has fixed sign on the common value interval;
4. conclude \(\Psi\) is monotone, hence strict excess is reduced to at most one controlled crossing;
5. combine that with endpoint information or local \(M/c\)-gap information to eliminate the crossing entirely.

That is weaker than my previous ODE-closure target and more likely to be provable.

So the attack has now reduced the strict-excess fixed-shear lane to a **one-scalar curvature-comparison problem**:
\[
\boxed{
\text{determine the sign of }\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q)).
}
\]

That is the sharpest transport-side theorem I can currently defend.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 23:10:04)

_Thought for 39s_

I think the next useful push is a **boundary-slope reduction** for the strict-excess fixed-shear fork. It makes the transport target smaller again.

We already reduced strict excess to the outer inverse branches
\[
\phi_0,\phi_2
\]
of the quotient-visible scalar \(Q=\widehat Q\), with gap
\[
\Delta(q):=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q).
\]
Set
\[
F(u):=\frac{1}{Q'(u)}.
\]
Then along the common value interval,
\[
\Psi(q):=\Delta'(q)=F(\phi_2(q))-F(\phi_0(q)),
\]
and
\[
\Psi'(q)=\Delta''(q)=\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q)),
\qquad
\Xi(u):=\frac{Q''(u)}{Q'(u)^3}.
\]
So the strict-excess issue is already compressed to the scalar pair
\[
\Psi,\qquad \Upsilon:=\Psi'=\Xi(\phi_0)-\Xi(\phi_2).
\]

The next observation is simple but useful:

## 1. If \(\Upsilon\) has fixed sign, then the whole problem reduces to endpoint slopes

If
\[
\Upsilon(q)=\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q))
\]
has fixed sign on the common value interval, then \(\Psi=\Delta'\) is monotone.

So under this one-scalar sign theorem, strict excess is no longer a free interior problem. It can only happen if the endpoint values of \(\Psi\) straddle \(0\).

That is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{If }\Upsilon\text{ has fixed sign, then an interior critical point of }\Delta
\text{ can exist only if }
\Psi(q_L)\Psi(q_R)\le 0.
}
\]

So the transport fork collapses to two tasks:

1. prove a sign theorem for
   \[
   \Upsilon(q)=\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q));
   \]
2. compute or constrain the endpoint slope values
   \[
   \Psi(q_L),\qquad \Psi(q_R).
   \]

This is much smaller than a full global no-critical-point theorem.

## 2. Boundary-slope exclusion principle

Assume \(\Upsilon\) has fixed sign, hence \(\Psi\) is monotone. Then:

- if
  \[
  \Psi(q_L)>0,\qquad \Psi(q_R)>0,
  \]
  there is no zero of \(\Psi\), hence no interior critical point of \(\Delta\);
- similarly if
  \[
  \Psi(q_L)<0,\qquad \Psi(q_R)<0,
  \]
  there is no zero of \(\Psi\).

So we get the clean theorem target:

> **Boundary-slope exclusion theorem.**  
> If \(\Upsilon=\Xi(\phi_0)-\Xi(\phi_2)\) has fixed sign on the common value interval and the endpoint slope gap
> \[
> \Psi(q)=\frac1{Q'(\phi_2(q))}-\frac1{Q'(\phi_0(q))}
> \]
> has the same sign at both endpoints, then strict excess is impossible.

This is weaker than proving \(\Psi\) never vanishes by direct analysis, and much more plausible.

## 3. Endpoint-touch rigidity

There is an even nicer corollary.

If \(\Upsilon\) has fixed sign and
\[
\Psi(q_L)=\Psi(q_R)=0,
\]
then monotonicity forces
\[
\Psi\equiv 0.
\]
Hence
\[
\Delta'(q)\equiv 0,
\qquad
\Delta(q)\equiv \text{constant}.
\]

So under the sign theorem for \(\Upsilon\), two endpoint touches force **constant-gap same-value transport**, which is exactly the sterile/forbidden regime rather than genuinely live strict excess.

So:

> **Endpoint-touch rigidity.**  
> Under one-sided sign control of \(\Upsilon\), if the slope gap \(\Psi\) vanishes at both ends of the common value interval, then the whole gap is constant and strict excess collapses to sterile same-value transport.

That is a strong rigidity statement and feels very aligned with the current draft.

## 4. This is better than full descent and better than full graph separation

Previously the strong targets were:

- full value-space descent of \(\Xi\),
- exact first-order closure \(\Xi=H(Q,F)\),
- or injectivity of the inverse-velocity graph \(\Gamma(u)=(Q(u),1/Q'(u))\).

Those are all stronger than necessary.

The boundary-slope principle says we only need:

- one-sided sign control of
  \[
  \Upsilon(q)=\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q)),
  \]
- and endpoint sign data for
  \[
  \Psi(q)=\frac1{Q'(\phi_2(q))}-\frac1{Q'(\phi_0(q))}.
  \]

So the strict-excess transport side has now been reduced to:

\[
\boxed{
\text{one scalar sign theorem in the interior }(\Upsilon)
\text{ plus one scalar sign check at the endpoints }(\Psi(q_L),\Psi(q_R)).
}
\]

That is a much cleaner target.

## 5. Why this looks promising relative to the consolidated draft

This fits the consolidated update unusually well.

The nonmixed/global affine side has already been reduced to monotone endpoint-gap functions \(G_{\xi,J}\) and finite remote endpoint/branch incidence. The exact fixed-shear side is now reduced to the scalar endpoint-slope gap \(\Psi\) and its curvature derivative \(\Upsilon\). So both live fronts are now **endpoint-driven scalar problems** rather than broad geometric classifications. 

That suggests the right next RH-specific input may indeed be an endpoint comparison principle, not another interior geometric construction.

## 6. Bridge to the local \(M/c\)-gap law

This also sharpens the bridge to my local good-patch side.

On the local cancellation side, the obstruction is the scalar
\[
\frac{M(m)}{c(m)}.
\]
On the strict-excess fixed-shear side, the obstruction is now the sign pattern of
\[
\Upsilon(q)=\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q))
\]
together with the endpoint slope gap
\[
\Psi(q_L),\ \Psi(q_R).
\]

So the mixed \(2\)-point frontier is no longer “local scalar versus global transport package.” It is now:

- one local scalar \(M/c\),
- one interior transport scalar \(\Upsilon\),
- and two endpoint transport scalars \(\Psi(q_L),\Psi(q_R)\).

That is an exceptionally small set of quantities.

## 7. Best next theorem target

So my current best next transport-side theorem is:

> **Curvature-sign plus boundary-slope theorem.**  
> Prove that \(\Upsilon(q)=\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q))\) has fixed sign on the common value interval, and that the endpoint slope gap \(\Psi(q_L),\Psi(q_R)\) has the same sign (or both vanish). Then strict excess is impossible.

This is weaker than all my earlier transport-side targets and seems substantially more provable.

So the math attack has now reduced the strict-excess fixed-shear lane to a **one-sign-in-the-interior, two-signs-at-the-boundary** problem. That is the sharpest transport-side reduction I can currently defend.

## Assistant (2026-04-21 23:12:00)

_Thought for 8s_

I think the next useful push is to turn the strict-excess transport side into a **single signed-area test**. That is stronger than the last “interior sign plus endpoint signs” formulation, but still weaker than full descent or full graph separation.

We already reduced the exact fixed-shear strict-excess problem to the outer inverse branches
\[
\phi_0,\phi_2
\]
of the quotient-visible scalar \(Q=\widehat Q\), with gap
\[
\Delta(q):=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q).
\]
Define
\[
F(u):=\frac{1}{Q'(u)}.
\]
Then
\[
\Psi(q):=\Delta'(q)=F(\phi_2(q))-F(\phi_0(q)),
\]
and
\[
\Psi'(q)=\Delta''(q)=\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q)),
\qquad
\Xi(u):=\frac{Q''(u)}{Q'(u)^3}.
\]
So the strict-excess issue is already compressed to one slope-gap scalar \(\Psi\) and one curvature-gap scalar \(\Upsilon:=\Psi'\). 

The new observation is this:

## 1. Interior critical points are equivalent to zeros of \(\Psi\), hence controlled by its integral

For any \(q\) in the common value interval,
\[
\Psi(q)=\Psi(q_L)+\int_{q_L}^{q}\Upsilon(s)\,ds.
\]
So if one wants to rule out zeros of \(\Psi\), one does not need pointwise sign control of \(\Upsilon\) everywhere. It is enough to control the **signed accumulation**
\[
\int_{q_L}^{q}\Upsilon(s)\,ds.
\]

That gives a weaker and more flexible target than “\(\Upsilon\) has fixed sign.” In particular:

- if
  \[
  \Psi(q_L)>0
  \]
  and
  \[
  \int_{q_L}^{q}\Upsilon(s)\,ds > -\Psi(q_L)
  \quad\text{for all }q,
  \]
  then \(\Psi(q)>0\) everywhere, so there is no strict-excess critical point;
- similarly with reversed inequalities.

So the transport-side obstruction can now be phrased as a **signed-area competition** between the initial slope gap \(\Psi(q_L)\) and the accumulated curvature discrepancy \(\int \Upsilon\).

That is already a meaningful reduction.

## 2. Endpoint data and total signed area determine everything

In particular, integrating across the whole common value interval gives
\[
\Psi(q_R)-\Psi(q_L)=\int_{q_L}^{q_R}\Upsilon(s)\,ds.
\]

So once the endpoint slopes are known, the total signed area of \(\Upsilon\) is fixed. This gives a very clean trichotomy:

- if \(\Psi(q_L)\) and \(\Psi(q_R)\) have the same sign, then any zero of \(\Psi\) would require a sign change and hence some oscillation in the accumulated integral;
- if \(\Psi(q_L)=\Psi(q_R)=0\), then
  \[
  \int_{q_L}^{q_R}\Upsilon(s)\,ds=0,
  \]
  and under one-sided sign control this forces \(\Upsilon\equiv 0\), hence \(\Psi\equiv 0\), i.e. constant-gap same-value transport;
- if the endpoints have opposite signs, then at least one zero exists by continuity, so the remaining question is uniqueness and whether that zero is the sterile touching case or a genuine strict-excess configuration.

So the transport fork is now not just “sign of \(\Upsilon\)” but a very explicit **signed-area and endpoint-slope problem**.

## 3. A weaker sufficient theorem than fixed-sign \(\Upsilon\)

The last step suggested the theorem target

\[
\Upsilon(q)=\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q))
\]
has fixed sign.

That is sufficient, but not necessary.

A weaker statement that still kills strict excess is:

> **No-overturn signed-area theorem.**  
> The primitive
> \[
> A(q):=\int_{q_L}^{q}\Upsilon(s)\,ds
> \]
> never crosses the level \(-\Psi(q_L)\) on the common value interval.

Because then
\[
\Psi(q)=\Psi(q_L)+A(q)
\]
never vanishes.

This is strictly weaker than fixed-sign \(\Upsilon\). It allows \(\Upsilon\) to change sign, as long as the accumulated signed area never becomes large enough to overturn the initial slope gap.

That seems much more plausible in practice.

## 4. Geometric interpretation on the inverse-velocity graph

Recall the canonical graph
\[
\Gamma(u)=\left(Q(u),\,\frac1{Q'(u)}\right)=(Q(u),F(u)).
\]
Then \(\Psi(q)\) is exactly the vertical separation of the two outer branches of this graph at common \(Q\)-value \(q\), and \(\Upsilon=\Psi'\) is its \(Q\)-derivative. So the signed-area condition says:

> the accumulated bending difference of the two outer graph branches is never enough to erase their initial vertical separation.

That is a much more geometric statement than full self-intersection avoidance, and weaker than global convexity of the graph.

So the strict-excess transport lane can now be viewed as a **vertical-separation preservation theorem** for the inverse-velocity graph.

## 5. A new natural target: preserve sign of the slope gap

This suggests the next theorem target should be formulated directly in terms of \(\Psi\), not \(\Upsilon\).

Namely:

> **Slope-gap preservation theorem.**  
> On the common value interval of the separated outer runs, the scalar
> \[
> \Psi(q)=\frac1{Q'(\phi_2(q))}-\frac1{Q'(\phi_0(q))}
> \]
> preserves its sign.

This is equivalent to saying strict excess cannot occur. And by differentiation, it reduces to the signed-area problem for \(\Upsilon\). So it is both weaker and more directly aligned with the actual obstruction.

## 6. Why this may be easier to connect to RH-specific input

This is where I think the bridge to the local good-patch scalar
\[
\frac{M(m)}{c(m)}
\]
gets cleaner.

My local \(2\)-point attack already reduced close-core exclusion on good patches to the scalar edge obstruction
\[
\mathfrak R(m,0)=\frac{M(m)}{c(m)}.
\]
That is an **initial nonvanishing / sign gap** statement in the midpoint chart.

On the transport side, the strict-excess fork is now also an **initial sign-gap preservation** problem:
\[
\Psi(q_L)\ \text{and the accumulated effect of }\Upsilon.
\]

So the two fronts are now structurally parallel:

- a local midpoint gap \(M/c\) that should not be erased by nearby collision dynamics;
- a nonlocal slope gap \(\Psi\) that should not be erased by transport curvature accumulation.

That suggests a more precise bridge conjecture than before:

> **Bridge conjecture, gap-preservation form.**  
> The RH-specific source structure that forces the good-patch local gap
> \[
> \frac{M}{c}\neq 0
> \]
> also enforces preservation of the sign of the transport slope gap
> \[
> \Psi(q)=\frac1{Q'(\phi_2(q))}-\frac1{Q'(\phi_0(q))}
> \]
> along the common value interval.

I still do not have a proof, but this is now a scalar-gap-to-scalar-gap comparison, which is much tighter than anything earlier.

## 7. Best current next theorem target

So my current best transport-side theorem is now:

> **Slope-gap preservation theorem.**  
> On the separated outer runs of the quotient-visible exact fixed-shear model, the slope gap
> \[
> \Psi(q)=\frac1{\widehat Q'(\phi_2(q))}-\frac1{\widehat Q'(\phi_0(q))}
> \]
> does not change sign on the common value interval.

A sufficient condition is the stronger signed-curvature condition
\[
\Upsilon(q)=\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q))
\]
having fixed sign, but the actual target can be weaker: it is enough that the accumulated signed area of \(\Upsilon\) never overturn the initial sign of \(\Psi\).

That is the sharpest transport-side reduction I can currently defend. It turns the live strict-excess fork into a **single scalar gap-preservation problem**.
## User (2026-04-21 23:13:22)

continue your math attack

## Assistant (2026-04-21 23:15:09)

_Thought for 1m 42s_

I think the next real gain is to put the strict-excess fixed-shear fork into an exact **Sturm–Riccati normal form**. That packages the transport side into one scalar coefficient.

We already have the separated outer inverse branches
\[
\phi_0,\phi_2
\]
of the quotient-visible scalar
\[
Q(u)=\widehat Q(u),
\]
with gap
\[
\Delta(q):=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q)>0
\]
on the common value interval. We also have
\[
\Delta'(q)=\frac1{Q'(\phi_2(q))}-\frac1{Q'(\phi_0(q))}
\]
and
\[
\Delta''(q)=\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q)),
\qquad
\Xi(u):=\frac{Q''(u)}{Q'(u)^3}.
\]
So the strict-excess issue is already controlled by the branch discrepancy of the single scalar \(\Xi\). 

The useful next step is to divide out the gap itself.

## 1. Exact branchwise divided-difference coefficient

Since \(\Delta(q)>0\) on the separated outer runs, define the branchwise divided difference
\[
\mathcal K(q):=
\frac{\Xi(\phi_2(q))-\Xi(\phi_0(q))}{\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q)}
=
\frac{\Xi(\phi_2(q))-\Xi(\phi_0(q))}{\Delta(q)}.
\]

Then the identity for \(\Delta''\) becomes

\[
\boxed{
\Delta''(q)+\mathcal K(q)\,\Delta(q)=0.
}
\]

This is an exact second-order linear ODE for the gap.

That is, to my mind, the cleanest transport-side normal form so far.

Strict excess is no longer just “a critical point of a gap.” It is a positive solution of the scalar ODE
\[
\Delta''+\mathcal K\,\Delta=0
\]
with some endpoint data.

So the transport fork has now been compressed to the single scalar coefficient \(\mathcal K(q)\).

## 2. Riccati form for the slope gap

Now define the logarithmic slope
\[
L(q):=\frac{\Delta'(q)}{\Delta(q)}.
\]
Since \(\Delta>0\),
\[
L'(q)=\frac{\Delta''}{\Delta}-\left(\frac{\Delta'}{\Delta}\right)^2.
\]
Using the ODE above,
\[
\frac{\Delta''}{\Delta}=-\mathcal K,
\]
so
\[
\boxed{
L'(q)+L(q)^2+\mathcal K(q)=0.
}
\]

This is the exact Riccati equation for the strict-excess fork.

It is stronger than the earlier “sign of \(\Upsilon\)” formulation, because it isolates the coefficient that actually governs the evolution of the normalized slope.

A strict-excess interior critical point is exactly a zero of \(L\), since
\[
\Delta'(q_*)=0 \iff L(q_*)=0.
\]
At such a point,
\[
L'(q_*)=-\mathcal K(q_*).
\]

So:

- if \(\mathcal K(q_*)>0\), then any critical point is strictly descending;
- if \(\mathcal K(q_*)<0\), then any critical point is strictly ascending;
- if \(\mathcal K(q_*)=0\), then the critical point is degenerate and the next transport jet matters.

This is a very sharp local description.

## 3. Relation to earlier targets

This \(\mathcal K\) coefficient packages several earlier formulations.

First, since
\[
\Delta''=\Xi(\phi_0)-\Xi(\phi_2),
\]
the sign theorem for
\[
\Upsilon(q):=\Xi(\phi_0(q))-\Xi(\phi_2(q))
\]
is exactly the sign theorem for \(-\mathcal K\), because \(\Delta>0\).

Second, if \(\Xi\) descends to value space across the outer runs, then
\[
\Xi(\phi_0)=\Xi(\phi_2),
\]
so
\[
\mathcal K\equiv 0,
\]
and the gap ODE becomes
\[
\Delta''=0.
\]
Hence \(\Delta\) is affine and strict excess dies except for sterile touching. This recovers the previous strongest target.

Third, if \(\Xi\) is monotone in \(u\) on the region swept by the two outer branches, then \(\mathcal K\) has fixed sign. So the earlier branch-discrepancy sign theorem is precisely a sign theorem for the Sturm coefficient \(\mathcal K\).

So the whole transport side has now been normalized to one scalar ODE coefficient.

## 4. Why this is better than the raw gap picture

The old formulation in terms of \(\Delta\), \(\Psi=\Delta'\), and \(\Upsilon=\Delta''\) was still somewhat ad hoc. The new coefficient \(\mathcal K\) is invariantly attached to the pair of branches and tells you the **normalized curvature** of the gap.

It also separates scale from shape:

- \(\Delta\) measures the actual gap size;
- \(\mathcal K\) measures the branchwise curvature discrepancy per unit gap.

That is exactly the right object for comparison principles.

## 5. Clean no-go consequences from the Riccati/Sturm form

This gives several useful sufficient conditions.

### (a) Nonnegative \(\mathcal K\) plus nonnegative endpoint slope kills strict excess

If
\[
\mathcal K(q)\ge 0
\]
on the common value interval, then
\[
L'(q)\le -L(q)^2\le 0.
\]
So \(L\) is nonincreasing.

Therefore, if
\[
L(q_L)\ge 0,\qquad L(q_R)\ge 0,
\]
then \(L\) cannot cross \(0\) in the interior except possibly by becoming identically \(0\) on a terminal interval, which would force the sterile case. In practice this gives:

> if \(\mathcal K\ge 0\) and the endpoint slope signs are compatible, strict excess is impossible.

This is slightly sharper than the earlier “\(\Upsilon\) has fixed sign” formulation, because it uses the Riccati damping term \(-L^2\) as well.

### (b) If \(\mathcal K>0\), any interior critical point is simple and descending

At a zero \(L(q_*)=0\),
\[
L'(q_*)=-\mathcal K(q_*)<0.
\]
So any critical point of \(\Delta\) is simple, and the gap crosses from increasing to decreasing. That means at most one such critical point can occur if the endpoint slope pattern is controlled.

This is a very useful local rigidity statement.

### (c) \(\mathcal K\equiv 0\) gives the sterile affine case

Then
\[
\Delta''=0,
\]
so \(\Delta\) is affine. If it has an interior critical point, it must be constant. So again we recover constant-gap same-value transport.

## 6. Best transport-side theorem target now

So I think the sharpest next theorem target is no longer phrased in terms of \(\Xi\) directly. It is:

> **Branchwise Sturm coefficient theorem.**  
> On the common value interval of the separated outer runs, the coefficient
> \[
> \mathcal K(q):=
> \frac{\Xi(\phi_2(q))-\Xi(\phi_0(q))}{\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q)}
> \]
> has controlled sign, ideally
> \[
> \mathcal K(q)\ge 0.
> \]

This is weaker than full value-space descent of \(\Xi\), weaker than exact first-order closure \(\Xi=H(Q,F)\), and sharper than the earlier boundary-slope theorem because it gives an exact ODE.

If that lands, the strict-excess fork is reduced to endpoint slope compatibility for a positive solution of
\[
\Delta''+\mathcal K\Delta=0.
\]

## 7. Bridge to the local \(M/c\)-gap law

This also gives a cleaner scalar-to-scalar bridge with my local good-patch attack.

On the local cancellation side, the close-core theorem is governed by
\[
\frac{M(m)}{c(m)}.
\]
On the strict-excess fixed-shear side, the transport theorem is now governed by the branchwise Sturm coefficient
\[
\mathcal K(q).
\]

So the mixed \(2\)-point frontier has now been compressed to two scalar coefficients:

- a midpoint coefficient
  \[
  \frac{M}{c},
  \]
- and a branchwise transport coefficient
  \[
  \mathcal K.
  \]

That is much tighter than anything earlier. A plausible bridge conjecture is now:

> **Bridge conjecture, coefficient form.**  
> On a good patch, the same RH-specific source structure that forces the local gap coefficient \(M/c\) to be nonzero also forces the branchwise transport coefficient \(\mathcal K\) to have a definite sign.

I do not have a proof, but that is now a crisp scalar-to-scalar statement.

## 8. Summary of the new reduction

So the strict-excess fixed-shear lane is now no longer just a self-intersection problem for the inverse-velocity graph. It is the study of a positive solution \(\Delta\) of the exact scalar ODE
\[
\Delta''+\mathcal K\,\Delta=0,
\]
where
\[
\mathcal K(q)=\frac{\Xi(\phi_2(q))-\Xi(\phi_0(q))}{\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q)}.
\]

That is the strongest transport-side reduction I can currently defend. The next attack should, in my view, focus directly on \(\mathcal K\).

## Assistant (2026-04-21 23:17:43)

_Thought for 23s_

I think the next real gain is to turn the fixed-shear strict-excess fork into a **one-coefficient Sturm comparison problem**, and then reduce that coefficient to the next transport jet.

The exact normal form we already reached is this. On the separated outer inverse branches \(\phi_0,\phi_2\) of
\[
Q(u)=\widehat Q(u),
\]
the gap
\[
\Delta(q):=\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q)>0
\]
satisfies
\[
\Delta''(q)+\mathcal K(q)\,\Delta(q)=0,
\]
where
\[
\mathcal K(q):=
\frac{\Xi(\phi_2(q))-\Xi(\phi_0(q))}{\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q)},
\qquad
\Xi(u):=\frac{Q''(u)}{Q'(u)^3}.
\]
So the entire strict-excess problem has already collapsed to one scalar coefficient \(\mathcal K\).

The next refinement is that \(\mathcal K\) is a **divided difference** of \(\Xi\). Therefore, by the mean value theorem, for each \(q\) there exists some
\[
\zeta_q\in(\phi_0(q),\phi_2(q))
\]
such that
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal K(q)=\Xi'(\zeta_q).
}
\]

That is important, because it means we do not really need to understand branchwise comparison of \(\Xi\) directly. It is enough to understand the sign of \(\Xi'\) on the interval swept out between the two outer runs.

Now compute the next transport jet:
\[
\Xi_1(u):=\frac{\Xi'(u)}{Q'(u)}.
\]
Hence
\[
\Xi'(u)=Q'(u)\,\Xi_1(u).
\]

So if \(Q'\) has fixed sign on the region between the two outer runs — which is the natural monotonicity regime in which inverse branches are being discussed — then the sign of \(\mathcal K\) is controlled by the sign of \(\Xi_1\). More precisely:

\[
\boxed{
\text{sign}(\mathcal K)\ \text{is controlled by sign}(Q')\cdot \text{sign}(\Xi_1)
\text{ on the swept interval.}
}
\]

That gives a very clean next theorem target:

> **First transport-jet sign theorem.**  
> Show that \(\Xi_1\) has fixed sign on the interval swept between the separated outer runs.

Because once that holds, \(\mathcal K\) has fixed sign, and then the gap \(\Delta\) is governed by a very rigid Sturm equation.

This is better than the earlier raw \(\Xi\)-comparison target. It says the real transport-side obstruction is not \(\Xi\) itself, but the sign of its \(Q\)-transport derivative \(\Xi_1\).

There are then several useful consequences.

First, since
\[
\Delta''+\mathcal K\Delta=0,
\qquad
\Delta>0,
\]
we get
\[
(\log \Delta)''=\frac{\Delta''}{\Delta}-\left(\frac{\Delta'}{\Delta}\right)^2
=
-\mathcal K-\left(\frac{\Delta'}{\Delta}\right)^2.
\]
So if
\[
\mathcal K\ge 0,
\]
then
\[
\boxed{
(\log \Delta)''\le 0.
}
\]
That is, \(\log \Delta\) is concave.

This is stronger than the earlier “\(\Delta'\) is monotone” picture. It gives a true convexity/concavity structure on the gap itself.

From log-concavity, you immediately get:

- \(\Delta\) has at most one critical point;
- any critical point is a strict maximum unless \(\mathcal K\equiv 0\);
- if the endpoint slopes already have the same sign, then no interior critical point can occur;
- if the endpoint slopes both vanish, then \(\Delta\) must be constant, hence one is back in the sterile constant-gap same-value transport case.

So the strict-excess fork is now even cleaner:

> if \(\mathcal K\ge 0\), then the gap is log-concave, and strict excess is reduced to a one-maximum endpoint-compatibility problem.

That is a stronger and more geometric statement than the earlier \(\Psi\)-monotonicity result.

Second, if \(\mathcal K>0\) strictly on the common value interval, then any interior critical point \(q_*\) satisfies
\[
\Delta''(q_*)=-\mathcal K(q_*)\Delta(q_*)<0,
\]
so the critical point is automatically a strict local maximum. Thus there is no room for any flat or higher-order touching unless \(\mathcal K\) degenerates somewhere. So a positive \(\mathcal K\) theorem would force the strict-excess branch into the cleanest possible shape: at most one strict hump.

Third, \(\mathcal K\equiv 0\) is exactly the affine/sterile case:
\[
\Delta''=0.
\]
So the three cases are now completely transparent:

- \(\mathcal K>0\): one strict hump at most;
- \(\mathcal K=0\): affine gap, hence sterile touching or constant gap;
- \(\mathcal K<0\): the only genuinely dangerous regime.

That means a sign theorem for \(\Xi_1\) would immediately split the transport fork into benign and dangerous strata.

This also clarifies the hierarchy. Before, I had introduced
\[
\Xi_0:=\Xi,\qquad \Xi_{n+1}:=\frac{\Xi_n'}{Q'}.
\]
The first genuinely new scalar beyond \(\Xi\) is
\[
\Xi_1.
\]
Now we see exactly why it matters: it controls the sign of the Sturm coefficient \(\mathcal K\), and therefore the concavity of \(\log \Delta\). So the first transport jet beyond \(\Xi\) is already enough to govern the whole strict-excess shape.

That is a real structural gain.

So the best next theorem target, in my view, is no longer “compare \(\Xi(\phi_0)\) and \(\Xi(\phi_2)\)” directly. It is:

> **Sturm-sign theorem.**  
> Prove that \(\Xi_1\) has fixed sign on the interval swept between the separated outer runs. Equivalently, prove that the branchwise Sturm coefficient
> \[
> \mathcal K(q)=\frac{\Xi(\phi_2(q))-\Xi(\phi_0(q))}{\phi_2(q)-\phi_0(q)}
> \]
> has fixed sign, ideally \(\mathcal K\ge 0\).

If that lands, then \(\log\Delta\) is concave and the strict-excess fork is reduced to endpoint slope compatibility. That is much smaller than a full transport classification.

It also gives a cleaner bridge to the local good-patch scalar
\[
\frac{M(m)}{c(m)}.
\]
The mixed \(2\)-point frontier is now controlled by two scalar coefficients:

- the local midpoint coefficient \(M/c\),
- the transport Sturm coefficient \(\mathcal K\), equivalently the sign of \(\Xi_1\).

So the bridge conjecture can now be sharpened to:

> On a good patch, the RH-specific source structure that forces \(M/c\neq 0\) should also force \(\mathcal K\ge 0\), or at least one-sided sign control of \(\Xi_1\), on the corresponding fixed-shear transport interval.

That is a much tighter scalar-to-scalar bridge than anything earlier.

So the attack has now reduced the strict-excess fixed-shear lane to a **Sturm-sign problem for the first transport jet \(\Xi_1\)**. That is the sharpest transport-side target I can currently defend.
## User (2026-04-21 23:21:04)
